WHISTLER.
JAMES ABBOTT McNEILL WHISTLER

As a child, Whistler was taken to Russia. The
Czar Nicholas treated his father as a personal friend, and
the boy played with Russian princes and princesses in
the Court nursery at Petersburg. For education he was
sent back to America. West Point Academy had trained
Whistler’s father, his uncle, his elder half-brother, and
his maternal grandfather ; and to West Point Whistler
went inevitably in 1851, in his seventeenth year. He
remained nearly four years, but failed to graduate, and
at last was “discharged” as hopeless. A fellow-pupil
says that “his intellect was brilliant and his ability
unquestioned, but he was unable to fix his mind on
mathematical'studies, and all he cared for was to draw
and paint.” in his fourth year his draftsmanship
attracted general attention, and the instructor in
drawing was inconsolable when he left.
to work at random, and one of his favourite tricks
was to draw first, say the face, from the model, then a
foot, then the body, skipping from one part of the
picture to another, apparently without keeping any
relation of the parts. But,, when . the picture was
completed, all the parts seemed to fit in together
WHISTLER.
From a drawing by Ernest Haskell.
like a mosaic.- On one occasion Whistler was painting in'
water-colours from a picture representing the interior of
a cathedral, with monks and nuns scattered about
Behind the tonsured head of one of the monks Whistler
had painted a shadow : the assistant-instructor, making
his round of examination, paused at Whistler’s seat and
said, very audibly, “ Your work, sir, is faulty in principle.
What is the meaning of that shadow ? There is none in
the model, and by no principle of light and shade could
any shadow be there.” Without saying a word, Whistler
tilled his brush, with one sweep of it he threw a cowl
over the head of the monk. He had painted the shadow
first. The instructor walked quietly away.
straw that broke down the patience of the
West Point authorities was an appalling chemistry paper.
Asked in later years about this, Whistler said he left
West Point through the fault of Nature. “She persisted
that silicon was not a gas.” But Whistler’s mathematics
were worse than his chemistry, and his horsemanship was
of al *' At cavalry drill it was not unusual for
Whistler, when the troop halted, te go over his horse’s
head. Then the instructor would call out, “ Er—Mr.
Whistler—are n’t you a little ahead of the squad ? ” One
day, a very bad fall somewhat alarmed the major in com-
mand, who called, “Mr. Whistler, are you hurt?” To
which Whistler, rising, drawing off his gauntlet, and
brushing away the tan, responded : “No, Major; lam
only wondering how any man con keep a horse for
pleasure l”
£
Whistler’s shortcoming caused his family anxiety,
and the next year a place was found for him in
the drawing division of the U.S. Coast Survey at
Washington. There his first etchings were executed for
Government plans. A fellow-employee describes him at
this period as “an elegant figure of a young man, with
an abundance of black, curly hair, soft and lustrous
eyes, finely-cut features, fair complexion, well-shaped
hands, and a graceful tournure. Handsome ; but
his mouth betokened more ease than firmness, his
brow more reverie than mental activity, and his
e\es more depth than penetration. Sensitiveness
and animation appeared to be his predominating
lra i t u’” As an °® o ' a *' h e was hopelessly unpunctual,
and he spent in fancy sketching the time he was expected
to employ in departmental work. So, after a short
period, his services were dispensed with. Then his
father gave him his own way, and he went to Paris, and
entered O. G. Gleyre’s studio as a pupil of painting.
While in Washington, the Russian Minister, a friend of
his father’s, showed him great hospitality ; and Whistler
responded with an invitation to dinner. He called at
the Minister’s house with a carriage; the Minister
entered, and was driven to a store, where Whistler
courteously asked permission to alight, and re-
turned with a paper parcel. Then they drove
along to other shops, and the procedure was
repeated. Finally they came to Whistler’s lodgings,
where he gave the Minister a comfortable chair, opened
his parcels, and cooked a dinner of several courses oyer a
gas stove, serving it himself with appropriate wines, and
following it with coffee and cigars. He talked the whole
time, explaining to the Minister that he had not the
money to tender the entertainment he wished to <dve
And this was the next best thing*. At the conclusion, the
guest was sent formally home in a carriage
j*
I n Paris Whistler lived upon an allowance
from his father. The money came promptly every month,
and was'promptly spent. In the last week of the month
he would be withopt a sou, living- on the remnants of his
studio supplies. These were generally exhausted a few
days before the arrival of the new remittance, and then
would Whistler lie on the door and rave at fate, some
times praying for death, but never borrowing a’ franc
Month after mouth this went ph.
After two years in Paris Whistler went to Italy, and
spent some time in Venice. He was always improvident
and always hard-up. There is a story that at one time
he and his model were in such straits that thev resolved
to drown themselves together in a canal. They left their
rooms in order, and started out. Luckily it was evening
and an especially brilliant sunset distracted Whistleris
attention. He sent the model bark for a canvas and his
colours, and painted the sunset. The sketch was sold the
same evening ; and they forgot all about suicide
111 1863 Whistler came to London, where his home was
fixed untU his return to Paris in 1895. He had- a house
at Chelsea, close to the Thames ; and there his remark-
abie etchings of river scenery were made. In 1888 he
married the widow of E. W. Goodwin, architect and
writer. The lady was a daughter of J. B. Philip, an Eng
sculptor of some celebrity in the last century 8 ‘

The first of Whistler’s three books—high-flown
often witty, and on the whole rather thin and
•rtiflciaj-was The Gentle Art of Making Enemies,
' nspuert by the famous quarrel with Ruskin
Whistlers nocturnes and symphonies were too much for
the nerves of the veteran critic, who wrote in Fors Clavi-
ffera in 1877; “I have heard and se/n much
of Cockney impudence before mow; but never
expected to hear a coxcomb -ask two hundred
guineas for flinging a pot of paint in the public’s face ”
W histler sued for libel; and got a (farthing damages.
77 * s “ b jectof Whistler’s caustic comment in
2he Gentle Art ; but he spared none of his “ enemies ”
Quoting a statement in Frith’s autobiography to’ the
effect that at one time in Frith’s early life it was a
toss-up whether he_ should become a painter or an
auctioneer, Whistler remarked that—he must have
■ Tollamerton, the critic who complained
that Whistler s Symphony in White” was not a sym-
phony in white, because there was yellow in the girl’s
hair and flesh tints in her cheeks, ‘Whistler retorted ■
Bon Dieu ! Did this wise person expect white hair and a
chalked face? And does he then, in his astounding con-
sequence, believe that a symphony in F contains no other
note, hut shall be a continued repetition of F, F, F?”
J*
In 1888 Whistler announced anew the artist’s
privilege in Ten O’Oloclc, a skittish little book published
a *v®“Listen ! he said, “.There never was an
artistic period. There never was an art loving nation.”
True , Art arose ; Art grew ; Art lived and spread and
• reigned supreme—by force of fact, not by election, and
there was no meddling from the outsider. ..
“And centuries passed in this using, and the world was
flooded with all that was beautiful, until there arose a
new class, who discovered the cheap, and foresaw fortune
m the future of the sham.
“Then sprang into existence the tawdry, the common
the gewgaw.
“The taste of the tradesman supplanted the science of
the artist, and what was born of the million went hack to
them, and ehartped them, for it was after their own
heart ; and the great and the small, the statesman and
the slave, took to themselves the abomination that was
tendered, and preferred it-and have lived with it ever
since !
“ An d the artist’s occupation was gone, and the manu-
facturer and the huckster took his place...
“ And the people—this time—had much to say in the
matter—and all were satisfied. And Birmingham and
Manchester arose in their might—and Art was relegated
to the curiosity shop.”
J*
Ten years later Whistler’s encounter in the
French law courts with Sir William Eden moved him to
write the story of The Baronet and the Butterfly— with
the motto “ Noblesse Abuse “ Being a rare and fas-
cinating history, wherein is shown, with much wit and
PORTRAIT OF THE ARTISTS MOTHER.
From the lithograph by Whistler.
circumstance, how the gentle Master, unsuspecting, was
sighted, tracked, waylaid, circumvented, and run to eari h
by a commercial knight of untiring industry,”—and
much more to similar effect.
By, this time Whistler’s wit was wearing thin ; and his
pose had grown familiar. The book had a success of
curiosity only. Yet, when he gave evidence in the Pennell-
Sickert libel case in 1897, he seemed to have retained
something of his old capacity for repartee. The case
turned on a distinction between direct and transfer
lithographs. Whistler declined to admit the distinction.
“Whatever the artist touches produces whatever effect
the quality of the medium affords.”
“ Do you know," Whistler was asked, «that the Royal
Academy does not accept transfer lithographs?” “I
hear this with surprise,” he answered. “ But 1 am not
at all aufait with the limitations of the Royal Academy,”
“ Mr. Sickert wrote,” continued counsel, “ that Mr.
Whistler’s almost nothings are priceless. You-don’t dis-
sent from that?” “It is very simple and very proper,”
-replied Whistler, with calmness, “that Mr. Sickert
should say that sort of thing ; but I attach no importance
to it.”
At the close of the trial Whistler begged to be allowed
to deliver a speech which he had prepared for a British
jury. Unluckily, the Judge refused permission.
J*
One of the best and best-known of Whistler’s
pictures is the portrait of his mother, “an arrangement
in gray and black,” hanging in the Luxembourg
gallery at Paris. The composition of this picture, painted
in 1872, was in Whistler’s mind ten years before, when he
was drawing illustrations cut on wood by Dalziel for Good
Words. In the portrait of Carlyle he held to the same
idea. Other pictures highly valued are The White Girl
(1862); Princesse des Pays de Porcelaine (1865) ; the Noc-
turnes in blue and gold, and blue and green (1878); the
“arrangement in black” of Lady Archibald Camp-
bell; the “arrangement in gray and green” of Miss
Alexander ; and the Sarasate.
Whistler’s art was undoubtedly influenced by the near-
sightedness from which he suffered all his life. In using
pencil, ink, or water-colour, he drew with his eyes
scarcely a hand-breadth from the paper. For the rest,
as George Moore has pointed out in what is possibly the
truest criticism of Whistler that has been printed
( Modern Painting), he was the victim of his physical
constitution. He had the brain to conceive great things,
but force failed him to create them. “The greatest
- painters, I mean' the very greatest—Michael Angelo,
Velasquez, and Rubens—were gifted by Nature with as
full a measure of health as of genius. Their physical,
constitutions resembled more those of bulls than of men.
Michael Angelo lay on his back for three rears pajnting
the Sistine Chapel. Rubens painted a life-size figure in
a morning of pleasant work, and went out to ride in the
afternoon. But Nature has dowered Mr. Whistler with
only genius. His artistic perceptions.are more exquisite
than Velasquez’s. He knows as much, possibly even a little
more, and yet the result is never quite equal. Why? A
question of health. Vest un temperament de chatte."
And Moore ends as a disciple : “ More than any other
painter, Mr. Whistler’s influence has made itself felt on
English art. More than any other man, Mr. Whistler has
helped to purge art of the vice of subject and belief that
the mission of the artist is to copy nature."
&
There was no pose about Whistler’s cheerful
irresponsibility ; he had the temperament of the can’t-be-
bothereds. His nerves were strung so highly that he
could not long maintain energy : a man of fits and starts,
he worked feverishly when in the vein, and collected his
strength anew in a following period of depression. Yet
the mere quantity of his work would do'notliseredit to a
man of persevering industry : several hundred etchings,
several score of paintings, and innumerable sketches and
drawings.
It is said that in Whistler’s Chelsea house two rooms
only were furnished. The others were full of furni-
ture, pictures, and- bric-ii-brac waiting to be un-
packed and arranged. Whistler never had the
money, or the time, or the energy to set the mass
in order. The large sums that he earned melted
away almost as soon as gained, and at last his creditors
installed a bailiff and advertised a sale. Whistler gave a
dinner-party while the auction-posters were on the house,
and the bailiff waited at table. In 1879 Whistler filed a
petition in insolvency for liquidation of his affairs “by
arrangement," and somebody printed a squib to the effeot
that
Of various ■“ arrangements ” we've had an array.
Black and white, gold and silver, and tawny and gray ;
But of all the arrangements there yet remains one,
And that‘s to arrange with the troublesome dun.
I*
Whistler stories abound— some o£ them well-
known. His retort to Oscar Wilde is famous. Said
Wilde, in admiration of a remark : " How I wish I had
said that, Jimmy !” “Never mind, Oscar: you will!”
Whistler’s acquaintances said that he would con such
mots in the manner of Sheridan, hoard them carefully.,
and lead up to them with the greatest pains so that they
might seem to flash out impromptu.
"Oh, Mr. Whistler,” said a lady, " I came down the
Thames this morning—such beautiful glimpses ! They
reminded me of your etchings." “ Y-e-e-s : Nature is
creeping up.”
When George Moore was still in the flush of his enthu-
siasm for Whistler, he was credited with the remark,
apropos the Portrait of. Miss Alexander•: “ This is your
masterpiece. I count you and Velasquez the two greatest
portrait-painters the world has seen.’,’ “But, my dear
fellow, why drag in Velasquez?”
One of Whistler’s early Venetian pictures, sold for a few
lire, was exhibited in a Bond-street shop at a high price.
Whistler came to see it, and found a band of critics and
disciples extolling its valuable quality. “ Look at that
background ! ’’ said Walter Sickert
to Roussel. “ And, see the deli-
cate outline of that tree 1 ” re
sponded Roussel. “ A master-
piece ! ” sang the chorus. Whistler
wiped his eye-glass and took a brief
look. “ Humph ! I remember it.
Why, it is n’t half finished ! ”
Portraits and photographs
of Whistler are numerous : that by
Mortimer Menpes being perhaps
the best known. The drawing by
Ernest Haskell here reproduced—
one of several done some years ago
seems to give the best idea of the
peculiar diablerie of Whistler’s ex-
pression. In the last ten years his
eccentricity of dress and appear-
ance became very pronounced.
“ His face was covered with count-
less wrinkles, but the complexion
remained clear. His brows were
unusually .bushy, and his glisten-
ing brown eyes peered out from
und era ;athlike snakes in the grass.
His hair” (wrote acontributor to
McClure’s Magazine in 1836) “is
the most amazing part of his get-
up. It is all arranged in. separate
curls, most artistically put to-
gether.” These were dyed black,
with the exception of the famous
lock in front, which was left quite
white, and on grand occasions was
tied up with a small ribbon.
One of Max Beerhohm's cari-
catures shows Whistler as he
appeared in the eighties “He
wore a very long black over-
coat, rather like that of one of the little men iu
‘Noah’s Ark,’ and a French top hat with the brim
sticking straight out. In his hand he carried a kind of
bamboo wand, -about four feet long and very thin.”
When he was seen walking, he was followed usually by a
bevy of appreciative small boys.
In his closing years Whistler realised the fable of the
ant and the grasshopper. He had lived too long. His
hand had lost its cunning, and he was in need of
money. He had become vieux jeu —out of date and
ridiculous ; and in Paris and London his vanity was
slighted. Money-lending creditors pursued him; and
he was sick, peevish, a martyr to neuralgia. His ending
ill ■ gloom and obscurity recalls Beau Brummell’s. He
took refuge in Brussels, where a year ago he was reported
seriously ill in an economical lodging-house. One can
imagine the rest. i

A SUNRISE FANTASY.
The Sea teas murmuring to the Hie \t
In ecstasy withdraum >
A lullaby of hushed delight
That thrilled with chords of Dawn.
Selene, weary in the West,
Cried, “ Ah Endymion !
MthLatmian loner, canst not rest *
Why walkest thou alone ?
“ Sleep, for my altered bosom beams
“ red to the heart’s core:
Sleep, till 1 shine within thy dreams
And kiss thee, as of yore.”
But all the world's true-lovers came
I rom some far paradise
T° shield me from, the bitter shame
Of faithless lovers’ lies.
Dark Sappho, Klee a damask rose,
Pressed Phaon fondly there :
Por him I leaped the rock of woes .
Wilt thou do less for Her ?”
And she, the lady Laura, stood
To guard me from disgrace:
1 looked within her lifted hood
And saw her lily face.
And Lancelot whispered to my-ear,
“ Your manhood’s trial this:
As I icas true to Guinevere
Be true to Brunelys ! ”
Then to the western sky 1 turned
And “ No \” for answer gave :
In desperate flame the goddess burned
And plunged beneath the wave.
Catullus spoke : “ A little while,
My brother, t ill we feast! ”
Then took the path that, seemed to smile
Into the budding Bast.
Troop after, troop I watched, amazed,
Speed joyously away
To where the pillared, clouds upraised
The portal of the Day.
Beneath the ebon lintel shone
In palpitating tides
The primrose light that, shines upon
The brows of angel brides.
Old. Ocean, from his sapphire throne
, Flung rubies to the land ;
And, all incarnadined, alone,
Sudden 1 saw Her stand,
Ho pale and timid beauty she ;
But strong, erect, elate
And glorious in majesty
She stood within the gate.
An emerald circlet decked her hair
Black as a nightmare’s mane ;
Her mouth was like a crimson lair
For Love to hide his pain.
Around her tawny breasts a wreath
Of red hibiscus glowed ;
Her huntress’ limbs, the tunic 'neath,
Like burnished copper slioived.
I saw the bosom rise and fall
Above "her maiden zone :
She waited bright Apollo « call
To yield her for his own.
I stretched my hand to grasp the prize,
I took my lyre, the Sun,
And smote the radiant harmonies
That wedded us in one.
Boor Crade JUtomisemont.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gum Blossoms.
On Monaro, in the summer, when the opal’s in the
sky,
And the West is flooded rose-red, green and gold;
When the hills are misty purple, when the day
birds homeward fly,
And the sheep are bleating, plaintive, from the
fold ;
Then there comes a soft, cool air-wave, blown
from distant, kindly seas,
And gently roughs my loved one’s golden hair;
And the white-stemmed, whispering trees, waving
softly in the breeze,
Waft the scent of sweet gum blossoms through
the air.
Gum blossoms ! Sweet white blossoms ! Ah, your
honey-golden scent
Can reach me even here, so far away !
I’m, in fancy, on Monaro—though my weary back
is bent
O’er the ledgers which record my working day.
In fancy, on Monaro I can see the river glow
With a radiance from the sunset, and I hear the
goblin blare
Of the kookaburra's laughter; and her hand’s in
mine, I know,
When I dream I’m on Monaro, and gum blossoms
scent the air.
K.S.W.
JILL.
At the Last.
The sky grows white with the moon,
Arid the sea yearns up to the night
As a soul to an unknown height,
Drawn thence by a starry rune.
Only a lost wind strays,
Like the breath of Passion blown
In the vault of the Night unknown;
And the heart in me sobs and says
“ After a while we, too,
Shall rest as the stars above,
When we have no more to do
With the dream of Life and Love.’
O Time ! thy feet that run
Over the hills arid waves,
Over the cradles and graves,
From the first to the farthest suri !
Some day thou too shalt cease—
Some way there ’ll come to thee
Death’s white tranquillity,
The boon of an awful peace —
When the latest grief shall flcjvv v ' " '
With the surge that drifts away,
And the night shall no more go
In its endless chase of day.
Then shall the worn heart rest,
Then shall the sad Sea yearn
No more for the Moon’s return
Like a bird on its frozen nest
Dead, with its young ones dead
Under her breast on the bough,
Where nothing can wake them now—
Not the Dawn, with its golden trea 1 :
Where Death has been good to all,
Glood to the mother and young
And the dreams are beyond recall,
And the songs have all been sung.
So, at the last, to sleep !
So, at the last, to be
Still as the dead, still sea I
Never to wake and weep,
Never to know Love’s pain,
Never to yearn on for
What is gone for evermore; >
To be as we were again,
Ere we came o’er the bourne of birth,
Ere we knew of the fading flowers,
Of Love and of Life on earth,
And the hearts that were not ours !
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
Baby’s Sick
M.L.
I’ve laid it all on Shamrock—
Ten to one.
The knowing ones are betting
On old Sun.
I got a wire this morning—
“ Come home, quick j
Mamma badly wants you—
Baby’s sick ! ”
The house is oddly quiet—
To my joy !
And yet I think I kind of
Miss the 1 oy.
His eyes are slow and heavy,
Breathing quick ;
His little pulse is flying—
Baby’s sick.
He scarce looks out the doorway,
Nothing cares
For joys of Alpine climbing
On the chairs.
There lie his little shovel
And his pick ;
No need to watch the pathway—
Baby’s sick
The house is all on tip-toes :
Shadows creep
And crouch about in corners—
He’s asleep.
“ Don’t let the new s-boy wake him !
Mabel, quick !
Run out and take the paper—
Baby’s sick.”
Here’s word about the races.
Oh, I say !
The Sun has won the Steeple;»
Second—May.
Old Shamrock kicked the hurd'e—
Let him kick !
The Cup may go to—pieoes !
Baby’s si«k.
LOLA RIDGE.

The Case of Mr. J. BARTLETT.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
It is to the interest of this country that
all citizens should be so educated that they
may become a self-reliant and prosperous
people, and to put this idea into practical
effect a reporter was commissioned to bring
before the community a matter here dealt
with by Mr. James Bartlett, of No. 5
Patten’s Buildings, Kent-street, Sydney.
“In the time of a great epidemic,” said
Mr. Bartlett, “ when influenza was ram-
pant in Sydney, I was one of the many
who failed to elude that terrible disease.”
“Were you in danger of meeting with
serious consequences ? ” aske.d the writer.
“ There was every likelihood that I
would never get well again, as I was in a
frightfully low state for many months.
Matters went so badly with me that I
thought I was fighting a hopeless fight
against an overwhelming force, and. as I
1 was laid up for weeks, and could not do a
stroke of work for seven months, I think I
had good cause to form that opinion. I got
so thin and ghastly white that everybody
thought I was going into consumption, be-
sides getting awfully weak into the bar-
gain. ' The pains in my head were enough
to run away with one’s senses, being of that
heavy, depressing nature which makes the
victim feel as if there is only care and
Sorrow in the world, It was not the in-
fluenza-itself that I had to regret so much
as the effects it left behind, for my system
seemed so upside down that there was not
a sound part about me. My limbs were so
racked with aches that if I walked a few
} u -‘ us I had to sit down and rest for the
purpose of easing the pain, but another
reason why I could not go far without
taking a spell was because I used to turn
very giddy. Through that cause I nearly
fell down stairs several times, so
that I was almost afraid to move in case
of meeting with an accident. I wondered
what was the good of living in such misery,
for I had no energy, no pleasure, no any-
thing that was worth having. For weeks
and weeks I could not get any sleep, and
there was a feeling in my eyes as if they had
swelled up. A dark haze was visible at
every turn, my sight being so bad that I
could scarcely see, and if I wanted to get
anything for myself I had to grope about
as if it were dark. I could not hear very
distinctly either, owing to a strange drum-
ming in my ears, so that I was not much
use to anybody. Indeed, I was a nuisance
to myself, and would like to have been out
of the way of the persecuting pains which
came to the small of my back and almost
paralysed me whenever I stooped. This,
together with the thickness of my urine”
told me that my kidneys were disordered,
and I was afterwards told so by the doctors
whom I was treated by, with the additional
information that my liver was also dis-
eased.” '
“ Did they give you any medicine ? ”
“Of course they did, and I was never
behind hand in taking it, as my enforced
absence from work meant a great loss to
me, and I wanted to get better. But, some-
how, my condition remained stationary.
When I examined my tongue it was al-
ways furred and rough, while my mouth
had a foul taste as if I had been drinking.
Not a morsel of food could I enjoy, but I
am not one to give in till I am fairly beaten,
and I used to make myself eat; but it
would have been better if I had spared my-
self the trouble, as everything seemed to
decay in the stomach, and give rise to ex-
cessive torture in the form of windy spasms.
My chest also felt as if a piece of lead had
Ndged there for the purpose of stopping my
breath, and there was no relief to be got
till I vomited everything up again. The
weakness of my stomach was so advanced
that I could not keep my food down, so
that accounts for my feebleness and the loss
of flesh I sustained. There was also some-
thing wrong with my heart, which palpi-
tated as if I had been indulging in some ex-
cessive exercise, and with my nerves all
gone I was completely wrecked from every
point of view. A twitching pain below my
neck irritated me, but for that matter I
was always in an irritable moodj even the
sound of voices raised in laughter being
sufficient to annoy me; but ;I was so
desperate and despondent that jl did not
know what to do with myself.' In any
short stretches of sleep I got flashed
an awful picture upon my vision, and when
I woke up I could not help thinking that
something I dreaded would surely 1 happen,
although I recognised that death would
have been preferable to any more suffer-
ing.”
“ Why did you not try a change of
medicine ? ”
“If I had not done so I would not be
here to-day, as I u as sinking very fast after
the long and torturing captivity in the
hands of my diseases, when a fellow-work-
man prompted mo to try Clements Tonic.
It was not till I carried out liis advice that
the influenza was beaten, and now, after a
long and convincing experience, I am sure
there is no medicine on earth like Clements
Tonic, for after taking a couple of bottles I
was feeling a new interest in existence.
The improvement was then so marked that
I ventured to hope that a possibility still
existed that I would yet recover, and by
closely pursuing my latest course of treat-
ment my heartfelt hopes were gratified. [
started on the right track with a slight
appetite, which came thi'ough using
Clements Tonic, and which that medicine
continued to increase. Then my liver be-
gan to work more regularly, a fact which
afforded great relief in several ways. For
instance, my headaches slackened off, and
the sickly taste left my mouth, besides hav-
ing full use of my eyes in consequence of the
disappearance of the haziness before them.
The pains in my back and shoulders were
lighter than before, and I had no trouble in
breathing, as my chest was quite free from
oppression. A feeling of vigour crept
through my system through the medium of
Clements Tonic, and I was gaining in flesh
already, which showed that my food was
digesting well. I marvelled at the rapidity
with which my vomiting troubles were
stopped, while the action of Clements Tonic
on my nervous system could not be too
highly spoken of. When my liver and
kidneys had been relieved of all symptoms
of disease by Clements Tonic I hardly knew
how to suppress the gladness I felt, es-
pecially as no cause remained for anxiety
as to the state of my heart, which ceased
palpitating shortly after flatulence was
remedied. ”
“And now your health is perfect. I
believe ?” * ’
“It could not be better, for I took
Clements Tonic till I was cured outright,
and for the past three years I have scaled
oyer 13 stone. lam thankful to be able to
give this information, which you can pub-
lish in any way it pleases you.”

THURSDAY, JULY 30, 1903.
The Federal Capital.
The Federal Capital Commission report has
evoked a remarkable outburst of that anti-federal
fanaticism, which evidently still maintains great
strength in some of the States. At the outset
the Australian union was confronted with the
venomous hostility of certain sections of the
community—the newspaper proprietaries who saw
in it a lessening of the chances of corrupt conces-
sions, the selfish State politicians who recognised in
it a loss of their petty dignities, the anti-Austra-
lians who foresaw and feared the growth of a
stronger national spirit. In its working the
Federation has done nothing to lessen the antag-
onism of these people. Whilst lacking, so far, an
administration which, by declaring straightly and
without reservation for a fully national programme,
would inspire a fierce approbation in the minds of
the majority, and so overwhelm the hostility
of unpatriotic sections—the Federation has, on
the whole, been marked by a reasonably honest
and somewhat national policy. So the forces of
of dishonesty, of corruption have been
in no way conciliated, and, seeing that the union
must ever be a dangerous foe to their aspirations,
are united to hamper its development in all
possible ways. The Federal capital question pro-
vides a good battle-issue, and the forces of the
anti-Federalists are being rallied to a fight on
that point. They profess a variety of motives,
adopt various tactics, but a very little examina-
tion shows that they have one common spur to
action, hatred of the Federation—not of this
Australian Government merely, but of the whole
policy of union. The suggestion comes from one
quarter that the Federal Administration needs no
home of its own, but can continue indefinitely to
occupy f urnished lodgings in Melbourne, and be
hustled on the stairs by the stout landlords of the
Victorian Upper House. From another quarter
it is urged that, whilst lodgings on toler-
ance are the proper thing, they should
be in Sydney, not Melbourne. Yet another
suggestion is that a capital may be built, but it
must be with a minimum of territory and so
situated that, as regards access to the outside
world and to the various parts of the Common-
wealth, it shall be at the mercy of one State or
another, and as regards trade, dependent on
Sydney or on Melbourne. Unanimity is dis-
played on one point—that the natural and proper
site, which will give to the Federation a seaport
of its own and ample room for the development of
an independent city, must be tabooed. Whether
the Federation is to be bound down by Melbourne :
twine or Sydney string is a point of difference
among the anti-Federalists; but all are agreed
that it is to Jbe fettered. And the time is ripe for
the Federation, which is the supreme power in
the land, to say something quite firm, even rude,
on this subject. The Federation, in the hands of
its present rulers, has contracted a habit of being
rather temporising and politic in its replies to the
sour grumblings of the anti-Federalists. It would
redeem its reputation a good deal by leaving
politeness out of its dealings with them in this
matter.
» * • *
The Federal Constitution gives to the Austra-
lian Government the right to choose a capital site
anywhere in N.S.W., so long as the site is 100
miles from Sydney. The Constitution, in con-
ferring this power, said nothing about picking
on a spot the trade from which would feed
Sydney, or Melbourne; suggested nothing
about placing the capital within the area of
influence of any of the big daily newspapers.
What it did suggest strongly by implication was
that the site should be such as to give the capital
an independent position, away from the shadow
of any of the great cities, and guarded from any
State Government interference. The absurd
claim has been made by Joseph Carruthers
that, according to the Constitution, N.S.W., and
N.S.W. alone, has the power to choose the site,
since the Federation must acquire the land, or
most of it, from the N.S.W. Government, and a
clause of the Act of union provides that land may
not be resumed by the Federation from a State
without the State’s permission. That it is pos-
sible to twist the Constitution into an apparent
sanction of this contention is a fact. From a
police court attorney’s point of view, many Acts
of Parliament can be turned to strange meanings ;
there is a clause of the Criminal Law Amendment
Act of N.S.W., for instance, which could be
interpreted to mean that every devoted husband
in the community is liable to 15 years penal
servitude. But, fortunately, serious legislation is
not usually treated in a spirit of ievity by digni-
fied courts. Where a plain meaning is possible, and
that plain meaning was the obvious intention of the
law-makers, it holds good, in spite of any ingenious
misreadings that may be possible. The Australian
Constitution did not give the Federation power
to generally resume a State’s lands without
the State’s consent, but made an exception in the
case of the Federal capital area. It made it man-
datory for the Australian Government to choose
that area and for the N.S.W. Government to
grant it without payment. That is the position
which N.S.W. herself sought to establish when,
by the treachery of George Reid, the first
Federal referendum failed—handicapped by a
minimum vote provision and further weighted by
his dishonest Yes-No. It is the position which
the other States conceded to N.S.W. querulous-
ness; and it is the position which now N.S.W.
anti-Federalists, led by the S.M. Herald and the
D. Telegraph and Joseph Carruthers, seek to
repudiate, on a law point which the brassiest of
attorneys would hesitate to bring before a court
of petty sessions. Part of the service which Syd-
ney daily newspapers and Sydney dreary politi-
cians seek to do their State is to always represent
it as a cranky infant howling for all the toys
and all the sweets in the nursery, and raising a
law point when another State ventures to even
look at its picture-book. But Joseph Carruthers
and the Sydney Freetrade newspapers to the con-
trary notwithstanding, it is absolutely certain
that the Australian Parliament has the fullest
power to choose its capital site, with as large an
area as it likes within reason, provided that it keeps
out of the 100 miles radius from Sydney. The
contention of Joseph Carruthers, backed up by
the Freetrade press, that it must accept any area
offered by N.S.W., even a sand-patch out at the
back of Bourke, is the foolish imagining of a man
whose ideas of Constitutional law are evidently
gleaned from the practice of a “ pointing ” police-
court solicitor.
• * * «
In th« exercise of its power to choose a capital
the Federation should submit to no dictation, or
even suggestion, from any State. It should ignore
absolutely the whines which may arise in N.S.W.
or in Victoria, and come to a decision based on
the needs of Australia as a whole. Whether the
Argus or the Daily Telegraph will have the
better chance of supplying the Federal capital
with absurdly padded cables and twisted politics
is no business of the Federation. Whether the
calico for the capital’s bed-linen will come from
Robert Reid’s store or William M'Millan’s is
another entirely unimportant circumstance; so
long as the invoice is right—which, unfortunately,
now that Kingston has left the Australian Minis-
try, it probably won’t be. It is Australia that is
to be served ; not a Sydney or Melbourne clique.
N.S.W., as the State most particularly insistent
in its lamentations over Federal injustice in the
matter of the capital, is the State which needs
particularly a telling rebuke. It has three
members in the Federal Cabinet —a larger
proportion than its population entitles it
to and a larger representation in the
Federal Parliament than any other State.
Further it has a constitutional guarantee that
the capital must be within its borders. A State
with an atom of dignity or of plain horse-sense
would, placed in this uniquely favorable position,
take up a dignified Australian attitude and pro-
fess, if it did not feel, a lofty disregard for small
State squabblings over the matter. With a royal
flush in hand anyone should be able to play a
calm and confident game. But N.S.W. is cursed
with a particularly undignified and poor-spirited
crowd of politicians and paper proprietors, who,
in their desire to damage the Federation, are will-
ing to drag their State’s dignity through any
puddle. But, however much Joseph Carruthers
and the Herald and the Telegraph may be-
spatter N.S.W. and expose their State as an un-
reasonable and foolish infant, the duty of the
Australian Government is in no wise affected. It
should treat N.S.W. and Victoria and all the
other States, as States, with absolute contempt,
and decide the capital matter purely in the light
of Australian needs.
• • • •
A decision on Australian lines would, as The
Bulletin has repeatedly shown, necessitate a
capital area of about 5000 square miles, embracing
a reasonably good seaport, with the Australian
Government as the sole ground landlord of the
territory. A less area than 5000 square miles
would fail to guarantee the full benefit to the
Federal Government of the increased land values
arising from the construction of the capital, and
would otherwise hamper the growth of the
Federal city. The Commonwealth must have
around its capital a district which is exclusively
its own, to provide food supplies for a fair-
sized city; which is sufficiently large to give
facilities for a wide range of manufactures ; and
which will enable the Government to have in its
own home its small-arms factory, its clothing
and other factories, its docks and arsenals and
shipyards. Further, it is vitally necessary that the
Federal Government should have an outlet of its
own to the sea, lest possibly a sour and embit-
tered State Government should attempt to
throttle its capital by refusing it decent railway
communication and allowing the roads to its
borders to go to ruin. Such folly does not seem
a probable event; but in view of the present
childish actions of the State-frights party, almost
any idiocy seems at least possible. One can’t be
too careful in dealing with a State which has the
Herald and the Telegraph as it chief advisers.
* * * *
There is only one site so far suggested which
meets all requirements, and that is an area
embracing the port of Eden and stretching back
to the highlands of Bombala. As regards natural
facilities and advantages, that site has been
proved clearly to be a good one, and proof of its
fitness is made more positive by the strong
hostility which every mention of it evokes from
the anti-Federal press. The Daily Telegraph
last week showed how the thought of .an entirely
suitable capital site vexes the enemies of the
union :
If Bombala were chosen, the Federal authorities would
have an opportunity, which they would probably take or
try to, of establishing a separate State there to an extent
which is not contemplated by the Constitution. Having
their own seaboard and a power to. acquire territory
which the Constitution does not limit precisely, they
would be under a certain temptation to create a kind of
toy State, of course at the expense of the rest of the
Commonwealth and inevitably to its lasting irritation.
There could not be a more cogent argument in
favor of the Eden-Bombala area. It is just
because that area gives the Commonwealth the
chance of breaking down State jealousies, and of
counteracting the viciQus centralising policy of
the great metropolitan cities, that its advantages
show out so clearly. As a rough and ready
principle the Federal Government may be assured
that in this matter, as in most others affecting the
Commonwealth, it cannot do better than to find
out what the Telegraph advises, and then do the
contrary.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Kingston Goes Out.
It is not necessary to accept the ostensible as the
real reason for C. C. Kingston’s departure from
the Barton Cabinet. In declining to continue in
office longer Kingston gives as his motive a dis-
agreement on a matter which is, after all, of mode
rather than of principle. His determination that
the Australian seaman shall be protected against
sweating, and the Australian ship-owner against
unfair competition at the hands of employers who
have not to conform to the same wage conditions,
is an admirable one, and his proposed method of
carrying it out—bringing all who participate in
Australian coastal shipping under the provisions
of an Australian Industrial Arbitration Act—is
simple and effective. The difficulties which, it is
professed, some of his colleagues saw in the en-
forcement of such legislation do not seem to have
any real existence. The United States of America
enforces much more rigorous terms in its coastal
trade —barring the foreigner out altogether. But
the Australian Government, whilstagreeing, or pro-
fessing to agree, entirely with Kingston as to the
end he had in view, disagreed, or professed to have
disagreed, with the means, and suggested another
method of reaching the same end which, though
not so good, was at any rate fairly good. In that
difference of opinion there is not sufficient material
for a Cabinet crisis. Taking Premier Barton as
representative of the Cabinet, his proposal was
that the Shipping Act should enforce certain
minimum rates of pay and that foreign shipowners
engaging in Australian coastal trade should
have to pay those rates. Kingston’s proposal—
characteristic of the man in its strength and
directness —was to provide in the Arbitration
Act that foreign shipowners trading between
Australian ports should be amenable to the court’s
decisions and its common rules, exactly the same
as local shipowners. The superiority of King-
ston’s method over the other is manifest. A
minimum wage enforced by a special Act would
be somewhat cumbrous; it could hardly provide
for absolutely equal conditions of employment.
But the application of the Arbitration Act to
every vessel trading between Australian ports
would. Still, convinced as Kingston was of the
superior effectiveness of his method of protecting
the Australian seamen and shipowners from
unfair foreign competition, he would hardly have
left the Cabinet on that disagreement alone.
It is necessary to conclude that there were other
points of difference, and Kingston’s letter of
resignation in a suggestive postscript
“ P.S. —I purposely refrain from reference to any other
point of difference.”
supports that conclusion. The plain fact of the
matter seems to be that Kingston finds himself
altogether out of step with the Barton Govern-
ment, which, since Barton’s return from London,
has drifted further away each week from the
democratic ideal. There have been several inci-
dents of late which have disgusted Australian
Nationalists —the Naval Tribute not the least of
them —and the drift of the Government away from
the sentiments which it was once supposed to
typify is shown by the fact that at recent
important divisions it has been directly opposed
by all the Radicals of the House of Representa-
tives; and where the Radicals are is Kingston’s
natural place. For what it has failed to do, as
much as for what it has done, the Federal Govern-
ment has lately brought keen disappointment to
Australian democrats. Kingston evidently wants
freedom from its shackles —a freer scope where
neither Barton drowses nor Lynk schemes. It is
not necessary, and would be foolish to believe,
that the last straw is the whole truss which over-
weighted his patience. Without a doubt the
resignation leaves the Barton Government
seriously weakened. Kingston has been its chief
member in the eyes of the public for months, and
is its only member who has made a popular repu-
tation as a Federal Minister. The public which
regards Lyne with disdain, Barton as a negli-
gable quantity, O’Connor and Deakin as
remarkably clever and—compared with most
—conspicuously trustworthy politicians, and
Turner as an intensely painstaking, cautious
and reliable financier, has had nothing but
frank, unlimited admiration for “Customs’’
Kingston. His courage and devotion to prin-
ciple have been all the more keenly appreciated
because as political attributes they have not
exactly been hackneyed of late in Australia.
Kingston’s defection is, therefore, in the true
sense, staggering to the Barton Cabinet. To
the country it is in one regard a loss, in another a
gain. Australia will lose an honest and firm
administrator, whose courage was equal to en-
countering dishonesty in high places, and who
can hardly be succeeded by anyone of the same
calibre. But- it will gain a patriotic and vigilant
watchman. The Opposition so far in the Australian
Parliament has been contemptible in the extreme,
with but little ability, with less industry, and
with practically no patriotism. So the Barton
Government has often had to be supported, very
reluctantly, because the alternative of a Govern-
ment formed from the other side was too awful to
contemplate. Now there will be a nucleus for a
National Opposition. With Kingston, vigorous
and vigilant, on the cross benches, the position
for Premier Barton will be a very much changed
one ; and the prospect of an ex-Premier Barton
will not be so disquieting for Australians. When
the issue was between Barton and Reid it was a
case of the less of two evils. There is now
another alternative.
■ ♦
The Maoriland Bona-Fide Traveller.
At odd times the Maoriland Courts struggle with
the problem of the “bona-fide” traveller. There
are already about 52 distinct legal definitions, so
that a man can go through a whole year and be a
different sort of “bona-fide traveller” every
Sunday, and each way is more impossible and
exasperating than the others. The Appeal Court
has just struggled with a test case. One Sunday
a man living at Sydenham rode to Sumner
(distance eight miles). He stayed there 20
minutes and left for home. On his way home he
stopped and had a drink at Heathcote, which is
four miles from either Sumner or Sydenham. The
prosecution of the publican at Heathcote formed
the basis of the appeal. The law provides that a
man may be served with liquor on a Sunday when
he “arrives from a journey,” and it is the defini-
tion of “arrival’’and of “journey” that causes
the trouble. The police have of late been prose-
cuting publicans vigorously, and, according to the
way it feels, the prosecution makes the following
contentions, and gets verdict? on them most of
time:—
vvliile a traveller may, on arrival from a journey, drink
one pint of beer legally, yet if he drinks two half-pints in
succession and without any apparent interval, the publi-
can is breaking the law.
If a man starts out from London to Wellington via
Auckland overland, and reaches a hotel on the way on a
Sunday, he is not entitled to a drink, as he won’t have
“ arrived from his journey ” till the lights of Wellington
loom up on the lee.
If a man starts from A to B, with the intention of re-
turning to A one day before he dies, he does nolr “ arrive
from a journey ’’ till he is hack at A again, where, if it is
Sunday, he cannot get a drink anyway.
If a man rides from A to B, the distance being outside
the three-mile radius, and gets a drink, and goes back
the way he came, the drink is a legal drink, hut if he re-
turns by some other road and so makes a round trip, then
the drink is not a legal drink. (This contention was
made by the Crown Prosecutor before the Appeal Court,
and was upheld).
It is illegal to sell a drink to a man on Sunday, even if
he has ridden fifty miles at full gallop, if the publican
doesn’t satisfy himself that the journey was not made
simply and solely to get the drink.
All of this, summed up, makes it appear that a publican
had better refuse to sell drink to a man on Sunday unless
the man can prove that he has that moment arrived from
Alaska; that he did not come solely in order to get a
drink ; that he did not have a drink on the way ; that the
journey really did give him a legitimate thirst, and that
he is never going any further as long as he lives, so that
his arrival is quite definite. Then the publican can give
him one drink, and a policeman will watch the perform-
ance, and then go and have doubts and lay an information
against the publican, and the chances are that the pub-
lican will be fined, and there will be another appeal, and
the Appeal Court will struggle with the situation for the
fifty-third time, and provide the thirsty Maorilander
with a new way of being a bona-fide traveller to last
him into the first week of the second year. And all the
time Maoriland only wants one clear definition, and more
drinks.
By the way, during a licensing discussion before the
full Bench of Maoriland Supreme Court, the Chief Justice
said : “ Another extraordinary thing is, that if a man
makes a false representation and gets liquor, he is liable
to a fine of £5. If he makes a false representation and
does not get the liquor he is fined £20.”

and adds:
Why land-grant railways in Westralia should—as
compared with State-owned lines—have answered' so
ill both for their owners and the State is a question that
will occur to many who are acquainted with the remark-
able developments which similar undertakings have
gained for Canada. That the system has been a complete
failure in this part of the world both our two great land-
grant propositions have established.
And probably if the Morning Herald were in
Canada it would read that the land-system of rail-
ways had proved a great success in Australia, and
on that information it would ask how it happens

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I went to get my pension out—
You see I’m gettin’ grey,
And rheumatiz has built a nest
Where Springtime used to play;
A fat official looks me up
And then he looks me down:
“ I hope yer reppitation’s good
Within yer little town.
“ You’re sure you never took to drink,
Or broke the country’s lor,
Or spent a spell within a cell
Behind a prison-door;
You couldn’t earn a bob a day
No matter how you tried ?
And now you’ll take your Bible oath
To show you haven’t lied.”
I’d got (I thought) as good a claim
As e’er a dook or judge;
It never struck me ’twas a dole
They’d give you with a grudge;
I never had a princely screw,
Since toiling I began;
I only worked for common pay,
Just like a common man.
And savin’s not an easy game
When times aregoin’ slow,
And things are hard and work is slack
And troubles seem to grow;
Twas once the wife whose sickness came.
Then I —l had a fall,
And doctors’ bills came thick and fast
And took our little all.
So when I saw the Pensions come
My heart was drummin’ gay,
I didn’t want a pauper’s home
To close my weary day ;
You see my neck was always stiff,
My work was always true,
And by this pension scheme I thought
The country felt it, too.
But—seems to me—l reckoned wrong,
For, since I gave my name,
A sort o’ shadow seems to fall
And cloud the door wi’ shame ;
I hear the p’lice are lookin’ out
A nd siftin’ every tale;
And, if they doubt the rheumatiz,
You will not doubt the gaol.
I never thought the Pension meant —
“ A Pauper till you die ! ”
I’ve not been used to bow and beg—
I’ve not been used to lie;
So now the Statesmen cut us down
And cry— “ Retrenchment Ho! ”
I’ll fling with pride their dole aside
And let the Pension go!
PHILANDER FLAM.


that a system which does so well in Australia is so
much of a failure in Canada. A whole lot
of things that are alleged to be great
successes a long way off are not reckoned
to be even small successes by the people
whose corns they are treading on. The only ab-
solutely safe plan is, having tried the weight of a
thing on our own corns, and found that it isn t
pleasant, to drop it—it is mere foolishness to rely
upon the probably unreliable yarn that it hasn t
proved at all heavy on someone else’s corns. The
tale that the land-grant system has been a huge
success in Canada is pretty certainly unreliable.
As the Ottawa correspondent of the Conservative
Otago (M.L.) Daily Times put it lately
The judgment of the people, enlightened by a some
what bitter experience during the past five years, has
now swung to the other side. .. . After having spoken
reproachfully for a generation of Government ownership
in railways, our people are now strongly in favor of carry-
ing the Government railway system to the Pacific Coast.
In fact, the conditions which have sprung up within the
last six years have changed the view of our people in
many respects.
So, after all, the story of the enormous, sucoess of
the land-grant and subsidy system in Canada
seems to have been all a flam.
The Remedy for the N.S.W. Public Works
Muddle.
The Bulletin reeeives a lengthy communication
from “ A.M.” as to the best way of abolishing
Owe’Sullivanism. The gist of it is as follows :
The N.S.W. Public Works Department should be re-
organised and placed under the control of a Board of
Management entirely free from political influences.
There should be a competent man at the head of the
Board, with a salary commensurate with the responsi-
bilities attaching to the position. The chief, perhaps,
should be imported from America as the State wants a
hustler in charge. The State could afford to pay £4OOO a
year to an official who, if competent, would save it several
times his salary. The Board would have complete control
of constructive operations and have the same powers over
its employees as the Railway Commissioners. The Depart-
ment would be placed under the jurisdiction of the
Arbitration Court, so that it would have no opportunity
of reducing wages below the ruling rates or of introducing
evils which possibly an American-trained captain of
industry might have a liking for.
This is the sort of proposition that makes The
Bulletin feel sorry. It is so pathetic in its help-
lessness, and its confidence in the shibboleth of
“abolishing political influence,” and in its utter
inability to realise the real nature of the disease.
There is no refuge from corruption in importing a
man from America—the land of the trust octopus,
of Tammany, of the ward politician, andthebood-
ling alderman, and the gentleman who said he and
his partners discussed the advisability of bribing
AlfredDeakin but decided the idea was of no use,
and that they had struck in Australia, that in-
credible thing—an incorruptible politician. The
Public Works Department, too, can’t be run by a
non-political board like the railways. It is mostly
a Department which spends money on works
yielding no direct return. The Railways can be
worked on the business rule of not building any
new line that doesn’t hold out good promise of
paying so much per cent., but there is no such
business rule about roads, culverts, and bridges.
And if it is held that the one way to make
everything right is to put everything under
non-political management, and place one De-
partment after another in charge of a possibly
corrupt despot, then Parliament might as well be
abolished altogether. The theory that Australians
are unfit to manage their own affairs, and that the
remedy for everything is to give almost unlimited
powers to some foreign boss, certainly suggests the
abolition of Parliament. Yet there is nothing
wrong with political management in itself —only
with the circumstances attaching to it.
The simple, obvious, effective remedy lies in
the abolition of borrowing and the abolition
of the practice of using the proceeds of land sales
as revenue instead of applying them to public
works or in reduction of the public debt. It is
only the loan disease which makesOwe’Sullivanism
possible, and which has bloated out the roads and
bridges member to his present dimensions. The
fact that extravagance doesn’t mean extra taxation
in the present but merely so much more liability
to hand down to posterity (and who cares for pos-
terity ?) is the one thing which makes the country
careless about extravagance. The fact that a
Ministry can gain popularity by scattering useless
or needless public works with both hands, and
being lavish about the “dirt money,” without gain-
ing any corresponding unpopularity by increasing
the taxes to pay for these things, is the reason
why Owe’Sullivan exists. The circumstance that
bridges and culverts drop down from the skies in
a manner of speaking, causes the department-
trotting member to be looked upon as an un-
alloyed benefactor, whereas if there were no loans
he would be looked upon partly as a benefactor
and partly as a noxious person who increased the
taxes, and one aspect would largely counteract
the other. If Owe’Sullivan’s Fitzroy Dock
muddle meant, as a matter of absolute necessity,
that the Owe’Sullivan Government must increase
the taxes, amid general execration, to pay for the
muddle, then a very different Owe’Sullivan
would have run a very different kind of dock.
The beginning, middle and end of political con-
fusion, corruption, incapacity and extravagance in
Australia is the loan disease and the habit of using
land-sale money as revenue. If these evils did
not exist no man could gain applause by
throwing away public funds, for the result would
come home to the whole community at once, and
for one blessing there would be ten or a hundred
execrations. Under a non-borrowing regime,
rigid purity of administration and the art of
making a shilling do the work of half-a-crown
would become the great public virtues of Aus-
tralia ; and the Ministry would get out of the
pottering habit of taking a personal interest in
Bill’s “dirt money,” because it.would have other
votes besides Bill’s to consider. But no mere
.scheme for shifting departments from political con-
trol would do any permanent good.

The Fitzroy Dock Report.
Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan writes as follows :
Your article on the above subject, though vigorous, is
very misleading. It is clear from its terms that you have
taken your views from certain official evidence only—
from men whose positions were at stake. Why rely upon
ex parte testimony in a case like this ? It is not correct,
as you imply, that I am the author of the political patron-
age and other abuses whicli prevailed at both Docks at
Cockatoo. The evidence of independent witnesses proves
that a very unsatisfactory state of things existed in these
directions long before I took office. Two previous investi-
gations had been made into complaints about the Docks,
but very little action was taken with regard to them.
Under my predecessors, men defied overseers to dismiss
them pointing out that they had influential Ministers at
their back. Even lately, confusion prevailed owing to
quarrels between overseers, and factions existed bearing
the names of these overseers. There was a lack of con-
trol and a want of discipline that told against the execu-
tion of work at a fair cost. It is notorious that heads of
branches for that reason did not care to send their work
to the Dock. Mr. Hickson; late Under secretary for
Public Works, who ought to know better than any man,
gave the following evidence in reply to questions put to
him: ■
Q We have it in evidence that there was very little
interference up to a few years ago ?
A. There was always interference, more or less.
Q. What shape did it take in your time ?
A. It took this shape: If the foreman or superin-
tendent thought a man was not fit for the work
he dismissed him. Some member would make a
representation, and the man would be put back
again after a while. A good deal of that was
done.
This was before my time. Where, then, is the justice of
imputing to me the blame of introducing political
interference 1 All I did was to follow the prac-
tice of my predecessors, and then only in cases
of distress, generally marking the note, “if there
is a vacancy.” I am really the man who killed political
influence at the Dock. When I took office I found that
trades unionists were not in favor at the Dock, but I took
care to put them on a fair footing with other men.
When I did so some non-unionists were discharged
to embarrass me, but these I at once re-instated. Small-
minded and tyrannical actions of this kind frequently
occurred, and I never failed to give justice to the men
when they complained. Why should I not do so?
A Minister should rule as well as reign, and he is the
last Court of Appeal for those who cannot get justice
elsewhere. These are the real complaints against my
administration of affairs at the Dock, and I am not
ashamed of them. You may, if you please, call them
“ Owe’Sullivanism,” but there are 60,000 trades unionists
in New South Wales, besides tens of thousands of fair-
minded persons not in unions, who will be inclined to
term it a policy of right and justice. That I was justified
in my action is proved by the change of Superintendents
at the Dock and other reforms which will save the
country about £30,000 per annum. What you absurdly
call “ The Owe’Sullivan Infamy ” is, therefore, rather a
benefit than a bane to New South Wales.
In reply to which—
(l) The Bulletin took its facts from a report of the Eoyal
Commission on the Cockatoo Island Docks. This Com-
mission was appointed by the See-Owe’Sullivan Govern-
ment and was sworn to make a judicial inquiry ; the evi-
dence taken by it was all on oath. If Mr. Owe’Sullivan
wishes to impugn that report, either on the ground of
corruption on the part of the Commissioners, or whole-
sale perjury on the part of the witnesses, why does he
not say so directly ?
(2) It is true that “ a very unsatisfactory state of things"
existed at the dock “ long before Mr. Owe’Sullivan took
office ” —so long before, indeed, that the fact hasn’t much
pertinence to the present issue. In 1887, it seems, the
dock was in a very bad way. In 1899, however (Mr.
Owe’Sullivan came into office in Sept., 1899), an inquiry •
as to the dock was held, and it was reported “ that the
officers then employed were competent and steady, that
the staff was insufficient, that the superintendent had
full power to take on or discharge men, as the exigencies
of the work may require, and that he was untrammelled
in this respect and in the management of the men.” In
April 1903 (Mr. Owe’Sullivan had' been then nearly
four years in office) another inquiry was held, and
it was then reported that political influence was rife;
that the office of superintendent was degraded by
Ministerial interference and left without real authority;
that Mr. Owe’Sullivan at his own instance and at the
dictation of politicians insisted on men being “ put on,”
prevented men from being dismissed, decided questions
as to individual increases and allowances ; and that “ the
Minister’s direct and active interest in all matters con-
nected with the employment and control of the men has
reduced all the officials nominally responsible for the dock
to figureheads.” It is for the difference between 1899 and
1903 that Mr. Owe’Sullivan is to be held responsible, and
that is the difference between moderate efficiency and
ruinous extravagance under scandalous political inter-
ference.
(3) The by.play about trades-unionism and the 60,000
trades-unionists’is characteristic Owe’Sullivanese, but it
has nothing todo with the case. It is, unfortunately, quite
possible that very many trades-unionists will endorse the
Owe’Sullivan policy, because it gave some trades-
unionists petty and dishonest advantages, just as a great
many people will endorse any injustice, or extravagance,
or dishonesty from which they get a personal benefit.
B,ut if it is a fact—which we very much doubt—that
60,000 trades-unionists will support the Owe’Sullivan in
wasting the country’s money and dragging the principle
of State enterprise in the dust, it doesn’t alter the merits
of the case one atom. If 600,000 people were willing to
sacrifice a great principle for a little dirt-money the
principle wouldn’t be affected thereby.
(4) The claim of Mr. Owe’Sullivan that he really
deserves credit for having “ reformed ” the dock is sheer
impudence. A house-breaker interrupted in the transfer
to his cart of the last instalment of valuables, and
deciding, in consequence of that interruption, not to
appropriate any more that day, might just as well claim
credit for his honesty.
(5) It will be noticed that Mr. Owe’Sullivan gives no
promise that, having been checked at Fitzroy Dock, he
intends to take that as a hint to change his methods in
other quarters. There isn’t, in fact, a trace of sackcloth
or ashes about him. He seems mostly disposed to. whoop
with joy over the prospect of his misdoings securing for
him the support of “ 60,000 unionists."
The Queensland Loan and the Labor
Parties.
The Australian Labor parties are likely to soon
find themselves in a forked stick. Queensland
has just asked for a small loan of £750,000 —hardly
more than enough to make good the deficits of
the past two years—and the appeal would have
failed utterly but that the issue had been under-
written at a stiff price. That is to say, though there
are few legitimate buyers of the stock, speculators
promise, for a heavy consideration, to take up any
part of the loan that the public won’t take, and
take their chance of selling later on. So crowded,
however, is the market that even the offering of a
paltry £750,000 loan has been followed by a serious
drop in the price of stocks. Worse still, the
Times announces that no more Australian loans
will be underwritten in London for a long while to
come. The underwriters, it seems, are full up.
In earlier days, when they simply got their
percentage for guaranteeing the loan, and hadn’t
to take up any of it, the underwriting game
paid well. But year by year the public has
taken less of the loan, and the underwriters
have had to take more, and each time they found
their securities less saleable, till at last they
are loaded down with millions of our paper which
the public won’t take off their hands except at
a heavy loss. The game doesn’t pay; and the
more we borrow the lower go our securities,
and the underwriters’only hopes of selling their
accumulation grow still less. The only people
who have, for some years past, been lending us
money to any great extent, it is announced, refuse
to lend any more. The Queensland loan just
floated is to be the last. Assuming this story to
be true, several of the Parliamentary Labor
parties are in a bad position. They have cer-
tainly kept extravagant Governments in office.
They have held in the highest favor those
Ministers who have been guilty of the most
extravagance. While they have been, generally
speaking, anxious to have money spent, they
have taken no great interest in the raising of
money. They have fought hard for high
wages for workmen, for short working hours,
for State employment for the unemployed ; but
they have fought hardly at all for an absentee
tax, a stiff land tax, or a higher tax on income
derived from property. They have hardly madfe
the least attempt at promoting solvency and
honest finance. There have, in consequence,
been deficits, against which the Labor party made
no protest ; and while there are deficits there
must be either borrowing or repudiation of debts.
When we can’t borrow there will be a burst of
blind fury against those who have brought the
country to this pass. Just because the Labor
party has been to so great an extent allied with
extravagance, and has shown so little regard for
the question where the money was to come
from, the people will naturalljr turn to the
opponents of that party when it is necessary
to pursue a new policy. That happened in
Victoria; and the consequences were disastrous
to the workers and the poor and aged. The
Labor party’s particular business is to protect
these people ; yet for the severe trouble that came
upon them me Labor party was in this way very
largely responsible. If it had been known as the
party of honesty and fair dealing; if it had in-
sisted upon every State paying its way like an
honest person ; if it had been as much concerned
about paying the bill as it was about incurring the
debt, the people would have had no excuse for
going elsewhere for salvation, and then the salva-
vation would not have taken a shape so unpleasant
to the people in whom the Labor party is most in-
terested. Its failure and neglect hit its own
friends hardest in the end. The public must
turn away from Owe’Sullivan and his works
when the trouble comes, and it can’t do that now
without turning away from the Labor party,
which has identified itself with Owe’Sullivan.
When the public sees the part that the Labor
people have taken so far, how can it, as a
matter of ordinary commonsense, be expected to
entrust the carrying-out of the financial reform
policy to men who were so largely responsible for
the very opposite policy ? Then, if the people for
this reason rush into the arms of the opponents of
the Labor party, it means that the “economy
will take very much the same unpleasant shape
as it has taken in Victoria. Even at this late hour
the Laborites may, nevertheless, save themselves.
But they can only do it by showing unmistakably
that they are interested not simply m the spending
of money but also in the raising of money; that
they are capable of saying “No’’to a request
even to raise a workman’s wages, if there is no
money wherewith to pay. They must shake off
this evil reputation which they have acquired by
their neglect to interest themselves in the financial
side of politics. If they don’t, they are doomed.
Flogging the Corpse.
The Argus and its well-to-do friends are just now
making desperate efforts to keep alive or revive
the Kyabram nightmare, which pranced to such
effect at the last Vic. State elections. At the
time of writing it isn’t quite certain whether the
animal is dead or only intoxicated ; but the
Argus, hoping for the best, is using the whip
with great violence, and paid secretary and hired
agitator Dean, who has a salaried billet with the
animal, is waving his arms frantically and ex-
horting the apparently defunct to make an effort.
A day or two ago a commencement was made
with what was called “ The Country. Campaign,
and the opening meeting was held, in. an almost
invisible town in the western district, where
Dean, who had travelled 150 miles to reach them,
preached to seven men and a cattle-dog. This
meeting was typical of the present attitude of
“the country producer” towards the whole ’Pootra
business, though, of course, it may be only tempo-
rary weariness on his part. Even in Warrnam-
bool, a town 6f over 6000 inhabitants,, only about
20 people assembled to hear the glad tidings. The
exasperating feature of this case was that the
same hall had, only three weeks previously, been
thronged by a crowd of 700 to hear Tom Mann
denounce the’Pootra’s pet Government. To the
surprise and disgust of the “Reformers,’ Mann
had splendid receptions all over the Western
District—a district that reeks with Toryism—even
pastoral centres like Hamilton and Camperdown
giving him good meetings. It is probable that
the success of Mann’s tour had something to do
with the selection of the Western District for the
opening of the “Reform” campaign; and the
unfortunate part of the business is that, now that
the Dean pilgrimage is proving a failure, people
are beginning to contrast it with Mann’s success-
ful tour, and point the finger of contumely at the
’Pootra itself. The impudent attempt of the
league to interfere with the Federal Parliament,
in connection with the Judiciary Bill, is bitterly
resented by Democrats, who are nursing their
revenge for the approaching elections. The sud-
den falling in of Lancaster and Doddard’s
League, and the indifference with which
it is now regarded by the vast majority
of electors, is a comforting sign, and next
Victorian State, elections will exhibit a
very strong reaction from the high-water Tory
mania that obsessed Victoria last year. The
portly old gentlemen with side whiskers and tall
hats who ambled down to Melbourne, at their
country’s expense, a few weeks ago, talked about
the corpse as if it were really alive, and the daily
paper headings—“ Enthusiastic meeting of Re-
form Delegates,” and the like—no doubt helped
to keep up the delusion. By the time of the next
annual conference, however, Kyabram will pro-
bably have dropped so completely back into its
old obscurity that it will only be remembered by
its arrears of water rates.

Interference With Private Enterprise.
It is reported with some pomp and. considerable
pride that a great combination is being arranged
in New South Wales, Victoria, and Queensland
to wipe the floor with the present Australian
“Socialist Labor Government ” at next election.
The Pastoralists’ Union has thrown in its lot with
the movement, and all the Tory elements of
society are included. In the manifestoes of the
combination it is declared that the foolish &nd
criminal interference with private enterprise that
is now going on must be suppressed, and Fat,
which has been for a time down below, must de-
finitely take its position on deck once more. The
Bulletin would feel some little sympathy even
with a movement of this kind if it could find out
where lies that great “ private enterprise ” about
which the Tory party rages so grievously. If
the great foundries, steel-nulls, shipbuilding
yards, textile factories, and so on of Australia
had been built up by a cruel policy of sweating, it
would be lamentable enough, but still the nidus-
tries would be there, and they would be something
to be proud of in a way. The trouble is to find
these great evidences of private enterprise. Aus-
tralia had many years of freedom from Labor
legislation, and except in Victoria, where a little
was done in the way of manufactures, the private
enterprise of the Tory crowd consisted almost
wholly in buying land from the State for a mere
song or getting it for nothing at all, and then
sitting down to watch the sheep grow and the bull
flourish by natural process. The country certainly
has banks and insurance offices largely owned in
Britain; railways, telegraphs and telephones, all
owned in Britain; drains, roads and harbors mostly
owned in Britain. Even its mines are getting to be
more and more owned in Britain every year. For
the rest, Australia imports more, in proportion to
population, than almost any other country on
earth, because, apart from the passive industries
of watching the sheep increase and the bull
flourish (which were just what Abraham did in
the backblocks of Genesis), it has less enterprise
than almost any other country on earth. Even
the sheep and bull industry is not up to Abra-
ham’s standard; it rests largely on cadging upon
the State for unprofitable railways, free railway
carriage, irrigation grants, and other aids, while
the old patriarch ran his industry on plain busi-
ness principles. The lamentation about the
State’s interference with private enterprise, on
the part of a class which never had any enterprise
worth mentioning, is a humorous affair. If the
Australian Tory party represented all the art,
enterprise and industry of Manchester, Glasgow,
Lyons, Rouen, Chicago, and Pittsburg combined,
instead of speaking mainly for a wool-wash and a
boiling-down establishment, it could hardly put
on more frill than it does.
The Age of Consent.
N.S.W. Parliament will shortly—at the instance
of Dr. Mackellar —approach that awkward sub-
ject, the Age of Consent law. At present, on
reaching the age of 14, a human female is legally
a woman in the sense that she can consent to
marriage, or to the rites of marriage without the i
ceremony. Dr. Mackellar proposes to raise
that age to 16, and there is a further sug-
gestion that 18 is a more fitting age. Probably,
to the minds of some, 80 would be even more
appropriate. The legal determination of the
age of female maturity is a subject on which a
general agreement is impossible, and a wise
decision most difficult. There is a section of male
maniacs against whom female infants need pro-
tection —the most effective protection the law can
design. There is, on the other hand, a large sec-
tion of vicious female children, abnormally pre-
cocious in physique and in wickedness, against
whom the male community needs to be guarded.
In Victoria, where the age of consent is 16,
there have arisen numerous scandalous cases
of hardened young wantons of under 16—
after, perhaps, two or three years of vice-
picking on some paramour as a subject of
blackmail, enforceable by threat of prosecution
for one of the most serious offences in the criminal
calendar. To put a check on the unhuman male
animal and at the same time to prevent, as far as
possible, the savage punishment of lads and men
who might be drawn into relations with females,
young in years but adult in body and depraved in
mind —that is the puzzling problem which con-
fronts the law. The majority of Australian-
females are certainly unfitted at 14 —men-
tally to gauge the responsibilities of mother-
hood, physically to undertake them. - At
16, it is equally certain, very many 'are
still unfitted. Development is a matter
which varies greatly. There are women at 12
and there are immature girls at 20. Wha,t is
wanted is that no female shall be persuaded into
a step possibly meaning motherhood when proper
physical development is lacking, and when mental
development is insufficient to allow her to gauge
its importance. (The mental question is, after
all, the more serious one ; it is against equity that
anyone should be persuaded into a contract, -
which Nature will, in most cases, insist on being
kept, whilst mentally an infant.) That is what is
wanted, and what seems also to be practically im-
possible, for it would call for an age of consent
varying from, say, 14 to 50; there are mentally de-
ficient women whom it would be a crime to co-
habit with at 50. What it is necessary to
bear in mind is that legislation is only wanted for
a very small—almost an infinitesimal—section of
the community, and that, with a high age of
consent, the number of lads made liable to savage
punishment for actions which are not, in the civil
sense, criminal, would be enormously increased.
A possible way out of the difficulty would be to
increase slightly the age at which consent is
lawful, making the proviso that it would
be a bar to conviction if an accused person
could show by medical evidence, either that the
prosecutrix had been previously of vicious life, or
that the accused was honestly deceived as to her
age, and she was, physically and mentally, mature
enough to give consent. That doesn’t seem a
very satisfactory solution. It would, perhaps, be
as well to leave things as they are—with a protec-
tion up to 14 against everyone, and up to 16
against a teacher, a guardian, or an employer.
