An Implication.
An obscure and, at first sight, harmless-looking
Paragraph appeared in the papers a few days ago.
t was published simply as an item of political in-
telligence, and the fact that it was loaded doesn t
seem to have occurred seriously to anybody so far:
The Commonwealth Government has decided not to
propose any loan to Parliament for public works during
the present session, consequently only the most urgent
works will be carried out. Among those not to be carried
out are the reconstruction of the telephone bureaux in
Sydney and Melbourne.
That item doesn’t look much, but nothing morO:
nefarious has been compressed into so small a
space for a very long while. The off-hand manner
in which it assumes certain things as being too
obvious to require proof or argument makes it all
the more irritating. In six lines it is put forward
as a self-evident fact—a fact that doesn’t even need
to be stated at length—that borrowing is the
natural and inevitable condition of this country,
and that the Australian Government must bor-.
row just as it must breathe. It is assumed that the.
present non-borrowing attitude of the Australian
Government is, as a matter of course, only a tem-
porary attitude; that no one can possibly dream
of building public works without borrowingand
that the period of non-borrowingmustnecessarilybe
a period of st agnation when the most essential pub-
lic undertakings are hung up and public buildings
fall helplessly into disrepair—all for want of loans.
The telephone buildings of Sydney and Melbourne
were both built out of loans. The security having
’ decayed or become antiquated, though the debt is
as fresh as ever, it is taken for granted that anew
debt must be incurred to reconstruct the old
fallen-down assets. Also other works, not speci-
fied, are, as a matter of course, postponed till the
loans begin to rain down on us again. By such
implications the daily press keeps the delusion
alive that we are, in some way or other, a Peculiar
People, who, unlike all other peoples on earth,
can’t be expected to pay for any work whatever
out of our own resources, or to pay off any of our
back debts, or to even keep our mortgaged assets
in repair so that they can maintain a pretence of
being sufficient value for the loans they represent.
The anti-loan policy of the Australian Govern-
ment is absolutely worthless if it is allowed to be
understood that it is only kept up by postponing
necessary works. On that basis Federal borrowing
will commence just as soon as the heap of post-
poned works grows so large that further postpone-
ment is impossible. The idea must be got rid of
that the non-borrowing period is an interval
during which we necessarily slide back towards
aboriginal barbarism—during which all the public
undertakings that distinguish the Barton Govern-
ment from the King Billy Government must
needs decay so that, if the non-borrowing period
only lasts long enough, we will get back to the
Billy period in the long run. Australia requires
a Government whichlwill not borrow, and which
will at the same time, construct all necessary
public works even if it taxes the big Victorian
landlords to get the money. It requires a Govern-
ment that will make the non-borrowing policy
respectable and permanent—not one that associ-
ates it with decay, broken roofs, lack of paint,
lack of progress, and a general falling-in of the
public assets. And when Australia gets a differ-
ent kind of press—one that doesn’t imply things
like the above—it will get that kind of Gqvern-
ment. ,

Not until Nellie Stewart demonstrated the
fact in “ A Country Mouse” did Sydneysiders
realise the absolute, baby-lamb air of innocence
that may go with a short, waistless white frock, a
yellow plait, a wreath of forget-me-nots, long
mittens, and a mouth painted to the size and
Shape and color of a red daisy-bud. A wisp of
lace handkerchief which she—the Mouse—twists
around her blue-ribboned wrist as she looks up into
the faces of dissipated noblemen, and excuses her
ignorance with, “I live in the country, you
know,” completes a valuable set of “props”
—When manipulated by such an artist as Nellie.
A “Country Mouse” crept on to Sydney Royal
stage for the first time last Saturday, and gnawed
her way straight to the playgoer’s heart. She is
a 19-year-old girl “from Dorking,” by name
Angela Muir, and she comes to London to visit
high-placed relatives who are in a fast crowd. Into
this intriguing, cigarette-smoking, punt-shoving,
hansom-cabbing set Angela “brings a breath as
of new-mown hay.” So the men assert. And they
—the men—all feel “ the better for knowing her,”
and with tears in their eyes (hitherto fixed on
some neighbor’s wife) “ wish they had met her 10
years ago.” And all the time Angela is as sly as
a; bag of cats and is out for a titled hus-
band, whom, of course, she secures—after various
croquet parties and a very compromising situa-
tion from which she and her ducal lover escape
by flight up a sooty chimney. Among the sub-
sidiary characters is the fashionablv-fast Lady
Sylvia, who is tired of her solid, rich, middle-aged
brewer husband, and determines to elope with
one Honorable Mr. Vyse, who, in the meantime,
has fallen in love with the buttercup from Dork-
ing. Miss Minnie Sadler plays Lady Sylvia as the
woman with' superb gowns and no soul ought to be
played, and the part of her beer-making husband
Is a fine study in the hands of returned actor
C. B. YVestmacott. Hareourt Beatty, as Hoh.
Yyse, acts the male complement of Lady Sylvia
as well as such a featherweight part can be done.
Miss Cynthia Brooke made her debut on Saturday,
but hadn’t much chance to make anything else, in
part of a lady of quality who desires to marry
a tailor’s jag named Lord Robert Wyckham.
Lord Bob is played bv Sydney Stirling. Alfred
lapping is the idiotic old-young Duke, and Harry
Hill tnakes a capital study of an ancient servitor.
* * * *
“Country Mouse” is preceded by “A
Pantomine Rehearsal.” Author of the curtain
raiser ought to be boiled alive—if he’s not already
Under the clay. By the way, his name is Clay.
“ Mice and Men ” will frolic for this Thursday
night only, and on Friday Miss Stewart will
farewell in “Sweet Nell of Old Drury” with
the bad king and the dog and the other appur-
tenances.

“ A Midsummer Night’s Dream ” com-
mences at Sydney Royal on Saturday. There
are some facts, 1 dee the deluge and the general
resurrection, which speak for themselves and
require no elaboration. This is one of them.
* iff * iff
Her Majesty’s Theatre, Sydney, which
took .fire some time in the dim back ages, and
burned Ben Hur's chariot, and the flaring city of
Antioch, and the great schemes of Messala, and
Pontius Pilate’s glory, and the lepers outside
Jerusalem and many other valuables, has been
resurrected. The partially-combusted building
lingered for so many months in ash and melan-
choly that it seemed to have settled dovyn into a
permanent desolation, but the work of resurrection
was done at last very suddenly. On Saturday
the resuscitated structure opens with a triple bill,
“Madame Butterfly,” which is new to Australia
—selected, perhaps, to signify that the old
singed theatrical caterpillar is itself again-*
“Kerry,” and “Cousin Joe.” J. C. William-
son, whose splendid record as an actor is
apt to be forgotten by a young generation of play-
goers that knows him onlv as Australia’s leading
manager, reappears as Kerry, a character that
was long so exclusively Iris own that it would be a
E to see it fair into other hands. It often
pens that when a retired veteran reappears on
the stage by General Desire, that military func-
tionary is afterwards very sorry he spoke, but in
this case he won’t be sorry. Mr. Williamson also
takes the part of Cousin jQe, and thsre also
General Desire will be almost certainly glad that
he signified himself. The new company includes
Misses Mollison, Susie Vaughan and Rose Mus-
grove, also Harry Plimmer a.n,d Philip Lytton.
Proceeds of the first night gp, to charity, and as
the first four seats offered fetched £4B in all
charity has made a very fair start.

Why anyone should buy tw.o pork-pies and
a crayfish for supper, when he can get just the
same results by going to Sydney Criterion and
witnessing “ Because I Love You,” The Bulletin
doesn t know. It is the most gruesome horror of
tliewi iiter-even Anderson’s play of “The Stomach
at the Door didn’t quite equal it. The central
qharacter is a monstrosity—half human, half
vulturq, with talons instead of fingers, a beaked
nose, a croak, and.a latent thirst for blood. Into
the claws of this repulsive freak, in his subterra-
nean chamber of hprrors, the wicked woman, who
wants the_ khaki hero for herself, lures the
heroine, being-aided by an alleged scientist who
wants to study a question of heredity. Of
course, the khaki hero intervenes in time to
save the. but not before the audience
has been treated' to some repulsive nonsense,
bbakspeare had a,good deal to answer for when
hq.gave us Caliban, and created a precedent for
stage monstrosities but Shakspeare doesn’t ex-
cise Scudamore, about, whose production there is
nothing striking, apart from the monstrosity.
Oharles.Hollow.ay mounts the loathsome piece far
better than it deserves. Miss Beatrice Holloway
as Norina Chandos, who is trapped in the
monster’s quarters, gives further proof of her
natural abilities; her work is even and pains-
Vivian Edwards is a fairly satisfactory
khaki hero. _ Albert Lucas, as the uncanny
freak, hides his talons as much as possible, which
may be accounted to him for virtue. Miss Alice
Deorwyn and E. G. Coughlan have the comedy
parts and make a good deal of the poor material
they have to work upon. John Hesford, in one
ot the few parts that are not unreal, and Rose
Conroy, the passionate and unscrupulous lover,
may also be numbered among the successes.

Probability and the man who wrote “The
Ladder of Life,” the resurrected melodrama at
Sydney Lyceum, were utter strangers. They
didn t meet in the first act, and the man didn’t
think it worth while afterwards to even get
so far acquainted as to tell Probability what
sort of weather it was. The plan of writing a
drama on these terms has its obvious advan-
tages. You can do what you like and don’t have
to explain why you do it. If you have a girl in a
room and want to kill her (Heaven only knows
why, but that doesn’t matter), you might stick
her with a knife or shoot her. (You have a gun
and a knife on you—you always have at the
.Lyceum.) But you don’t do anything so com-
mon ; you put her head under a steam-hammer.
Ihe hammer ought to come down thud, and that
would end the business ; but it doesn’t—it hangs
up long enough to let three Cockney High-
landers swing themselves down from the
ceding and save the girl. Then the curtain
comes down and you shout like mad in order
to see the girl saved the same way again; but, as
the mechanical effects won’t allow of that, the
orchestra strikes up and you buy three-pennorth
of peanuts instead. The bulk result is a succession
ot thrills and gorgeous tableaux which would be
impossible if Probability had any say in the
matter. The hammer business is printed in the
biggest type on the bills; but the crowd (and the
.Lyceum is nearly always crowded under Wm
Anderson’s management) likes the Storming of
J -A ei "!' ta °t P a rgai alI( i the Disembarkation of
the Gordon Highlanders best; they really are
striking and well-managed stage pictures. There
a £ e 5. cou P le of hundred people, or more, “on ”in
the disembarkation scene, besides a score or so of
horses—black horses, from somebody’s under-
takery and coffin-mill apparently ; very decorous
horses indeed. The players don’t amount to
much; stage manager (J. H. Nunn) is the
principal actor this time, and about the only
improvement he could make would be to issue
instructions that next time the Gordons
storm the heights of Dargai they should use
smokeless powder. Last Saturday night the
audience forgot its damp, cold feet, and demanded
that the Gordons should go on storming the
heights so long that by the time the curtain was
allowed to stay down' the building was filled
with smoke, and one couldn’t read on the adver-
tising “drop ” whose pills it is that cure that pale
feeling in tired people.

Sydney Tivoli strengthened its programme
considerably last week with W. C. Fields—a
clever juggler, who does some tricks after the
manner of Cinquevalli, and exhibits considerable
skill and some humor. Another newcomer is
Miss Frances Leslie, a pleasant soprano. The
entertainments of Rudinoff show no sign of stale-
ness; he does a new smoke picture now, and
varies his programme otherwise—in all except the
jokes; they are immutable.

** * *
the Pace,” the latest production
at Melbourne Royal, is reminiscent of some by-
gone horse-dramas which were not so well ,ribbed-
up or so easy in their action. It breaks new
ground before turning into the beaten track. The
first scene shows a virtuous Baker dissipating the
remnants of his fortune in order that he may de-
vote himself to hard work without the encum-
brance of any worldly possession except his wife.
Ihe lady, who is Frances Ross, shares her hero’s
enthusiasm for this reckless slaughter of furni-
ture and effects. It seems that Baker’s for-
tune came to him from a dead uncle in the
usury line of business, and because uncle’s-
gold was ill-gotten the inheritor placates
an outraged Providence by spending his substance
in champagne luncheons and fox-hunting. There-
fore another usurer, a comparatively mild and
amiable robber named Abe Nathan, buys back
the spendthrift’s goods and chattels at an alarm-
ing reduction, and leaves him to battle impecuni-
P ie same old false suspicions and
difficulties wherefrom Baker has emerged trium-
phant in about forty-nine previous dramas.
Albert Norman, as a circus rider of Spanish
extraction, conducts tjie villainy against Baker,
with the assistance of the Betrayed Girl and her
furious wooden-headed father (Arthur Styan).
Brown and Miss Ireland, from the circus, also
Bland Holt, as a horsey person who was born
m a hay-loft, and weaned on oats' and Mrs.
Holt, as a familiar Ruth or Naomi character, are
ranged on the side of in-no-cence. Eventually
Albert Norman gets run to earth by Baker whilst
they are both performing in Brown’s circus, and
then the clouds roll away, and the wooden-headed
father apologises for his mistake. In the second
act, Baker had returned from a fox hunt with the
live body of Norman slung across the pommel of
his saddle, but in that case the capture of the
villain led to nothing definite. It was merely
introduced as an astounding event.
“ Going the Pace ” puts no strain on the
powers of the co. as far as the principal characters
are concerned, and some of the minor parts are
likewise played for all they are worth. Max
Maxwell and A. Scarlett, who often deserve to be
passed over with something harder than silence,
happen to fill their places very satisfactorily this
time. The erring spinster is not a brilliant
actress, but she has her good points, and it is
greatly to her credit that she hovers around the
dear old home in an ordinary impenitent costume
instead of a woeful cloak of invisible green.
Baker, Bland Holt and other important per-
formers of speaking parts are not more important
than the characters that don’t talk at all. The
last scene contains numerous horses, and twenty
silent ballet girls. There are horses at every
stage of the drama; horses ambling over a hurdle,
horses in a cab yard, horses running round an
arena with bounding jockeys and Mexican marvels
on their aged backs. The arrest of the villain is
an equestrian sensation, and the general verdict
on Blandolt’s show last Saturday was that it will
gee.
** * *
The Marshall-Hall Orchestra gave more
than a good concert at Melbourne Town Hall last
Saturday. With some 70 performers under his
command the masterful Professor led them to a
really splendid attack on the “Tannhauser”
Overture and the “Ride of the Valkyrie,” not to
mention two other Wagner pieces. As for the
principal item on the programme, Strauss’s
“Death and Transfiguration,” the Orchestra
didn’t account for the European reputation of
that pretentious tone-poem, although the Trans-
figuration end of it was impressive—about one-
tenth part as impressive as the bright and
heavenly section of Chopin’s Funeral March.
** * *
The first week of “The Serenade” at
Melbourne Princess’s brought some hope that
its weak-minded story will not prevent the music
and glitter of the opera-bouffe getting their
financial deserts. Given a more attractive and
nimble leading lady in the shoes of Madame
Slapoffski, and a Florence Young to sing the
music that falls to May Beatty, the tuneful
work would be popular enough. More
pleasing than “The Fortune Teller” it is
not less strong in sound and substance.
Even Edward Lauri’s conversational songs
have capital choral backgrounds, and the composi-
tion of the higher-toned numbers includes honey
and ginger as a rule. The vast unmusicianly
majority of the public can perceive airs as well as
graces in the music, and the average eyesight dis-
covers goodness in the spectacle. But the
“book” ! The foolishness of the book is now
declared to be a droll travesty of Wagner’s
“Meistersinger.” Melbourne has never met
“ Meistersinger ” on the stage, neither can it
be persuaded that “The Serenade” amounts to
a laughable burlesque of anything. The spirit of
humor is not in the show. The construction is
clumsy, and during the second half of the 2nd
act, when the narrative is particularly painful,
the management omits to modify the situation by
turning the lights down. This is a deplorable
instance of neglected opportunity.
The Westminster Abbey Concert Party
is returning from Ballarat or Bendigo, to give
two more last final definite departing farewells at
Melbourne Town Hall, the previous final having
turned great masses of money away from the
doors. These extra consolation concerts are
fixed for next Saturday (Ist. August) afternoon
and evening.

The new “turn” at Melbourne Opera
House is a comedy sketch by Fred Hallen and
Miss Mollie Fuller, who made a first appearance
on Saturday. Their sketch is only a brief event,
and with nothing else more important than Dan
Crawley, the jigging songster, the bill at Rickards’
seems to be in a state of waiting for reinforce-
ments.
** * *
“As You Like It ” is winding up the
season gloriously at the. Adelaide Royal. The
public rushed the “I’ream ” up to the very last,
but is less enthusiastic about the new play, which
was very well done by H. H. Vincent and the
Sherlock Holmes co. a few months back. Cynthia
Brooke and Dorothy Marsden are found, with
regret, to be out of the cast. Denny’s Touchstone
is at least equal to his rendering of Bottom, and
Bucklaw is much approved as the Melancholy
One. Comic opera is announced to follow.
At the Tivoli, the Boissetts have come
in with their knockabout “Bricklayer” sketch,
and Fields has gone away to Sydney with
his uncanny billiard-table. Victor Kelly is
a humorous, if somewhat aimless, item in this co.,
and Ernest Fitts produces a grand-opera bass!
Also there is a lady of uncertain age who per-
sonates coons, and asks the lady conducting the
■orchestra to “Shake them up, lilly gal! ” On one
■eventful evening a singer failed to remember the
words of an item which was being sung “by
request”; whereupon someone in the audience
insisted on having his money’s worth, and climbed
■on the stage to take it out of the corner-men.
The curtain fell on a general scrimmage, and Ade-
laide is still wondering whether the whole thing
was a pre-arranged “extra turn.”

• • • •
That pathetic individual, N.S.W. Treasurer
Waddell, has made a proposition. He suggests that—his
State being at present a financial derelict andascoffed-at
wild cat on the London market- it should start a reform
by pledging itself not to borrow more than £1,500,000 a
year for the next ten years. The horribly inept Waddell
calls that refor’m ! It will take something very different
and much more energetic to save this distracted com-
munity. If Britain reformed the same way Britain would
borrow about £4",900,000 a year abroad in time of peace
and that wouldn’t be called reform ; it would be regarded
as the last thing possible in the way of disgrace and
bankruptcy. Treasurer Waddell, apparently, doesn’t
even begin to understand the nature of the situation.
The principal charge brought against this country is that
it has already built out of loans all the public works that,
considering the present size pf the population, have any
present chanceof paying their way; and that now it doesn’t
build works because they are really needed and will pay
their way, but hunts for something or anything in the
way of public jobs that will furnish an excuse for loans
in order to keep its population in wages. Other charges
are that it owes a darned lot too much already and
should pay something off the debt rather than add
£1,500,000 a year to it; and that after borrowing
£75,000,000 to “develop the magnificent natural re-
sources of the country ” these n sources should begin to
show' some capacity for standing by themselves. The
proposal, by way of a great reform, to only borrow
£1,500,000 a year for pauper relief is calculated to make
the foreign creditor sit up. When that ghastly extrava-
gance is called reform it must surely dawn on him that
he has been lending money to a very remarkable specimen
of humanity.

The paid-up capital is £85,000 ; on this and the reserves
at the commencement of the half-year (£62,979), the
profits were equal to £4 13s. lid. per cent, per annum,
as against £4 18s. sd. in the previous half, £5 os. Ild. in
the period ending June, 1902, and £4 ,19s. 9d. in that
ending December, 1901. The cash and liquid assets
(£72,530) represent 32 per cent, of the total liabilities to
the public (£226,700). Compared with the position
disclosed 12 months ago, there is no substantial change
anywhere; the slight reduction in profit is due to the
appearance of income tax among the items of expen-
diture. For that matter, indeed, there has been prac-
tically no change in the business during the past eight or
ten years ; deposits have been almost stationary, and the
somewhat larger business, with the shade higher average
profits, has been made possible by the steady, if not
rapid, accumulation of reserves.
••: r * *
“What,” asked a member in N.S.W. Assembly
the other night—“ what has been the shrinkage in the
cash deposits in the Savings Bank of N.S.W. and the
Post-Office Savings Bank for the six months ended June
30th as compared with the corresponding six months of
1902 ? ” And the Premier, after admitting that there had
been a falling-off, said in reply to the specific question—
“ It would be inadvisable to publish this information.”
So it is inadvisable for the State institutions, which hold
the shillings of the poor, to give (except once a year) the
information which is given every quarter, both as a
matter of course and as a matter of law, by the private
institutions which hold the pounds of the rich ! Surely
the people who 1, ,ve their savings in these public in-
stitutions have t! e right, as a matter of business, to as
much information as the depositors in the other banks
have ; and surely, as a matter of policy, a democratic
Government, which is supposed to trust the people, will
see that they get it. If it is a question of maintaining
confidence in the public banks, then the worst way to go
to woik is to say anything that may be taken as signify-
ing thao the truth is too awful to be revealed.

Agamemnon: As an investment, certainly not:

* • • •
The N.S.W. Government is so hard-up that it
does not want to spend anything at Broken Hill in con-
nection with the water famine; and so it is giving the
impression that the trouble is practically over. Yet the
fact is that the water supply-, trained up mostly from
South Australia, is so inadequate that. they can’t spare
enough to even start the street trams (steam-motors)
running again.

On the shareholders’ funds as at the commencement of
the half-year (capital £1,168,041 and reserves £280,612)
the banking profit was equal to 11s. 7d. per cent, per
annum. This compares with recent results as follows :
June, 1900.. 6s. 2d. June, 1902 .. ss. lOd.
Dec., 1900.. 7s. 7d. Dec., 1902 .. 9s. 9d.
June, 1901.. £1 19s. 6d. June, 1903 .. 11s. 7d.
Dec., 1901.. 10s. 4d.
On capital alone for the past half year the return was 14s.
5d., as against 11s. lOd. for the preceding half-year. The
reserves now consist of ordinary reserve fund of £52,000,
undivided profit, £30,356 ; and a special reserve accumu-
lated by the repurchase of “ B ” deposits at a discount,
£220,830. The actual existence of these or any reserves
is, however, problematical. The total assets are £7,450,116,
it is stated ; but of this amount £5,963,842 is represented
by “ bills discounted, advances, and all other debts due,
and other assets at old valuations.” This item has
appeared practically in that form since 1897. What the
“ old valuations ’’ are has never been stated. Remember-
ing, however, the A. J.S. Bank’s large interest in pastoral
property and the serious decline which has taken place
during the last few years in that class of property, it is to
be feared that, if the valuations were brought up to date,
a considerable discrepancy would appear. In other
words, probably the real explanation of the very low
earnings per cent, of shareholders’ funds is that the
shareholders’ funds (the capital and reserves intact) are
not actually there wherewith to earn any more. Anyhow,
the banking profits for the half-year are not quite 3 per
cent, per annum on even the reserves alone, taking no
account of capital at all.
In the circumstances, it is impossible to say
what progress is being made. ' Ordinary current banking
profits are certainly improving. For the half-year they
are a good deal better than for any other term since the
cleaning-up, with a single exception ; and that exception
was due to a favorable cattle season in Queensland.
What depreciation, if any, of the assets included at the
“ old valuations ” is going on cannot, however, be stated ;
and so the real progress, if any, which is being made
cannot be estimated. The best feature disclosed is the
fairly rapid reduction of the reconstructed deposits :

The reserves, all but £1205, have gone “ to make provi-

enough provision has yet been made cannot, however, be
told from the published accounts. As to the current
business, it is represented to have been rather more
profitable than usual. It began to look as though a rule
had been made that a profit should be shown of a pound
or two over the amount necessary to pay the 4 per cent,
dividend. Probably the heroic measure of wiping off the
reserves has allowed this policy to be changed, and it is
possible that the real current profit may be shown in the
future, and that it may be better than the amounts shown
latterly.

* * * *
Liquidator Templeton lias, after 13J years, got
very near the end of the disastrous Premier Permanent
Building Society (Melbourne). The total receipts have
been £l, 216,634, out of which the creditors have received
£1,008,431 ; and there is still a credit balance of £38,498,
out of which they will probably receive about £36,000.
Taking these last figures as accurate, the liquidation will
have cost £172,203 ; but this includes £62,754 spent on
paint and putty prior to the buildings being offered for
sale, as well as rates, taxes, and insurance. The actual
expenditure in commissions, charges, and costs
appears to have been £68,543, which is equal to
£5 12s. 6d. per cent, of total receipts. Assuming that
of the £38,498 now in hand the creditors will get £36,000,
and that the balance will be absorbed in liquidator’s
costs, then the latter alone (independent of agents’
commissions, law charges, etc.) will have amounted to
£2 13s. 6d. per cent, of total receipts. Considering the
tangle in which the society’s affairs had got this doesn’t
seem excessive. The results of the liquidation indicate
over again what a riotous life the society lived. Of 980
properties on hand when the liquidation began, only 402
were ultimately redeemed ; some of the other borrowers
hung on in a desperate struggle to pay interest until the
eighth year of the winding-up, and then had to give in.
The losses on this mortgage business totalled £453,856 ;
the society’s own speculations in freehold panned out
£259,096 to the bad ; making an aggregate loss of £693,321.
Of the total amount realised the unsecured creditors have
received only £94,146, in three poor little dividends
totalling 3s. 6d. in the £ ; what more they will get the
court has yet to say, but, only £36,000 being now
available, it will be little.

For a pastoral finance co. the S.A. firm has had a re-
markably good time ; it hasn’t had to “ pass” a dividend
since 1893, and half of the time it has been paying 9 per
cent., and earning more. On its paid-up capital
(£190,000) and reserves at the commencement of its
last year, its profits equalled almost 13 per cent.
Hitherto the co. has not had a formal reserve fund; it
has now, however, made a good start with £lO,OOO,
though it doesn’t say what it proposes to keep this
reserve in—whether in Government stocks or otherwise.
About the most striking feature of its recent history is
the steady growth of its banking business ; in June, 1899,
it reported “ Deposits and all other liabilities, £477,551,”
and from that point there has been a steady movement
until this year the item stands at £623,777. The liquid
assets, including money at short call and Government
stocks (the items are not separated), are equal to 32 per
cent, of these liabilities.
* * * *
Queensland is almost desperately hard-up. It
did not make ends meet by £431,939 in 1901-2, and in the
past 12 months its deficit was £191,341. It is so hard
pushed that it has had to strike a poll-tax on its people,
and cut its charity and education votes to the bone. But
at Mt. Morgan it has a gold mine, owned chiefly by people
who don’t live in Australia, out of which dividends
amounting to £150,000 were paid, last year—earned by
digging up and carting away over half-a-million of the
natural wealth of the country. Of course, on a predatory
concern of this kind, the hard-up Government made a
large call. It did, indeed. It demanded a whole 10s.
income-tax, and got it, too. A strong Government like
Philp’s won’t stand any fooling.

Not only was there a falling-off in the export business
(largely a war business) whose profits swelled the
figures for the previous year, but the co. has had
to pay drought-prices for the raw foodstuffs, with
the result that business could only be carried on
at a loss. Meantime, though the stock has been
reduced somewhat (from £110,212 to £94,386) book
debts have moved up from £20,388 to £29,799, and the
heavy total of unclaimed dividends has been reduced ; so
that the previous heavy liability on mortgage and
overdraft has gone up to £94,293—it was down to
£68,516 in 1899, and reached £84,809 in April, 1902. For
a co. with a paid-up capital of only £145,953, this is a
heavy weight to battle under ; the capital was probably
insufficient at the outset, and, with the company’s
expanding business, it has become obviously more and
more inadeouate since then. > *
• • • *
No wonder Great Britain wants preferential-
trade relations with a few markets. In 1872 it found a
foreign market for £35,996,000 of iron and steel; in 1900,
in spite of the increase of population capable of producing
iron and steel, the exports were worth only £31,992,000.
Yet that is the trade which has held its own better than
almost any other. The export of cotton yarn has fallen
from £16,697,000 in 1872 to £7,740,000 in 1900 ; of hard-
ware and cutlery, Sheffield’s stand-by, from £5,089,000 to
£2,139,000 ; of woollen manufactures, from £32,383,000 to
£15,682,000 ; of linen manufactures, from £8,225,000 to
£5,224,000; of silk manufactures, from £4,084,000 to
£2,062,000. A country that could be content with a
movement like that must be a very cow-like country
indeed.

London Times’ criticisms on Australia’s loan
habits should be seriously discounted by the Times’ own
habit of pushing its defunct dictionaries and other
literary merchandise on to Australians on time payments.
The time-payment dictionary purchaser is really borrow-
ing from the Times, and, everything weighed in, at far
heavier rates than he pays anywhere else.

The published accounts of this company are more or less
of a joke. An odd thousand or two bob up quite unex-
pectedly ; the company sold some land' long ago on
time-payment, and it has put the payments into a private
stocking until it reckons that enough has been made to
make it reasonably sure that the properties won’t come
back on its hands ; then it transfers something to the
balance-sheet, where the amount then appears as an
addition to a “ contingent account to provide for possible
loss on land sold on extended payments.” So far as the
balance-sheet shows, the bank has in cash and liquid
assets £37, while on deposit (with accrued interest) alone
it owes the public £13,318. The rest of its assets are
mostly “ balances due by land purchasers and borrowers.”
Presumably the bank has arranged so that its depositors
shall not call for their money in a hurry ; if it hasn’t, any*
thing might happen.

For August Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) has
announced a div. of 6s. (£90,000).
• • * •
Queen Margaret (W.A.) has now the cages
working in the shaft on its South Clippsland leases, and
this will enable it to run the mill full time. The average
of the last three crushings, taken from ore broken during
development, has been 37dvvt. per ton. It is expected
the mine will produce about 900 oz. monthly. > At present
work is proceeding at three levels, but the fourth will be
started immediately. It is over 18 months since this co.
paid a div.
*•• * *
Associated Northern ( W.A.) is pushing on with
the foundations of its treatment plant. All sorts of
rumors continue current as to the ore reserves ; the direc-
tors are again sending out William Griffiths to verify his
statements about the millions of sovereigns in sight.
Prof. Liveing and manager Boberts are as dumb as
oysters. The late Oapt. Bodda, unlike these two, was a
practical miner, and his right-hand man, Morgan, who
took all samples, and his assayer, who made all assays,
are still on the spot to verify or refute his statements.
The rich ore alleged to have been located by the diamond
drill at 912 ft. still refuses to be found, but the same thing
was experienced by Oapt. Parkes on the • adjoining
Oroya, the lode eventually being found 100 ft. east
of its supposed course. A diamond drill is working
on the Medindie Hill, next the Brown Hill, in
addition to the one boring on the Iron Monarch. No
reliable information can be gleaned as to the result* at
either of these drills. During its four years existence the
co. has treated 25,801 tons of ore for' 148,1960 z. of gold,
worth £592,780. Out of this £280,000 has been paid in
divs., and enough retained to pay for the new plant. In
addition, the original £lOO,OOO of working is still
available. Shipments of rich ores to smelters have been
resumed.

Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.), seems to
have fully surmounted its difficulties, and is gradually
improving its financial position. Costs of mining and
milling, which were 355. a year ago, are now reduced to
215., while total costs, including development and
upkeep, are only 325. 6d. a ton. As the ore treated is
worth £2 17s. 6d. a ton there is a net profit of £1 ss.
a ton, or £lO,OOO for the month. This compares with a
total net profit of only £38,100 for the whole of last year,
which shows a marked improvement. Beserves of ore
are estimated at about 200,000 tons, or two years’ supply,
worth 535. a ton, and have been increasing at the rate of
15,000 tons monthly. The shaft is down about 1600 ft.,
and eleven levels in all are being driven, most of the work':
being to the south. The mine was horriblymessed J ’'
before Loring’s time, and got to be known as the death
trap, owing to the numerous fatal accidents, which
occurred there.

The Pioneer Tin Mining Coy., of Bradshaw’s
Creek, Tasmania, which has had a most successful career,
as decided to increase its plant, and has placed an
order for a very large and powerful Suction Dredge and
appliances with the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, who built its original plant. The
new dredge will be fitted with a 15" Sand pump, and a
compound condensing vertical engine of 450 horse power,
and two boilers of the marine type, fora working pressure
of 1601bs. to the square inch. The pontoon to carry this
plant will be 60ft. x 50ft. by a depth of sft. The plant will
be capable of raising 100 cubic yards of tin drift per hour
to a height of 80 feet into sluice boxes. The new plant is
very similar to the older one but contains improvements
in detail suggested by the two years experience of the
older plant. The latter has done excellent and con-
tinuous service since its first start. The average cost of
raising tin drift to a height of 70ft. during the period
above mentioned, including cost of all repairs and
renewals, has been less than 3d. per cubic yard.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOJin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

Ida H., Laverton (W.A.), continues to do fairly
well, and has just declared a further dividend of 10 per
cent., the same amount having been paid in April on
account of 1903 earnings. The issued capital of the co.
has been 54,000 in £1 shares; but the shares have now
been split into 216,000 scrip of ss. each, in addition to
which 24,000 are held in reserve. Three levels have been
hitherto worked, but the fourth has now- been started,
and it is in contemplation to double the mill and the
returns if the new level warrants it. The reef was very,
consistent in gold contents cn the surface, but pinched at
both ends, making it impossible to materially increase
ore reserves, owing to the. shortness of the chute opened
up. Shares are almost exclusively in the hands of :
“Boulder King” Waddington, who states that he has
split them to allow investors of limited means to acquire
an interest in a div.-paying mine ! The Laverton district,
where the mine is situated, has so far earned a very un-
enviable notoriety on account of the unreliability of its
reefs.

At Ingham (N.Q.) recently, a “gentle”
Kanaka was sentenced to three months for
assault on a girl 16 years of age. He admitted
the crime, by the way. How tenderly and
mercifully Mongrelian justice handles its colored
brethren ! If common white trash does anything
of the kind, he is sentenced to five or ten years,
including solitary confinement. But “kind, gen-
tle, soft-hearted” Tommy Tanna receives three
months 1
NEARLY FATAL.
1ST. MAN : “Did you ever meet with an accident?”
2ND. MAN : “ Yes! When I met my wife"
Some members of a Maoriland lodge, who
intended travelling by train to the funeral of a
deceased brother, were shocked to find that the
body had been stowed away in the milk van, and
were more shocked on being told that it was the
“ usual thing” to dump corpses in with either the
milk or the meat.
‘D. : The R.C. clergy are before all
others at “ raising the wind;” The habit of read*
mg the quarterly collection-list
from the altar (after giving
notice the Sunday previous
so that the last straggler
may rush in with his
money) draws the guineas
like a ten - horse - power
magnet. When I was a
boy, M‘Guanne, the pub-
lican, headed the list for
years with ten guineas a
quarter. Then Casey,
who kept the other
pub., tied him, and
they were read out
quarter after quarter
“Michael M‘-
Guanne, ten guineas; Ter-
ence Casey, ten guineas,”
M'Guanne always keeping
senior place. Then bad
times struck Michael, and
one Sunday Casey had
the pleasure of hear-
ing himself thunder
out five guineas in
the lead of anybody
else, with “Michael
M‘Guan n e, a
guinea.” He looked
round at Michael’s
“sitting,’' but the
M'Guanne family
was absent; the dis-
grace was too much.
His reverence has a
peculiar way of in-
flecting a guinea sub.
which he thinks might
well have been two, and
the soft hint is often
taken. Last time I was
there M'Guanne was coming
strong again, but a heretic told
me he only gave a guinea but
was “called” for five, so as to
keep Casey up to the ten, Casey
having mentioned that the drought
might compel him to reduce his sub.
to five.
In order to kill time while time is getting
in its own deadly work, society must have some-
thing to do, so it spends its leisure in invent-
ing some new form of insanity with which to
startle its other self. The latest idea in Melbourne
was to cause the maids waiting at table to wear
belltoppers. The head-gear was cocked over the
left eye in a rakish sort of way, and created quite
a mild sensation at a recent big dinner function.
The next thing will be a maid reaching under your
chin in ballet costume, or handing round the
humble “spud’ in bathing dress—the waitress
not the “spud.”

They bring the gallows and other appur-
tenances of Justice to your door in New South
Wales. Judge Gibson and Crown Prosecutor
Bevan lately travelled from Sydney via Mel-
bourne and Adelaide to Wentworth (N.S.W.).
On opening the Quarter Sessions there was one
case for trial, the jury acquitted the accused, and
next morning the Judge and C.P. departed down
river per steamer to Adelaide on the return to
Sydney. That was a costly prisoner.
The Cold-tea apostles have been
enjoying themselves in the S. American
republic of Peru. The fun started
when they led President Romana
up to the penitent form, and
caused him to see the error of
his ways, and got him to
solemnly sign the pledge. In
his new - born
zeal he got his
party to pass
prohibition laws,
to come into
effect on April 1.
The distillers, how-
ever, prepared a
coup ; they offered
the army free drinks
for ever, and the army
struck a bargain on
the spot, and promptly
got drunk. Then, just
when the army was get-
ting sober, and while it
was looking for some-
thing wet to tie round
its head, the President
put in a higher bid—he
offered to raise the army’s
screw ; and the army
ratted back again. Then
the Government seized the distilleries, and
destroyed the stills, and when the last mail left it
was putting the premises up to auction.

The off-hand young Australian again.
Alleged, in a down-south Court, that a young
man of 22 or so, who had been roped into a secret
marriage by a pining maid of 45 or thereabouts,
thought that, as he hadn’t lived with the latter,
there wasn’t any harm in taking on another—this
time something less seasoned—aged about 17.
He is quite surprised at the prosecution for
bigamy which has followed.

The mysterious French derelict who has
confessed to sending five bulletsthrough an ancient
recluse, near Dandenong (Vic.), in the hope of
plunder (his actual loot amounted to a pair of old
boots), has only his own folly to thank for his
arrest. Not even a rough description of the sus-
pected murderer could be got —he was merely
supposed to be a casual loafer wandering around.
After shooting the old man this extraordinary
ruffian got away as far as Gembrook, 30 miles
distant; then turned and meandered right
over the scene of his crime, where his peculiar
antics and his obtrusive desire to avoid
observation drew attention to him. The boots
and the black trackers did the rest.
Another health scandal in Sydney : Of
305 dwellings inspected by the Central Board of
Health in a Redfern area, 177 were unfit for
human habitation. There is not the slightest
hope of keeping the metropolitan area decently
clean until a Greater Sydney Government sweeps
away the dirty little suburban councils. The
average suburban council consists chiefly of the
local property owners and their agents, who sacri-
fice time and money to secure seats on an unpaid
body, soithat they can promote their own interests
as property-owners: and those interests they
often consider best served by giving themselves
full permission to save the cost of cleaning and
sewering and repairing their properties. The
sanitary inspection by the paid servant of such
people is in one respect a farce ; in another it is
a tragedy.
The Melbourne dailies spread themselves
at some length over the case of Miss Layton —the
St. Kilda “ hermit ” whom the local council
adjudged insanitary and subsequently removed to
Kew Asylum. In reality it was merely a sor-
did tale of a would-be-respectable street. The
poor old lady, who, a few years back, lost father,
mother and brother, had no friends in the country,
so brooded over her sorrows. Her home became
dilapidated, the fences disappeared, and a rank
!rowth of weeds and grass covered the garden.
'his deteriorated the value of adjacent property,
so certain aldermen decided that she was insane
and she was sent to the madhouse. It took Mel-
bourne four or five days to realise what it had
done, and then there were demands for her release.
It is notable that while the press grew very tear-
ful about the old lady’s grief for her lost relatives,
it didn’t dwell at all on the fact that her alleged
madness might be partly owing to the fact that
she lost most of her money in a highly respec-
table gentleman’s bank. The old lady’s house,
declared to be “insanitary,” was really very clean,
though ramshackle enough, and certainly it was
curious that St. Kilda council, which keeps a
most objectionable tip in a public park, should
rush anybody into a lunatic asylum because her
house hadn’t been painted recently. r
•»* * i
Committee of Melbourne Benevolent
Asylum has done a little to discourage the un-
common practice of bequeathing money to charit-
able institutions. Kingston, a former member of
the committee, died in England some months
back, and by his will devoted £27,000 —nearly the
whole of his property —towards Melbourne Bene-
volent Asylum. Also beset apart a sum of £5O
for fixing himself up comfortably in a London
cemetery after his decease. It happened that
James Kingston died in Devonshire, and the £5O
was swallowed by the expenses of conveying his
body to London and burying it in the family
grave. Previous inmates of the grave were
accorded tablets on the family tombstone. The
English legal managerof James Kingston’s funeral
thought that the deceased owner aud final occu-
pant of the grave should also have a tablet. He
wrote to the committeemen of the Benevolent,
asking them to vote £2O for Kingston’s tombstone.
The committee replied stating how sorry it was
that the by-laws prohibited it from allotting the
1350th part of Kingston’s bequest to the purpose
suggested. If a bequest of £27,000 doesn’t earn a
common £2O courtesy for the testator’s ghost, is it
likely that good old Kingston’s example will
tempt the average vainglorious capitalist to die
and do likewise ? All he wants is the whoop and
glory of the business. If there is neither whoop
nor glory what inducement is there? ,

The report of King Ned’s visit to Ireland :
As the Royal cortege passed along a woman,'in a fit of
enthusiasm, broke through the military cordon, and,
rushing to the side of the carriage, clasped Queen
Alexandra’s hand. Amid ringing cheers from the excited
multitude, her Majesty restrained a policeman from
arresting the enthusiast.
Good old stage-management! But why the
absence of a trained dove fluttering down with the
shamrock of suavity in his beak, and of an edu-
cated pig bowing low as he presented the potato of
prosperity? And what’s the matter with the
strange but faithful dog which ought to insist on
following the royal carriage?
THE PLACE OF BEST.
OLD LADY : “ Poor man! Is it talcin' him to

FIRST BEARER: “O no! He's been given up by

Bent acquired one brilliant idea while he
was wandering round the Mallee, what time he
was being rushed out of his Railways’ billet before
he could get back to object. When he saw Lake
Hattah (a woebegone swamp at the back of
beyond) he told a deputation that “he came to
the conclusion that it was an ideal site for a Con-
sumptive Hospital.” The idea has its points,
however; three or four beds would be enough,
and the railway returns on that line would go up
with a jump. Single fare up and corpse fare
down—and corpse rates are high.
PILGRIMS TO JAPAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Pars, in any issue of any paper.)
“A complimentary banquet was tendered to Alderman
Bosh last night, on the eve of his departure for a six
months’ holiday in Japan. Alderman Bosh will not be
accompanied by Mrs. Bosh.”
“.Councillor M'Smith returned from Japan by the s.s.
Yoshiwari yesterday, and was subsequently welcomed at
the town hall by the mayor and his brother-councillors.
In a short speech Councillor M’Smith said that he had
derived great pleasure and benefit from his trip, and
practically he felt like anew man.”
Let us sing a ballad Jappy of the brown land fair and
happy,
Where the Geisha twines a laurel round the pilgrim’s
aching brows!
Where our Judges go when weary of this common land
and beery,
And our aldermen in stacks beside the Inland Ocean
drowse.
Once the Saxon went a-grailing, but at present he goes
sailing
To the damsels of Tokio, to be soothed with palm and
fan ;
And he leaves behind the missus, and he stroketh tawny
tresses
When he joins the rising tide of pious pilgrims to Japan.
For their pilgrimage is pleasant, and the way shines
phosphorescent
To the weary ones that travel to that brown and happy
land ;
The chrysanthemums are waiting, and the blooms are
coruscating,
And the brownish damsel gently leads the pilgrim by the
hand.
And his bald head shineth brightly in the haunts of
pleasure nightly,
And his long ears flap with pleasure as he sits on the
divan.
O ! the track is much frequented; and few pilgrims are
contented,
Till they return again to worship at the shrines of old
Japan.
GRANT HERVET.
THE WINE OF LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Give me the wine of Life—red wine—
With a brilliant sparkle gleaming.
I am tired of tame and tasteless joys
And the lazy life of dreaming.
Give me the passion, the fever glow,
The strength of a fervid loving ;
I am sick of a luke-warm, listless thing
That is hardly worth the proving.
'Stead of the sleepy lullabies
Give me Life’s music’s marches,
Only to feel alive, ALIVE,
Under the sky’s blue arches.
Down in the heart of the city’s heart,
’Mid living lives of men.
Give me the wine of Life—red wine
To drink to the dregs, and then !
vio,
KATHLEEN WALKER.

The Melbourne butcher-boy who stuffed
liis girl’s baby into a ferret-cage, and then dropped
it into a creek, and the other butcher-boy who
helped him, have been sentenced to death ; but,
the jury having recommended the lads to mercy
on account of their age, Justice a’Beckett merely
passed sentence, and left out the millinery and
mummery generally associated with the tragic
business. The most remarkable feature in the
whole of this sordid patch of life is the cheerful
indifference of the butcher-boys. They never saw
anybody worry over sheep and calves, which were
worth something, when they were killed, and
they seem surprised in a brutish sort of way that
there should be all this fuss over a wholly unsale-
able infant that was only in the way, and didn’t
seem to be worth anything at all.
* » * •
The emoanelling a jury to try the North-
cote (Melbj baby-murder case was a tedious pro-
cess. Old men were challenged on behalf of the
two young fellows who “put baby away,” whilst
the Crown challenged from an exactly opposite
point of view. Finally, a very young jury was
arrived at, but the prisoners might have got just
as kind a verdict—“ Guilty, with strong recom-
mendation to mercy on account of their youth ”
from 12 of the oldest of the rejected ones. On the
other hand, the Crown might have got an unquali-
fied hanging verdict from a dozen of the challenged
youngsters. As a matter of fact, there were one
or two notoriously humane veterans among the
“throw-outs.” Old men are apt to think of them-
selves as having been hard-hearted brutes 40 years
ago, and young men are disposed to feel unreason-
ably horrified at the careless brutality of their
contemporaries. An ancient, softened by family
troubles, sees in his own son a potential murderer,
but the son sees himself a much softer-hearted
chap than he is. Present scribe, speaking from
middle-aged experience, would advise all mur-
derers on trial to chance their luck with a jury of
sorrowful-looking greybeards.
* * * *
“ Adelaide ” : British law has been turning
its usual somersaults. A month ago a professional
lady in Adelaide was acquitted because her clever
young counsel found a recent English case which
made “ dying depositions ” of no use unless they
were taken down verbatim, question with answer,
to show whether there was any prompting. Now
the mail brings news that that decision has still
more recently been contemptuously handled and
upset! The lady, having been found “Nob
Guilty ” on the legal point, can dwell in peace till
next time, but the point that secured her release
was taken just in time.

This is the compressed diary of a man
from the Mallee who struck Melbourne a little
over a week ago. On Wednesday morning he
was drunk and addressing the public on a
device for diverting the water from the
gutter in Elizabeth-street into the North-West
country. Same day about noon he was drunker
and was run in. Late in the afternoon he was
comparatively sober and was let out on bail.
Same evening he was horribly and wonderfully
drunk and was run in again. In the court next
morning he met a drunken friend, and was so
overjoyed that, in the fullness of his heart, he paid
his own fine, his friend’s fine, and the fine of
a young man who was also in trouble. The three
departed to get drunk, when the young man
picked the friend’s pocket of all his cash and
belongings and fled. The friend went to look for
a detective while the man from the Mallee got
drunk on hi 3 solitary own. Later that day he
held forth to a considerable crowd in Bourke-
street on irrigation. In the afternoon he was at
Melbourne hospital being treated for bruises
received through falling down while lecturing on
irrigation. In the evening he was sitting in a
gutter buzzing like a large bee, and was run in.
At the police-station he insisted on regarding the
place as a public-house and said that his drink
was whisky. Next morning he was fined again
and urged to retire to his native fastnesses, as he
was evidently unsuited to the social atmosphere
of a large city. *•'

“R.A.K.”: Saw in South Melbourne,
the other day, a well-fed spinster-looking person
holding forth earnestly to a down-at-heel family
(father, mother and kid). She had a finger signi-
ficantly planted in the open page of a pocket
Bible. As I passed I heard : “And you’ll never
have any disease, and your bread will be sure. ”
The father looked especially hungry and woe-
begone, and gazed up and down the street as he
listened, as if he half expected to see a ticket to
S’Africa floating past on the breeze or dropped
from a tram. Put the spinster with the Bible
down for a Dowieite.
A subtle joke was played off on the unsus-
pecting spectators at the military demonstration
on Yarra bank the other night. The most inter-
esting business of the evening was an explosion of
submarine mines under the polluted river. Ihe
first was a small ladylike charge; it threw up
a delicate column of opalescent water which fell
gracefully to the depths again and behaved itself
admirably all through. Ihe crowd was encour-
aged, and pressed forward to the very edge of the
stream to view the next explosion. But an extra
large charge had been put in by the mill ary
humorists, and a regular waterspout arose,
carrying with it mud, dead cats, rags, bones and
old fellmongery, and all these fell like a,deluge
upon the onlookers. A hundred P e p Pie least
were drenched to the skin on a bitterly cold
night, and it wasn’t safe, to ask them whether
they had enjoyed themselves, as they sat shivering
W their homeward bound trams witl the Yarra
streaming off their ruinedlk*»,and [costumes A
few more episodes like that and the military and
navai forces will 1» cut dead at the Mayorai hop,

When James Horn was recently brought
up for trial a second time, at Perth (W.A.), on a
charge of criminally assaulting his
he put in a plea, that, having been once tried and
acquitted on a charge, he could not be retried on
the same charge. A jury was empanelled to
decide the plea. Before the jurymen retired,
however, the presiding judge said that, before
they decided whether or not accused had been
acquitted, it was his bounden duty to tell them
that he had been so acquitted ! After retirement,
they tried to dodge between the two halves of
this legal split liair by bringing in a verdict that
accused should be retried; _ but they were
informed by the bench that their opinion wasn’t
wanted on the matter; they were to say whether
or not the man who was acquitted was acquitted,
So they brought in a verdict that the man whom
they knew’was acquitted wasn’t acquitted, and
then business was able to resume.
A Christchurch (M.L.) court has decided
that swearing is justifiable under certain circum-
stances. There had been a bike collision, and
the man run into had sat up in the road and
told the other he might go to a place where the
temperature is considerable. He was sued, and
his counsel explained that cuss words
Were not illegal save by an Act of George 11. which was
not in force in the State ; that the Act provided only
nominal penalties rising from Is. for a common seaman to
ss. for a gentleman, and that, as regards his client’s
remark that the plaintiff could depart into Hades, this
was neither promise, entreaty, demand nor threat, but
merely an unsolicited permission which recipient could
act upon or otherwise at his own discretion.
The Bench cut the matter short by saying it would
probably have sworn itself under like circum-
stances. Case dismissed.

Sydney Sabbath Tipster has started to re-
echo Spruce Myth’s doctrine that it is utterly
wrong to tax the hard-working man who brings
up his family decently, in order to support the
children of the useless loafer who will not support
them properly himself. The Shellback paper
complains that the Sobraon boys are better looked
after than the children of many workers, and yet
hasn’t the courage to advocate that the Sobraon
boys be slaughtered in order to save expense.
The Sabbath organ is always weeping about this
country’s need of population, yet it objects to the
Government taking steps to make the best of the
population that is running to waste, and bringing
it up in a way that will eventually prove remunera-
tive to the State. The country must either pay
to educate its lost children, or pay to bury them,
or pay to gaol them in later life. Which does
the Sunday horror recommend ?
* * * *
lb’s an ill wind that blows no one any good.
In Wellington (M.L.) the wind is so reliable that
house cleaning is quite simple. You securely
moor anything you wish to keep, and then open
the windows and doors and the rest blows away.
Even a carpet or a bloodstain will blow away if
not chained down.
A SPORTING OFFER.
SPORTING CUSTOMER: “ Pound of cheese, please.”
GROCER: “ Yes, sir; Gorgonzola or Cheddar?”
S.C.: “ Oh, I don’t care’, start ’em both across
the counter and Til take the winner.”
There is something refreshing about the
resolve of the committee called together to distri-
bute the fund subscribed by the public for blackleg
drivers, firemen and others, that the recipients
are to have medals and chain pendants of uniform
design to remind them of their services, and to
let the public know at whom to throw clods. The
melting value of the trinkets is low, and no man
wants to buy one and pretend he is a blackleg,
and if the owner wears the thing himself he has
to drink his beer all alone, a shunned and solitary
man.
• • * •
When a prominent man like Leo XIII.
dies, every axe-grinder, political or otherwise,
speeds to paste his advt. on the coffin. The Aus-
tbalian Stab announces John See’s sorrow at the
Pope’s death —it is its business to keep See before
the public. The Telegbaph explains that Car-
ruthers can’t enjoy his food for grief over the
Pope’s death. Humbler papers explain that
Alderman Wobbles, who is standing for re-elec-
tion, is torn by conflicting emotions. Even a
butchers’ organ comes out at such times with the
news that prominent meat salesmen are all broke
up. As for the person who died, he is practically
lost in the scuffle, and his bones are rushed from
pillar to post and trodden under foot in the
advertising scrum, till he might almost as well be
a football.

Rookwood’s happy home for indigent old
men has been under suspicion for a considerable
time, but a few nights ago, in N.S.W. Leg.
Assembly, Andy Kelly produced a lurid verbal
photo.;
The scene at meal times was deplorable. As soon as
the bell rang the men, numbering 400, rushed their living
room like a crowd of madmen, and the weakest were
often knocked down and trampled upon in the wild
scramble, sometimes receiving serious injury.
Assuming this to be true, why is the ungodly
rush 1 Are there not enough victuals, and do
Nos. 398, 399, and 400 go short ? Or are there not
enough seats, and do those who are left have to
wait and dine later upon cold victuals’ Or is
it simply that the dinner rush is the only break
in the deadly, dull, grey monotone of existence—
that it is the one thing that lets the old men know
they aren’t dead ?
Love—The storm. Courtship—The ship-
wreck. Marriage—Taking to the boats. Divorce
—The rescue.
“ Jaf.” . Wonderful the number of land-
scape gardeners running wild in this
country. Fenced in a garden a couple
of months ago and put on an “ expert ”
to lay it out. His method was to make
various little mounds of earth all over
the place and put a seed in each.
Fired him. Another came along.
Had been a sailor. Cut the garden
up into hearts, anchors, etc. —at
least he said they were those
devices. Wasn’t taking any stock
in emblems. A third tramped
into my plot—had been for
“siven years in the satanical
gardins in Shydney.” Showed
him what I wished done. Re-
turned at night to find every
bottle within half-a-mile of
that garden stuck on end
round the beds. I never
knew until then what hard
boozers my neighbors were!
Paid him off on the spot. Land-
scape gardeners are still coming
—and going !

The other day a man got drunk
at Addington (M.L.), and started to
walk along a railway line. If he
had been sober, and remembered that
the Maoriland railways are the dead-
liest in the world, he would not have
done so. Anyway, giving evidence at
the inquest, an engine-cleaner said that,
on the arrival of the train that coincided
with the inebriate, he had noticed blood
on the cowcatcher, but he merely thought
that he had cut his hand. He didn’t exy
amine his hand to see if it had been {cut,
and, in any case, he said he often had to
clean gore and brains and hides off the
cowcatcher. It seems it is so customary for
the Maoriland engines to run over stieep or the
public that the cleaners only get alarmed
when there isn't any blood. During the last
six weeks the cleaners have had to remove off
the cowcatoher the remains of three shunters, one
stationmaster, and four drunks. •'[' '

“P.X.” : Be recent paragraph referring
to fortune-tellers’ popular trick of promising
married women much happiness with a second
husband after the living one is laid to rest.
Remember one will in which the deceased pro-
vided that on the re-marriage of his relict the
money left in trust for her use and benefit was to
be distributed in charity—“ as my wife is certain
of happiness in a second marriage, several clair-
voyants having assured her to that effect, and
consequently will not require any of my money.”
It turned out that, after a domestic row, the
wife had blurted out to the husband what ; the
fortune-tellers had told her—just by way of bring-
ing him to his bearings. She doesn’t consult
fortune-tellers now, and is still unmarried.

Some while back a Bulletin correspond-
ent mooted the idea of giving prizes for each child
born. Berlin has formed a society on this basis.
A prize, varying from £4O to £l2O, goes to each
member when the stork nests on the roof-tree.
Why not an Australian Minister for Propagation
or Department of Philoprogenitiveness ?
ACROSS THE BAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Across the bar
Sweet, smiling’ Kitty stands.
Her shapely wrists encased in golden bands,
While jewels glitter on her dimpled hands
Bright as a star.
It you should ask, “ Whence all this wealth I see?”
“ I have some uncles who are good to me ! ”
She straightway pouts, a trifle saucily,
Across the bar.
Across the bar
Sweet Kitty lightly skips
From bench to bar—daily a thousand trips—
Bringing refreshers to dry, thirsty lips.
Mine is Three Star.
All the chaps like her ; some are badly hit—
They raise the glass and say, “ Here’s to you, Kit,"
That she may throw a glad smile into it
Across the bar.
Across the bar
Stood ever maid so ran?
She has relations, unoles, I will swear
Who never kinsfolk of her parents were
Near kin or far.
Were ever uncles such as these before !
No doting parent could be generous more.
They hand her costly presents by the score
Across the bar.
Across the bar
Our hearts play telephone,
The rhythm of her heart vibrates my own.
It says, “ Old Jack, for you, and you alone,
My kisses are."
Secure in this first hand assurance then,
I feel no pangs of jealous anger when
My Kitty flirts for hours with other men
Across the bar
N.S.W.
J.P.O.
TOOTH OUT!—TOO THIN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He did not know if the jury ever had the miserable
experience of going to the dentist’s, but if they had they
would have felt somewhat astonished to find a young
apprentice, between 18 and 21 years of age, ready to
operate on their teeth.— Just ice Owen (N.S.W.) in a
recent summing-up.
When the infant who’s apprenticed
For a period to a dentist
Gives you greeting and inserts you in the torture-
chamber chair,
It’s a lump of consolation
When he lisps in explanation
That he’s just a sort of office-boy who happens to be
there!
When, with confidence precocious.
He puts on a “ holt ” ferocious,
And breaks a solid molar that is aching in your gum,
And he babbles, “ I’ve forgotten
Which the one you said was rotten,”
You forgive him—he’s but learning, and you’re very glad
you’ve come !
H.C.C.

Last week I took to my bed
With a cough, and a cold in my head,
My friends were all sighing, as they thought I
was dying,
But I banished all fears when I said :
“ Bad cold I may have for sure,
But to die—that I will not endure ;
Just send for the stuff that will cure any cough
Some Woods’ Giieat Peppermint Cure.”*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ Sydney, July 28,1903.
Mr Dear Arini,—
Isn’t the Royal Princess asserting herself ! Isn’t
she calling her soul her own, and her person and
actions also, and isn’t she taking all three properties
to furrin ’ parts when her husband or father-in-
law (who is generally named Karl, or Wilhelm, or
Ludwig) hedges her round with forty times more
thou shalt nots ” than there are in the ten com-
mandments, and puts inside those restrictions an
equal number of equally disagreeable “thou
shalts,’ and when, to top the horror, there is his
own German countenance, which she probably
didn’t want to marry, only Papa Franz and
Mama Luise and brother Gottlieb assured her it
would grow on her by-and-bye ?
Cable tells us that the Grand-Duchess of Saxe-
Weimar has run away to Switzerland to escape the
tyranny of her surroundings. What a bad time a
big section of female royalties must have had in
times—not very old times, either—gone by! Women
are not as religious as they used to be, hence their
revolt. So long as Princess Schwartzbrod truly
believed, that God’s eye was on her whilst she was
being grilled, and the more patiently she bore it
the more He loved her, the “tyranny of her
surroundings ” was meekly endured. Other
causes also operated. There was the castle-prison
for the unsubmissive she. Late Prince Albert’s
grandfather kept his Consort cooped up for 30
years. The ancestral endurance of close confine-
ment thus established came in handy for the
Prince Consort later, for Victoria keDt him very
strictly to quarters.
The old plan of insisting on 'their women-folk
being extra religious worked very well indeed—for
the men. “I am proud to say lam an atheist,”
remarked a Spanish grandee to an English
traveller. “ And your wife, does she share your
views ?” “ Great heavens, no,” said the horrified
grandee; “my wife is a respectable woman and
goes to church daily.” But nowadays women
show an inclination to refuse the narcotic of reli-
gionism ; and husbands feel the difference.
A vignette of the girl player or singer’s pro-
fessional debut: A minor hall in the city, a
nicely palm-potted platform with a grand piano
placed slantwise; ushers and ticket-takers who
look like (perhaps are) third year Univer-
sity men; excited relatives and friends of
the debutante making alarms and excur-
sions. At twenty minutes before eight the
audience begins to arrive. Seventy per cent,
of it is young and fresh and feminine, and is
accompanied by its mother. The mother looks as
if Ethel had dragged her away from the washing-
up, rushed her into a black evening dress,
brushed the top of her screwed-up hair, thrown a
woollen shawl on her head, and raced her off to
the city-bound trolly. Ethel herself carries no
suggestion of kitchen or hurried dressing into the
hall. Her Eiffel head carries a big bow, or a
wreath of baby roses; her shoulders and neck
shine under the lamps; she wears a string of
imitation pearls, and a white, pink, or pale-blue
frock, a,nd she sits down near Edith
and begins to comment on the programme,
until her mother discovers that Edith’s mother
is further along the seat and there is a shuffle of
the maternals. Most of the audience personally
know the debutante or know of her, and are kindly
disposed, but its gossip about her, her family, and
her prospects in the profession is jagged because
it is always turning its head suddenly over its
shoulder in expectation of the vice-regal people.
At eight o’clock some critics come in, and, in
spite of the ushers’ entreaties to go up top, slink
into the very hindmost seats. At one minute
past eight enter the graciously-smiling State
Governoress, with daughter. Alice, who glances
round for familiar faces, and an aide who
carries his hands behind him (for fear the
flurried ushers will shake one) and on his face an
I-am-glad-I-carne expression which he assumed
on the footpath. The presentation posies are
accepted and the programme starts. If the
debutante is a player, she leads off with the crash-
bang ; if she is a singer, her white frock, pearls
and voice don’t come on till later. In either
case, the people in the street slacken their pace
as they pass the hall and wonder if it’s a vivi-
section chamber.
Death of Mrs. Lyne, wife of the Home Sec.,
has caused a genuine feeling of sorrow even
amongst people who knew the lady only by
report. The report was always of such a high
character. Mrs. Lyne was of a charitable and
unaffected nature. After her husband was
beti tied a damsel from a daily paper called upon her
for a portrait and an interview. “Stay to lunch,”
said the Home Sec.’s wife, “and never mind
the portrait or the monograph. The last wouldn’t
interest anyone, and as for the first—well, I’ve
only had one, and that was taken some 25 years
ago—a ‘tinograph ’ I think, at a picnic.” Mrs.
Lyne succumbed to pneumonia following on
influenza. Her family consisted of three daugh-
ters and one son.
Any unmarried who wishes for a matrimonial
mate has only to take a fairly protracted sea
voyage in order to become Mrs. at the end of it.
The husband seems a kind of marine product,
so often is he to be found at sea. Most
Sydney-siders will recollect a classic-faced
beauty whose lusciousness of late years
threatened to perish in the overblowing. She left
for a trip to S’Africa some months ago, and now
report says that she is fiancee. Met him on the
voyage. Aud there’s—anyone of you can easily
recollect half-a-dozen acquaintances who went to
sea and had a catch.
Aucklander Tom Manders Jackson is, I see,
about to return to Maoriland, and take up the
auctioneering business which he left some ten.
years ago for the lyric stage. He has been some
eight years in London, and always seemed to have
good engagements. He certainly has, or had, a.
beautiful voice. So perhaps climatio reasons
Ada Crossleyis generous with her star voice! She
sang six songs at inaugural reception of recently-
founded Austral Club. Many of her old Mel-
bourne friends are in some way interested in the
bantling. The Browne sisters —Hirst and Myra—
and Nellie Veitch were to the fore, and Mrs.
Alfred Ibbotson. Most of the other clubites are
the Countess This or My Lady That.
Didn’t somebody say that all mankind loves
a lover? Suppose that’s why a section of the
’kind is constantly manufacturing engagements.
It wants to gloat over the “ lovers ” who
probably have only a how-d’ye-doing acquaint*
ance. However, five warship men are truly 1
engaged to Sydney girls, and there is more thao
a rumor of an engagement between two Sydney
musicians. He’s a widower—she’s a widow. And
both are very good looking.
Freemasons put up a record for brilliancy and
attendance at their last annual ball. The State
Ex., by the way, promptly sent along his annual
sub. of five guineas. His annual fivers to various
charities are fairly numerous. Both Governors.
(General and Particular) were present, and
amongst the guests brought by the Governor-
General was Mrs. Harmer, wife of the Bishop of
Adelaide. A real lilac spray is Mrs. Harmer,
very pretty and fair. Bishops usually have such
square wives with a look of solid sense below
their plainly-brushed hair. Brother A. J. Bcott
hon. sec’d the ball, and he must have worn &
smile like an earthquake fissure when (with the
packed galleries) he beheld a £4OO or so house.
Proceeds go to the Freemasons’ Benevolent Insti-
tution. Distressed or infirm Freemasons, their
wives or widows, have small pensions from the
institution, which is only one of the F.’s several
charity organisations, and all have about them
the unusual point that, outside a small commit-
tee, none know to whom the charity is dispensed.
When Mrs. Fitzclarence, of Darling Point, gets
cards for a free musical show, she generally gives
them to her housemaid, who takes the coachman.
If the housemaid has a prior engagement she
gives the cards to brother Bill’s landlady—and of
such is the audience of a free musical show in
Sydney. But the crowd that filled Centenary
Hall at invitation of the Henry M. Birge Pianola
Co. to listen to the first performance in Sydney of
Lisa Lehmann’s “Persian Garden” last Thursday
was an exception to the rule. The Fitzclarences
came in force, and the audience’s names, if printed,
would be a yard of the Government House list.
By the way it buried its face in the programme
(all the verses of the song cycle were printed) old
Omar must have been for it a new writer. Com-
poser L.L. has got considerable blood and wine
and fire and roses and nightingales into the music
that breathes over that eastern garden. Misses
Violet Mount and Maud Dalrymple, and Messrs.
Robyns and Weir did the Gardening with
Persian languorousness, and Mr Adams operated
the Pianola which played the pianoforte accom-
paniments. “In a Persian Garden” will be
repeated to-night (Thursday).
Death of Mr. Quong Tart, the merchant from
Loch China, will be a sad loss, not only to his own
young family of six children, but to the charity
organisations of Sydney. He was a cheerful
giver. Mrs. Quong Tart, who was a bookkeeper
in one of the big establishments he owned in
Sydney some years ago, is an Australian, and is
still a very fine looking woman.
The new Her Majesty’s Theatre leaves a color
memory of sapphire velvet, old gold, dull silver,
and various tints of soft greens and greys. At
the auction of seats for opening night, “ Colonel ”
J. P. Smith, a.n American oil spring owner, who
has been living in Sydney for some time,
bought the first four put up for £l2 each,
and handed up the money in notes. This gentle-
man is he who takes a seat for his hat at a theatre
and a passage for it on our railways. A free-born
’Murkan’s hat is worth first-class accommodation
anywhere.
The moaning of the tied was to be heard at
N.S.W. Kennel Club show, last week—said to be
the best of the club’s shows up to date. There
was the usual display of caninolatry—men
and women who stood near the pets all day
and brushed them every few minutes. Women
exhibitors must make the committee very mad
sometimes. One lady who was showing a pair of
valuable small dogs took them out of their boxes
and tucked them under her arms, and so toured
around behind the vice-regal party. The Rawsons
are very doggy, and Miss Alice’s Australian ter-
riers—low-comedian-looking dogs—are having the
day that every dog is said to enjoy some time in
his life. I see by a foreign exchange that some
scientist has been experimenting with dogs to
learn if they are capable of reasoning, and his
verdict is that they are not. Don’t think any
of us believe a dog is capable of playing
chess—that requires close reasoning; or of
giving a new reading of Hamlet’s “Solilo-
quies.” But if his food is hot he will lie down
and wait quietly for it to cool. That proves some
reasoning faculty. I had a dog who used to lift
the heavy earthenware cover off a jar to steal bis-
cuits. He would only do it when he thought he
was unobserved, and no amount of coaxing would
induce him to do it as a trick. He pretended he
didn’t know how. And after the steal he didn’t
know enough to re-cover the jar. I thought he
showed some reason once in stopping a runaway
horse. The pony started for home by a long high-
road and the dog (he was a big Newfoundland)
went in pursuit, but suddenly ran off at a right
angle, took a short cut over two paddocks, jumped
several fences, and bailed up the moke (who was
a stable friend of his). When he was a puppy
I. used to punish him by boxing his ears with
his own fore paws. One day when he was a
biggish dog he killed my ’possum, and I was
so mad I looked for a club wherewith to wallop
him. He was abjectly frightened, and backed
away whimpering ; then, thinking there was no
escape from the wrath to come, sought to depre-
cate it by standing on his hind legs and boxing
his ears with his paws. I think the picked dog
can reason—some.
