, The marriage of Justice Hodges’ daughter
™ Majah Brown passed off quietly enough.
The bride had some wonderful presentation lace
to enhance her sacrificial outfit, and a tall
Elizabethan collar to make for queenliness. The
effect was novel. The weight of the toilette, al-
though two children toyed with the five-yard-long
tram, must have induced her extreme pallor, for
the.two Hodges sisters have been much envied for
their rose-freshness. The maids supplied a quaint
group. Large blue-and-white hats with curling
Gainsborough rims and plumes harmonised with
loose scarfs of blue, draped from the floral staff,
round the rear, to the disengaged hand. With
their white frocks and deep sloping yokes of lace
they looked like the gallery types of Marcus
Stones maidens. Surgeon Fitzgerald’s second
daughter was, perhaps, the sweetest of the bevy.
After years of delicate health she has at last de-
veloped into a beauty with a rather wistful plea
in her fine eyes.
The family of Major Clifton Brown (how
effective is Clifton just there !) are described as
very wealthy, and Toorak fervently talks over
the trifling cheque, running into five figures,
sent by his Colonel dad to mark the marriage.
This, says Toorak, is merely ‘a something to go
on with.’ The couple honeymoon at ‘ New-
mmster ’ —the homestead of the soldier-son of
‘Cliveden.’
“An advance Spring fashion appeared at a
recent wedding—a tulle veil drawn over to the
back of a picture hat, and brought round again to
form a large soft bow with ends under the chin.
11 s a dainty, Dolly Varden, demure fashion that
should go with a plump figure, a short skirt, and
a mole. For the present most fashionables con-
tent themselves with an expensive grey and white
double net of. tulle and chiffon that throws the
pink and white, or its color-scheme substitute,
into charming relief.
Janet Lady Clarke must have lately caught
the eye of Mr. David Syme and smiled her newest
Pans smile at that not very susceptible potentate.
The Age now serves as a Melbourne Court
chronicle of the sprightly Dowager’s career. Her
name fills a ‘ star ’ position in the paper, and the
attention of the local world is particularly directed
to the fact that ‘ Janet Lady Clarke and party
arnved at Deniliquin on Thursday night. They
left for Thule Station yesterday on a short visit,’
and so forth. One expects to find Janetlady’s
sojourn at the station recorded under the fanciful
heading of ‘ The Princess of Thule.’
" At the Princess, the ‘ Serenade ’suffered from
dull business in its first week, thanks, partly to the
Serenade ’ itself; partly to lack of personal
charm on the part of the principals. Mdme. Slap.,
sound singer though she is, should be bound over to
solid opera, where she scores by her vocal merits.
In lighter work, her stage- 1 business ’ is quite
pathetic. In boy’s dress, edifying the monks with
display of ‘the way we dance in Andalusia,’ she
prances rather than danoes, and all through,
instead of flitting like a saucy wren, she struts
and curvets like an emu. May Beatty, in page
attire, is also none too sparkling a revelation,
as to either matter or manner. Piddock is deco-
rative enough, but local damsels, somehow, seem
lukewarm. Too much has been printed, inethinks,
re the showy baritone’s ‘ charming wife and sweet
little children ! ’ Lempriere Pringle, now, does
interest she-opera-goers a bit, but, in the ‘ Sere-
nade,’ he has a thoroughly platonic part—no open-
ing at all for airing his Romeo methods !
“The ‘Serenade’ has a piquant scene with a
monkery on one side of a wall and a nunnery on
the other. In East Melbourne, the Presbyscotch
Ladies College is separated only by the breadth
of a street from the Christian Brothers, but com-
munication, even a glance, was scotched—nay
more—killed, ldng, long ago. To the Christian
Brethren s credit, they never did waste many
glances on the Scotch sistern, but the latter were
interested in their gowned neighbors and made
no secret of it. One day the school was assembled
and the stern Principal, his face firm as Scotch
granite, announced in awful tones, ‘ I have just
had a visit from the Rev. Father Z.’ (Everyone
knew what was coming and inwardly called upon a
far-away mother to come and snatch her from the
wrath to come.) According to Father Z., the
pngs next-door had been ‘pained and shocked ’
to the last degree—had declined, in fact, to take
exercise in their grounds, so embarrassed were
they by the she-scrutiny from the college’s up-
stair windows. At this stage of the address there
was an angry swish of ‘ pigtails ’ as every girl in
the room, including the meekest, fiercely tossed
her head and mentally swore off, henceforward
and for ever more, looking on the same side of the
road as a Christian brother.
“ Prof. Peterson has pulled round slowly. He
looked washy the other night, though a full house
welcomed the Conservatorium concert and all
went well. Probably he wouldn’t be half as well
as he is were it not for his clever wife—plucky
strong-hearted, and zealous for the Peterson
prestige. The Professor’s illness, however, has
affected her spirits. A year or two ago Mrs.
Peterson was a bright, sparkling, quizzical soul.
JNow she is staid and quiet and just a little sad.
Agnes Thorn, the star of the Peterson
Conservatorium, is a very capable performer—
and one of many gifts. Her piano playing is as
good as her vocalism, and neither is as promising
as her turn of histrionics.
“ At ‘ Stonington ’ scraps of news are circulated
as to Miss Clarke’s trip abroad. The latest tell
of brilliant jaunts and swagger shivoos under
Melba’s- wide wing. Lady Silverlocks glows at
the very mention of the diva’s name, and to all
who stood by that dominant dame when scandal
was at its height, the Vice-Regals show unstinted
gratitude. It was whispered that a current Aide
last summer suggested caution in regard to
booming and haloing Melba at ‘Stonington,’ but
the head of the house retorted with a stinging
censure. Miss Mignonette Clarke does not start
with Marchesi until Melba is prepared to hie to
Paris, too.
“The eldest hope of the Pursey Chirnsides last
month was cast for one of the train-bearers at the
wedding of Miss Clarke (daughter of the late
Agent-General for the State) in the Big Smoke.
Many Victorians were present, and Miss Nora
Dane—the big, handsome graduate of the Marshall
Hall Conservatorium—helped the tone of things
with the bridal chant from ‘Lohengrin.’ This
bride flourished all her diamonds—among them a
costly tiara. Simplicity in nuptial garments is
going out. The London brides depart from pearls
and lilies-of-the-valley and orange blossom and go
in for an array of gems and exotics. When sim-
plicity is vanishing from the feminine character it
may as well take its characteristic garments
with it.
“ Brighton is a suburb that’s to be respected.
Its bachelors took heart of grace the other day
and issued cards for a ball. In other districts the
tired mothers who are, year in and year out,
pushing ahead bread-and-butter dances, or house
to house parties, are only too grateful if the young
Gussies of their circle casually drop into the whirl
and honor a few of the freshest girls with polka
partnership. A bachelor’s hop is a perfect
wonder. I can almost forgive Brighton its Bent.
“Chief Commissioner Tait’s fair mate is pre-
pared to buckle on the Social armor. Society has
called in battalions on her at the ‘Grand.’ Mrs.
Tait is much younger than her greying lord, and
has all the light conversational froth that generally
characterises the pale gold-haired. There is a
family of one, a youthful daughter.
“A barrister son of Pastor Fitchett married
the other day Mrs. Maynard—a youthful and
very attractive widow. Once more mixed religions
had to be reconciled, and Melbourne witnessed in
surprise one of the Methodist Fitchetts being
harnessed according to the Roman Catholic rules.
Several mixed matches have occurred recently,
and Dr. Carr, at St. Patrick’s, makes his consent
less cordial every time. Sermons are hurled at
the heads of the R.C. congregations constantly,
but without avail. What is wanted is an arrest
of Bridget’s tendency to grow superior to Mike.
“ Great joy in the ranks of science over the
appointment of the hard-working Prof. Martin to
the directorship of the Jenner Institute for Pre-
ventive Medicine. He has long mourned his
restrictions in research, and his helplessness to
advance experimental work ‘through lack of the
very necessary appointments to the Laboratory
here. All scientists lose hope and see their
enthusiasms wane in a State that looks scofflngly
on higher education and runs things generally oil
Bent principles. So the joy of Prof. Martin at
getting a chance elsewhere is shared by sympa-
thisers, who understood how limited was his
scope up at the ‘Shop.’ Even the Melbourne
Board of Health is so badly stocked with instru-
ments that the dootors have to improvise vaccina-
tion needles.
“The first of the novel afternoon rallies, pro--
moted by Mrs. York Syme to aid womankind to
cut her political wisdom tooth, is timed for this
Monday (27th) at Balwyn. Several Federalists
are billed to enlighten the feminine understand-
ing. Like her plump brother—Yes-No George—
Mrs. Syme likes to be moving in a public radius.
For years she has mothered a dozen deserving
charities. They say one Senator has been taking
elocution lessons to witch the ears of the voting
womankind at these cake-and-talk rallies.
“Rep. Crouch, who proposed a Federal Go-as-
you-please, and was tipped to win by a little more
than a perspiration bead, is sadly disappointed
that his suggestion didn’t go. He fought for'it
vigorously, but in a Parliament where the average
age of members is 50 the harrier sprint found few
sympathisers.
“Bishop Clarke and his daughters attended the
Senate galleries last week, under the pilotage of
Mrs. Chairman Best. He had been promised a
treat in oratory, but Mr. O’Connor plugged away
unemotionally on the subject of sugar bounties.
The same afternoon one giddy member of the
Senate had four relays of lady friends, and the
neglected gallants of the House could only grin in
a spirit of covetousness.
A season or two ago two smart sisters-in-law
—Mrs. Pursey and Mrs. George Chirusido—wefe
rival contestants in the chiffon competition.
Society was amused and interested. Then
London ‘ called,’ and off went one after the other
to the Big Smoke. Now two other sisters-in-law
—Mrs. Arthur Payne and Mrs. T. H. Payne-
lead m the cake-walk. They rustle much toge-
ther and consort cheerily, thus robbing the com-
petition of much of its snap. :

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Governor Le Hunte, of S.A., has a grand-
nephew of Nelson for private secretary.
Treasonable lines from recent Brisbane
Telegraph leader:
England’s monarchical line bears so many bars sinister
that it is hardly politic to recall the genealogy.
The strong man of an Australian State
Ministry made his first rise some 20 years back
by engineering a three-card and “ Yankee sweat”
table on a bakblox racecourse.
Some latter-day female names picked from
a list of a couple of dozen appearing in connection
with a fair at a swell suburb of Sydney : Be,
Dick, Bebe, Cecile, Fearon and Billetta.
The still youthful-looking Alfred Deakin
has worn wig and gown for over a quarter of a
century. He has been an off and on journalist a
little longer, and a politician a little longer still.
Cardinal Cullen, Cardinal Moran’s uncle,
was too late to vote for a successor to Pius IX.
Cardinal Moran is certain to be late for the elec-
tion of a successor to Leo XIII. The habit of
being late is in the family.
Short as was the stay in Sydney of novelist
W. E. Norris, a large number of insufficiently
stamped packets of manuscript from city and
suburbs found him out. Only his opinion “as an
impartial judge” was solicited.
Died at Ashfield, Sydney, lrtst Sunday,
Mandarin Quong Tart, aged 53. Quong Tart was
something of a public character years ago, but of
late he dropped a good deal out of notice, and
became an ordinary business citizen.
Justice Cohen, President of N.S.W. Arbi-
tration Court, carried through his law course from
start to finish in three years. Before setting out
for London in pursuit of a call to the Bar he and
his brother ran a haberdashery at Maitland.
Late Sydney brewer J. T. Tooliey, out of
£275,000, left only £9OO to charities—and that
exclusively to R.C. institutions. It was a poor,
contribution to relieving the poverty and distress
which the making of a quarter of a million in beer
inevitably causes.
N.S. VV.’s Nosey Bob, who scragged a
pair the other day, always rises to a big
job. A single hanging doesn’t stir his professional
instincts. “We can accommodate four a time
comfortably,” he remarked lately, “at Darling-
hurst.” “The new gallows,” said chaplain
Bedford in the early days, “ takes seven comfort-
ably—but eight would be a bit crowded.”
RELIGIOUS INSTRUCTION IN STATE-
SCHOOLS.—HERETIC DRILL.
SENATOR FRASER (speaking air; inst Rome at recent
Orange meeting): “ A great responsibility rested on
parents in this and all lands in regard to the rearing of
their offspring. He was sorry to say that in Australia
they did not pay sufficient attention to these matters.”
Queensland Minister Leahy, who has ex-
pressed a determination to put down drinking in
the Civil Service, made the foundation of his
fortune as a Western publican. Now, having
made his own pile, he doesn’t care what becomes
of the next publican.
The irrepressible Smith. At the meeting
of the Riverina branch of the Australian Workers’
Union, held lately v at St. Arnaud, a local commit-
tee was appointed, consisting of J. Smith, S.
Smith, R. Smith, Joseph Smith, W. Smith, and
three others not Smith.
At an inspection of a M.L. Volunteer
corps General Babington put a few questions to a
tire 1-looking swaddie, the last one being a poser.
“ Vou’ve got ren beat, General,” said the trooper,
with a we try smile, and his mates sniggered.
“Babs” glared and passed on.
The familiar face of Adelaide’s leading
paper boy, George, was lately missing for some
days, and the following notice hung on the post
where he was accustomed to howl his wares :
“ George is ill in bed. Won’t be here for a few
days. Society papers please copy.”
Little Chief Justice Way, of S.A., has
played Governor 25 times. He first went into the
business 20 years ago, after Sir Anthony Mus-
grave retired and before his successor, Cairns’s,
arrival. His last “turn” was the longest of all,
lasting 12 months less only a fortnight.
Five out of the seven minor court Judges
of New South Wales have, on occasion, acted as
Justices of the Supreme Court. In fact the
“acting” business has reached such a point that
the State might almost as well dispense with
Judges and let the barristers “ act ” turn about.
Many slanders were uttered about Henry
Parkes before and after his death, but none more
villainous than that put forth recently by Zion
Elijah Dowie, that the old Parliamentary hand
offered him, the said Dowie, the portfolio of
Public Instruction, at the same time guarantee-
ing him a safe seat in Parliament. And Dowie
refused!
The evils of popularity! In a N.S.W.
mining town is a tin-buyer—a man always helping
his poorer brethren in different ways. He has put
up for the Assembly once or twice and been
beaten because his friends voted solidly against
him. As one elector puts it: “ Who’d we go to
to borrow a fiver from if he wuz down at Sydney in
tlie blessed Parlyment ? ”
Dr. Angel Money’s house in Macquarie-
street, Sydney, is advertised for sale. It has been
an unlucky place. Dr. Harman Tarrant, for mat y
years its tenant, stumbled and came to griet,
quackery and an unhappy death. Then Dr.
Angel Money —for many years one of the city’s
leading physicians—broke down utterly from over-
work. ° The house will probably be pulled down
now and the site used for a big boarding-house or
a nest of “ fiats.”
Braddon of the Blot, ordinarily so tame
and courteous, was thirty years ago known in
India as the King of Tiger-slayers.
Reynolds’ says that the Princelet of
Wales is constantly under doctor’s orders, and
has been prescribed another long sea-voyage—to
India this time—for the good of his health.
“lam quite willing to give you a couple
of columns’ talk,” said the M.L.A. to the news-
paper interviewer, “ but I’ll want payment.
“But,” said the interviewer, “the adver-
tisement of the thing counts.” “ I have
my own ways of advertising myself,
and don’t need to enlarge them.”
The interviewer returned later and
said, “We’ll introduce the article
with the statement that your
popularity and assured position
in the front rank of politicians
make your opinions of the highest
public value.” M.L.A. suc-
cumbed.
Little Justice Holroyd, now
senior Yic. puisne Judge, regu-
larly every morning has a boxing bout for his
health’s sake with his son Spencer. The latter
is the old man’s associate in court, and some-
times raises the dander of “the Bench” by not
being visible at the exact moment he is wanted.
When this occurs the legal element winks and
predicts that his Honor will hit out hard at
Spencer when he puts on the gloves next morning.
His Honor is said to punch like a demon when his
blood is up.
R. L. J. Ellery, who for two generations was
Government Astronomer and Official yVeather'Pro-
phet at Melbourne, was the first medical man who
attended John Price when he was fatally injured
by the convicts at Williamstown. The old
flogging days look as if they were hull down on
the foggy horizon of antiquity, but they only
belong to yesterday, after all.
Bishop Frodshain (Anglican), of N.Q.,
who is now taking the plate round Australia to
get coin to repair the ravages of the cyclone in his
diocese, is a handsome, clean-shaven young man,
who, in any crowd of R.C.’s, would pass for one of
them. To add to the resemblance, he wears, like
a Roman bishop, a huge amethyst ring and a
purple “ dickie. ” The reason why a Roman
bishop wears a bit of purple where the Anglican
man sports white is, it has been stated on unre-
liable authority, that the Roman is mostly a
bachelor, and purple doesn’t need to be “done
up” so often in the laundry. To a bachelor, the
laundry question is a great one.
In a certain Australian State lives a man
related to a political joss. This man might be
called Jones, though he isn’t. It was Jones who
rushed to the edge of the wharf, when
the boat was approaching, and sang out,
“ Hurrah ! boys, the drought has broke ;
there ’as been an electoral disturbance all
over Australia.” One of . Jones’s weak-
nesses was teaching his family the.following
parrot rhyme: “I am Mr. Bill Jones,
Esq., J.P., coroner, Mayor of Mudbank-
ville, brother to the almighty politician
Thomas Jones, brother to the Mayor of
Buncoville, and monarch of all I survey.”
This went on for some time, and when
any member of the family misbehaved,
he was placed in a corner and made to re-
peat the story to his indignant father. At
last, the eldest of the family went out
into the world and received his first
lesson in the relative sizes of things.
One day, he “ crossed ” the paternal one,
and was called to the corner to purge
himself of the offence. “Who am I?”
thundered Jones, lookingfiercely at his
offspring. There was silence for a moment
and the son replied: “A great damphool,
father, and the only idiot in Mudbankvdle
as doesn’t know it! ” Then the roars of father
Jones could have been heard half-a-mile off as he
shouted for blood.
Mr. William Pritchard, an old Sydney
identity, died at Burwood recently, aged 74 years.
M any years ago, while seeking election to the
City Council, annoyed at interruptions, he said
that if he had his way he would poll tax every
Irishman that came to Australia. That expres-
sion lost him the election. Dan O’Connor, how-
ever, was his life-long friend. They were once
in a pork butchery together.
J. Alexander, a Scotchman, who has just
retired (on reaching 70) from the E.S. and A
Bank in Mount Gambier (S.A), told some in-
teresting yarns to a big meeting that farewelled
him. Alexander was banking in Chicago during
the Secession war, when it was an untidy little place
of 75,000 people. In the sixties he established
branches of the Standard Bank all over South
Africa, and was then taken with the diamond
fever, holding a claim right in the middle of what
is now Kimberley. Here l.e struck a boulder,
about the size of a baby mountain, which he had
not the means to remove, so he sold out to Cecil
Rhodes, who afterwards took £lOO,OOO worth of
stones out of that claim. For the last 30 years
Alexander has been managing country banks in
S.A., and for the last 20 he has been at Mfc.
Gambier, where he proposes to end his days.
QUITE RESTFUL.
“If Labor would only shake off that false old notion
that wealth employs labor and realise that labor works
for labor the country would soon prosper.” —Brilliant
hunk from Age article against Socialism.
“ Well, Jim,! if we weren't doing it for ourselves
I'd reckon it work.”
The Age again clubs its Joss :
With the exception of Graham Berry, Victorian
politicians have been entirely lacking in Chat attractive
force without which leadership is a very short-lived
affair. Not one of the political leaders of the day
possesses it. Mr. Irvine, for instance, who came into
power on the crest of a wave of popular feeling, stands
only temporarily at the head of the Reform party, for not
even his most ardent admirers will credit him with being
adequately equipped for leadership.
When the late Pope took the title in 1878
it was commonly remarked that the cardinals had
chosen a very aged and physically fragile man for
the distinction in order that others among them
might have a chance of taking their turns in the
Chair. Nobody thought of Leo XIII. becoming
a great institution a quarter of a century ahead—
except the insurance companies which speculated
in Pontifical life policies.
Sydney University is in mourning over
the death of F. W. Lloyd, universally known as
“Tommy Lloyd,” lecturer in Latin. Though
born in U.S.A. Lloyd was educated at Sydney
Grammar School and entered the University in
1877. He graduated in 1890 with the University
gold medal, and became in succession master to
his old school and lecturer to his old faculty. He
was only 35 at the time of his death, and his mar-
riage had been set down for August 22.
Chief Justice Way (S.A.) has a habit of
cracking small jokelets on the Bench, often when
a counsel is in the middle of his address or cross-
examination. Juniors, as a rule, smile or laugh
obsequiously as the size of the joke apparently
demands. Symon waits, witli an unsmiling face,
until the chief has finished his wild burst of
humor, and then continues. Nesbit once went
one better. He waited until the “little man”
got through, and then remarked, quietly : “I—
er—suppose we laugh here, your Honor?” Every-
one did laugh except Way.
“J.”: Apropos father and son contem-
porary pensioners (8., 16/7/ 03). The late John
Cecil Read, of Darlinghurst Gaol, and his son
William Vero Read, of the N.S.W. railways,
were pensioners on the same list. J. C. Read re-
tired in 1889 on £372 per annum (now dead); the
son retired on March 1, 1890, on £546 per annum
(still living). There is an alleged baronetcy in
the Read family, but the last to use thi *itle
was the present W. V. Read’s grandfather.
Whitaker’s Peerage speaks scornfully of the
baronetcy, and says it became extinct in 1711.
Premier Barton fought for the Naval
Tribute Bill as he never before in his life fought
for a principle or a policy. He threatened to take
any amendment as a vote of censure, and went so
far as to use as an argument the statement that
Captain Cresswell—our local expert on naval
matters and the champion of an Australian navy—
had approved of the Tribute Bill, a statement
which he had to afterwards withdraw as utterly
incorrect. Australia’s Nobbled Son only aroused
himself from a long torpor to bargain away the
Commonwealth’s best interests to Chamberlain.
Policeman John Johnston, orderly at Wel-
lington (M.L.) magistrate’s court, shot himself
dead, at the police court, a fortnight ago. John-
ston was the man who, a few years back, boarded
a Yankee barque at the Wellington wharf, and,
single-handed, arrested one of the crew who was
shooting his mates promiscuously on the deck.
The shooter had a loaded revolver in his hand
when the policeman looked him in the eye and
Slid, “You’re my prisoner.” For this act of
ridiculous bravery, Johnston was given an extra
6d. a day and made court orderly, and his arrestee
was given 10 years. The jury opined that the big
man was temporarily insane with the usual tem-
porary insanity of coroners’ juries, but his friends
know that it was just indigestion that made him
kill himself. The indigestion horror is sufficient
explanation of anything, and why a jury should
go out of its way to tell a conventional lie in a
case where the truth is so much more convincing
no one knows.
A Mohammedan interpreter, in the Ade-
laide police court the other day, blocked the pro- /
ceedings by absolutely’ refusing to translate a bad
word which had been used bv a colored brother.
It was against his religion, he said, and he
wouldn’t repeat it for anything. The point being
of some importance, as an insult inviting an
assault in return, counsel'gave him a lead over.
“Was it —— ?’ 1 said Paris Nesbit, and the inter
prefer, in his haste to agree, said “Yes, it was ——’’
After which he mournfully assured the court, that
it was the first time he had ever used the word—
“ and I have been in these courts for 14 years. ”
“ Jeroug ” writes : .-■<
So far as my knowledge is concerned of the late James :
Torpy and the stirring times at Lambing Flat (whiefr
knowledge dates from the first riots that took place
against the Chows at Stony Creek in December, 1860), I
never knew Mr. Torpy to do any digging on the Flat.
Many were aware he sold a claim on the Turon to the
Chows for a tidy sum. lam not aware of his taking pairt ■
in any of the proceedings on the visit of the then Colonial
Secretary, Charley Oowper, who came and held meetings,
on Spring and Stony Creeks, and settled a plough-line ,
boundary from Back Creek to Blackguard Gully, over
which the Chows were not to trespass. The Chows ig-
nored the plough-line and encroached over. Result:
Second big roll-up on the 30th June, 1861, that wiped
them off the Flat. The attack on the camp took place on-,
the 14th July, same year, and moved from Torpy’s Hotel,
Tipperary Gully. Spicer, Cameron and Stewart were the
leaders. Never knew Torpy as an agitator, or to make a
speech until he and Baker returned from Sydney. Torpy
and Baker (one a publican, the other keeper of a Temper-
ance Caf6) were selected by the diggers to lay their griev-
ance before the Governor (John Young), hut he refused to
see them. Excitement ran high with the Sydney people
over the Governor’s action, and Torpy was invited to
address a meeting in the School of Arts. The police
apprehended Torpy on a bogus warrant. Bennett, of
Hanson and Bennett, who owned the Empire of those '
days (not Parkes), came to the aid of Torpy. The authori-
ties refused bail for Torpy. Bennett demanded it, if it.
went to the amount of £50,000. Torpy was liberated, ,
and the affair hushed up. David Buchanan asked in the
Assembly for the production of the sworn testimony oh
which the warrant was issued, but he had no
save Moffit, the Blue Nose solicitor. Parkes never by
word or deed showed any sympathy for the diggers.
Torpy and Baker gained notoriety over the affair, which
helped them politically in after years. The Empire stuck
to the diggers manfully ; the S.M. Herald and Melbourne
Argus were continually sooling the Government on ta
reinstate the Chows.
Tired of waiting for Snow to electrify the
Adelaide trams, the city council has appointed A
committee to inquire if something cannot be done
in the way of motor omnibuses. Young Bonython
has the matter in hand, so presumably pa’s paper,
is at the back of him. But, if pa’s paper had done
its duty in the past, Snow would not have blocked
the way to the improvement of the tram system. .
A TRIFLE.
“Mr. Glassev (Q.) presented a petition with four signa-
tures from the'Brisbane Chamber of Commerce against,
the anti-black labor clause of the Postal Act.”
Premier Barton only got the Naval Tribute
Bill through the House of Reps, by energetic
cracking of the party whip. He threatened
resignation if the Bill were rejected or amended,
and, as that meant losing Kingston, Turner,
Deakin and O’Connor and replacing them with
Heaven knows whom, some members were doubt-
less influenced by the threat. If the opponei ts
of the measure had made a strong demonstration
against it on the Address-in-Reply, there would
have been a better chance of delaying the con-
clusion of the Tribute agreement until public
opinion had been awakened to the nature of its
betrayal of Australian interests. The Australian
party in the Senate will probably put up a strong
fight, and there is yet a remote hope of rejection
or modification.

“ Chasselas ” supplies more fox and bunny
theories:—
Re foxes killing lambs (Bulletin 16,'7/’03): “ Oumbo *
is wrong in his description of the way a fox deals with a
lamb. Reynard is an artist at the biz. He makes a small
hole close to the last rib, sucks the blood, and gets the
heart, liver and caul fat. Never knew any other animal
to treat its prey in this fashion except a half-bred dingo
up Wimmera (Vic.) way. Most sheep-slayers get to the
throat and legs. The fox does not kill as many lambs as
he gets credit for. He prefers young rabbits, and when
he finds a closed burrow he scratches a hole right over
the nest and hits the mark every time.
* * * *
The first fox heard of in the Paroo
(N.S.W.) district was killed at Goorimpa a few
days ago. Reynard, like bunny, marches north.

“Wilgar”: Accounts of recent trip
through the Vic. Mallee make me suspect that
the “magnificent natural resources of this great
country” wouldn’t support a suburb, let alone a
nation. Lascelles, of Tyrrell Downs, applying
for an extension of his lease, said that he had
cleared the land (at a cost of 10s. 9d. per acre) for
his tenants ; erected a vermin-proof fence round
the whole cultivated area ; advanced seed, fodder,
meat and other provisions (money payable after
harvest) the first year; and in most cases, for
three out of the six years, got no returns at all.
His third-share, in one exceptionally good
instance, panned out like this: Ist harvest, nil:
2nd, 3 bushels an acre ; 3rd, 4 bushels ; 4th, l£
bushels ; sth, nil; 6th, nil; or, for the six seasons,
1000 bags off 1800 acres cultivated ! In the
Waitchie country, across the boundary, there
were, last year, 200 families, now there are 20—
and Waitchie is mostly suckers and white ants.

The faithful dog again. A short time back
a foreigner, named Carl Rudolph Gellert (well
known about Georgetown), was found dead in a
bush gunvah at Talbot Creek (Q.). Neighbors had
frequently passed the gunyah, and though they
had not seen the owner for some days, they did
not suspect anything wrong, as his dog always ran
out, harking. When they did look in, they found
Gellert had been dead a week, and was partially
consumed. Presumably the faithful dog would
have kept on watohing while his master lasted.
About an axe-
man :
“ Did y’ ever meet a
bloke called Totara Joe ?”
said Dirty Beard to Billy.
“ Who was Totara
Joe?" asked Billy.
“ He was a well-known
bash-whacker. Yermust
of heard of him. He was
the greatest axeman in
the world. Why, I
thought any chump
knoo ’im. Chop 1 He
could have chopped up
an earthquake 1 He uster
take a mate to work
with him—a cove that
carried a long-handled
shovel. Soon as Joe got
ter business this cove
with the shovel had ter
keep blanky well movin’,
of the two of ’em would
’er been buried alive
with the chips. Chop 1
My oath, Joe could chop?
Yous all know that the
game in bush-failin’ is to
make “ drives,” that is,
to put a back scarf in
the trees in line, and
then cut a big bloke
down, so that in its fall
it sends the one in front
crashin’ into the other
ahead, and so on. You’s
got yer wood down quick
if yer cunnin’ at that.
Joe’s best performance
at that was in the Forty
Mile Bush. He went
back scarfing for a week,
and after he had cut
his driver down, him
and the cove with the
shovel went into Dannevirke. The everlastin’ row them
trees made cornin’ down was terrible. Blokes thought
the end of the world had come. After Btoppin’ in town
for a week’s spree, Joe and the shovel bloke went back
and measured up the drive.”
“ How much ? ” said Billy Hart.
“ Forty acres,” replied Dirty Beard.
Billy staggered to the door. “ I’ll go out for a spell,'
he said.

More casualness : A Gippsland wood-
carter left his horse to find its way home, the
other day, while he slumbered placidly in the
dray. As the horse was quite blind, it presently
walked over a bank into the canal and took with
it driver, dray and another horse and jinker which
were tied on behind. The casual woodman
wakened and sat up on the bottom of the canal,
and swam gradually ashore, but no more was seen
of the blind horse. Some canal boat will pro-
bably go aground on its bones in future years.

GRAHAM BERRY, of Victoria, ex-Premier, ex-Agent-
General, and Tribune of the People (photographed
at the age of 81),
Photo, by Johnstone O'Shannessy & Co., Melbourne.
A refreshing whiff from the terminus of
the first Victorian narrow-gauge railway, in the
Upper King country, via Wangaratta :
The closer settlement at Whitfield is an eye-opener as
to what can be done by practical industrious men on a
small area of land. The estate that formerly fed cattle,
sheep, and rabbits, and employed two or three people to
manage it, now feeds scores of families directly and in-
directly. Every holding is a picture—the homestead
plain and neat, and dairy herd sleek and fat, the fields
strong and vigorous in crops of all kinds. Bags of
potatoes stand thick in every field, maize 10 and 12 feet
high drying off, and pumpkins by thousands of tons
lying like huge cannon balls over the fields. The
creamery is besieged by loaded milk carts, and the State
schools (two) are filled with the settlers’ “olive branches.”
They are evidently making more money out of their 100-
acre blocks than most selectors do out of their thousands
of acres.
If the big land monopolist were abolished, that
great waste and wilderness of blank grass which
stretches from Melbourne’s back door almost to
Geelong might flourish in similar fashion, and
then the rush of Victoria’s population to look for
land in Africa might perhaps be stayed.

“ Paroo ” sends a picture of the hospitality
of a wayback licensed caravanserai:—
Outback recently I struck an old shanty bear-
ing the legend “Travellers’ Rest.” Walked
into the so-called bar, and found a man sitting
on the floor operating on his corns with a
buteher’s-knife.
“ Are you the landlord?”
“ Can’t you see I am ? ”
“ Well, give me a glass of brandy.”
“Brandy? D’ye think you’ve struck the
blooming Hotel Australia?”
“ Got any whisky, then ?”
“No ; sold the last bottle t’other day to the
overseer on the station. But I’ve get some
rum.”
“ Is that it?”—pointing to a venomous-looking
greenish fluid in a white bottle.
“ ’Tis so.”
No ; I didn’t drink rum. Could I have a
bed? Was shown into a room ; bedstead made
of old packing-cases, walls of whitewashed calico.
In the room on my left was a man (evidently full
up of the landlord’s “rum”) singing “Rock of
Ages.” In the room on my right was a boy in
bed teaching a cattle-pup to “sit up.” Verily,
this was a “ Travellers’ Rest.”
About midnight I fell asleep, and when I
awoke in the morning I heard the landlady
call out—“ Tommy, run down and see if there is
anything on the line.” In a few minutes back
came Tommy, bawling : “No, mother ; there’s
nothing on the hook.”
On coming out of the room I met the landlady.
“ Good morning, sir," she said. “ I am very
sorry there is no meat in the house, and Tommy
tells me there is no fish on the line, but if you
wouldn’t mind killing a goat (as my ’usband
ain’t none too good this morning) you could ’ave
some ‘fry’ for breakfust.” Told the lady I
didn’t care about “fry,” but, to oblige her, I
would kill the goat. While she was hunting for
the butcher’s tools I looked out into the yard,
and saw the victim waiting—a half-bred Angora,
and a gentleman at that. The butcher’s
knife couldn’t be found ; and, as I wasn’t over-
anxious to assassinate an old William-goat with
a blunt pocket-knife, I left without any break,
fast.

“ A.C.” : Why is it that no effort
has been made to acclimatise the Murray
cod in the eastern streams of N.S.W. and
Victoria? Great trouble and expense have
been taken to acclimatise trout, 6almon,
Eerch, &c. These imported fish are no
etter in flavor than the cod, and they don’t
come within coo-ee of him for size. When
the inland fish are taken from running water they
have no “ muddy ” flavor. It is when the streams
cease flowing that the jew, cod, and yellow-belly
develop that peculiar twang.

N.S.W. Aborigines Protection Board re-
port for 1902 shows that there are now 3036 full-
blood blacks in the State, as against 6450 in 1882.
In 1901 the black race lost 65 by excess of deaths
over births.
“6xB Re the oft-told yarn
about the luck of the boozer. Walking
along our under-ground paddock fence
t’other afternoon, I spied between tl.e
trunk of a Cape gooseberry-tree and a
straining-post a huddled something
which, at first glance, appeared to be a
second-1 land reaper and binder. Closer
inspection, however, revealed a sound-
asleep tipsy drover, in charge of
a travelling mob of Angora cats.
Aroused, he informed me in lurid lingo
that be bad climbed the gooseberry-
tree after a White Bird of Sheol with
green wings, pink tail, blue top-knot,
four yellow legs, three diamond-stud-
ded golden beaks, and red claws of
galvanised iron. When he reached tne
tree-top the bird flew upward, and he
climbed after it into space for a tew
hundred feet, when he suddenly came
down “wallop,” with Ins back across
the top rail of the fence, breaking right
through the two ironbark rails and
five steel wires ! His neck, back, botn
arms, breast and collar-bones and all
his ribs were broken. He rose and
hiccoughed : “ Thash nothing ; I m all
right, ole man,”—and then staggered
across the near-by railroad just in time
to get caught and entangled m the
wheels of a passing express train, which
dragged him along out of sight. Going
home late that night from a prayei-
meeting, I met him again in the
crowded bar of the “Drovers’ Rest, in
the middle of a step-dance. He didn t
know me again, but casually remem-
bered a bit of a spill be had bad during
the afternoon. The luck of the boozy
per ion is indeed wonderful to behold .
It often borders on grnesomeness.

“ Vigilans ” writes ;
One hundred and twenty miles from Eucla, on the S.A.
side, there is a station called Null-Arbor. This property
has been ruined owing to the depredations of wi
dogs and rabbits, and other causes. Stock has almost
entirely disappeared, and the few remaining sheep have
to be'shepherded. The S.A. Government, in all its
wisdom, is now fencing 60 miles of this run with a so-
called “dog-proof fence.” It makes me laugh . ihe
posts are two chains apart, with braces here and there,
the wire-netting is 3ft, sin. out of the ground, with two
barbed wires on top. It blows like blazes across these
plains, and Jenkins’ fence will go flying into N.S.W. one
of these days. The people who have this run are now
attempting, or going te attempt, the task of clearing it
of vermin when fenced. By the way, a squatter has the
contract for carrying this netting from Eucla at £5 per
ton, and has five camel teams employed ; not a white
man is to be found amongst them.

“War E.” : Even a land agitator in
N.S.W. needs a long memory! At the recent
inquiry into the resumption of Mimosa Station
(N.S.W.) for closer settlement purposes, two
leading lights of the Fanners’ and Settlers
Association stated on oath that a man could make
a living off 600 acres of land and pay 2s. 6d. rent
per acre for it. Yet these are the same persons
who are howling so violently about the tyranny of
the Government in asking the poor down-trodden
“ cocky ” to pay more than Is. per acre for O.P. s
(the land becomes absolutely freehold in 28 years);
the soil is similar and in many cases nearer the
railway than the Mimosa blocks. At the same
inquiry one “expert” based his experience as a
land-valuer on the fact that as a boy he had won a
ploughing match.
DINNER CAMP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Easy, lads, for a long way yet
Is the road you’ll travel to Hergott town ;
But the feed is good, and the season wet,
So block the leaders, and steady ’em down.
See ’em spreading across the flat—
Half a thousand, and Queensland bred,
Roans, whites, cherries, and rolling fat,
Cropping the trefoil with bended head.
Hear ’em sigh in their full content
As they drop in the shade of the noonday camp,
Where the pungent strength of the gidgee’s scent
Mingles with odors of grasses damp.
A softened jingle of pack-horse bells
Rings out from the curve of a distant bend,
While the far-off mutter of thunder tells
Of a storm to fall ere the daylight end.
Through the lazy hours of the midday halt
The lean brown horsemen smoke and drowse,
Till the sun droops down in the clear blue vault
To the west, and the sluggish cattle rouse
And straggle out from the shady trees
As their heads to the lush green grass go down.
Steady, beauties, and take your ease,
’Tis a long, long journey to Hergott town.

“E.S.S.”: Under the new regulations
issued by the Queensland Government, employers
of half-caste and black gins are required to pay the
wages agreed upon in full to
the local Protector, who will
place the money to the black
depositor’s oredit in the Sav-
ings Bank. Such money will
be available when necessaries
are wanted, upon application
being made to the Protector.
The males are left to look
after their own financial
affairs until the present ex-
periment has had a trial.
Murri’s “necessaries” are
mostly rum and tobacco,
and as to his business
acumen, his wages account
in the old days was mostly
settled by “ book yabba.”
The formula was some*
thing like this: Squatter :
“There’s 21b. tobacco you
got—lss.” Murri: “Das
right.” S. : There’s lib.
you didn’t get, 7s. 6d.”
M. : “ Das right.” S. :
“One pound you’ll never
get—7s. 6d.” And so with the
boots he was going to get, and the shirts he
ought to have got —till only a couple of shillings
were required to balance the account. Very
often the luxuries he had no hope of getting
left Murri in debt, and he had then to work off
the “dead horse ” before he could get credit for
the pants he didn’t want. Murri has gained a lot
of financial experience since those days, but still,
when accounts are settled quarterly, the old book
can * ‘ yabba ” a heap.
“ Peri ” re the great Irvine-Bent job ab
Waranga Basin :
Things are awfully bad. The splendid rains have proved
the country’s salvation, but the toilers’ damnation.
Haven’t worked a full week these last ten weeks. The
ground is glue and pea soup, and unworkable after rain.
Horses are sitting on their tails half their time instead of
hauling, having no foothold. Contractor is at his wits’
end to keep his unfortunates together, so he has put
them on at stone-breaking. They earn 4s. 6d. a day
when proficient—9d. a yard, or Is. 6d. a yard and take
the stuff out yourself. Another lot he put on at sleeper
hewing (ss. a hundred, ye gods) for the proposed tram-
way to the quarry, 1| miles away. Three men went out,
strong of heart, put in three weeks at the game, cut 500
sleepers, which averaged Bs. 3d. per man for the three
weeks work, so the contractor raised the rate to 7s. 6d.
per 100 —treacle and pollard at that. Fossicking is the
last resource till machinery supersedes the team-haulage,
and the king of the Waranga Basin fossickers made a
haul of 18s. for a week’s fossicking. “No work, no
credit!” is the cry in the few stores remaining. On
O’Reilly Bros’, contract things are just as bad. No
farmer within cooee can keep a fowl or pig, and there’s
the smell of Sheol all round. Spring should see thing 3
improving, but the Lord only know?.

“ Djin Djin ” : In some parts of the North
Island of Maoriland, especially up Maungakaiiia
way, the much-belauded brown man and woman
are living the life of the dog, and worse—herding
together in hopeless indolence, abject poverty,
and unspeakable squalor. Traversing a country
of 40 miles, not a solitary crop is to be seen, but
on all sides evidence of abso'Ae want and lack of
the decencies of life. The filthiness of the Maori’s
surroundings is too amazing for print; unkempt,
unwashed, half-naked, they huddle together like
swine, in smoky, grimy whares scarcely fit for the
habitation of the pigs and mongrels that in-
fest the settlement. Referring to the degenerate
state of the natives in the King Country since the
advent of the white man, Stipendiary Magistrate
Northcote recently said he had been through the
King Country many years ago, when the natives
were a healthy, clean-living, vigorous people, but
now men, women, and children were addicted to
drunkenness, and an all-round degeneracy had set
in.
“ Sat On”: When prospecting in Queens-
land, a blackfellow and lubra came to tha camp.
Gave them a good feed. Presently the blissful
feeling in the belt region sent the man off into a
doze. The lubra squatted on the ground a little
distance off smoking. I got my sun-glass fixed
on a longish bit of stick and got the sun-ray
focussed on the part of the aboriginal which a
white man usually covers with pants. Presently
there was a most fearsome yell, and a wildly
excited blackfellow clawing at his hindquarters.
“My word, what phella that, mine tinket big
phella hornet bite alonga this phella hide. Why
you laugh, you woman. I teach it you laugh at
me.” After the lubra was duly corrected, and
things settled a bit, I reckoned the lady deserved
her revenge, and I showed her how to manipulate
the burning glass. In due course she got a most
lovely point on the same spot. Then that black
man went four feet into the air at a moment’s
notice. “Ah-a-a, blurry big phella snake bite
this phella hide this time, plenty deep, plenty
soon die, mine tinkit,” he remarked before he
came down out of the atmosphere. Fear of more
waddy kept the gin from grinning that time, but
she looked the most completely satisfied female in
Australia.

The following appeared in the Age lately :
A gentleman at Beeac has been unfortunate in losing a
number of his best fowls in a peculiar manner. The
birds were in the habit of roosting on the axles of the
railway trucks and a special train which left Beeac at
night took away the trucks and the fowls also, some of
them hanging to the perch till the train reached Geelong.
The owner, who was returning home, was startled to sea
some of his own fowls on the Geelong station.
About which it can only be said that they were a
very adhesive kind of fowl.

HIS LAST COIN.
STONY TRAVELLER: “ For Gated’s sake gimme a
dorg’s worth o’ beer, boss.”
The sad story of a drunken pony :
Tartarin was a piebald pony on the station where I was
working. Left an orphan early, he was brought up by
hand. He was the horror of the women-folk, as he had
an affectionate way of running after everyone who came
into the paddock and wanting to shake “hands.” The
boss’s boy had taught him, amongst numerous other
tricks, to stand perfectly still while the lad danced api s
seul on his back ; after which he would gallop him roun ,
sitting back to front, and steering by the tail. Tartarn.
would steal into the yard when the washing was hanging
out, and chew the edges of the articles till each item was
graced with a sort of embroidered border. Sometimes
his appetite took a turn for curtains, which he would pull
out of windows left open on warm nights, frightening
the youngsters into fits. He was also a petty lareenist.
I have seen him coming out of the store, after stealing
sugar, with a face of bahe-like innocence, blissfully un-
conscious that his muzzle was beautifully frosted with
white crystals. He would eat red herrings and drink
brandy. Drink, by-the-way, was the death of him. One
Sunday afternoon I had occasion to go into the store,
and there was Tartarin lying hopelessly drunk, and with
both fore-legs broken. He had pulled the plug out of a
Cask of whisky, tipped it to one side, and “ toped” his
fatal fill.
A PLAIN UNVARNISHED LIE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am not good at truthful tabs,
So let me tell a lie !
There is a place in New South Wales
That’s known as Gundabj e.
If you have never heard its fame
You needn’t hang your head for shame,
Because that ain’t its proper name—
This yarn is purely Lie !
All Lie I
It’s nothing else but Lie !
Well, Thomas Brown and William Jcnes
Had farms near Gundabye ;
Grew thistles, Bathurst burrs and stones,
Or wheat when not too dry.
Now Brown’s farm, you must undi rstand,
Was on the hill-side. Soil was grand.
But, Lord ! it was the steepest land ;
Shot upwards to the sky !
The farm of Jones was down below,
The farm of Brown up high,
The mountain slops was steep, you know,
Straight plumb, or very nigh !
An earthquake came ! The farm < f Brown,
With house and orchard, all slid down
And smothered Jones’s ! Like that town
Some folks call “ Pompey-Eye ” !
Well, Jones he swore he'd go to law
And make Brown shift his farm.
But Brown replied : “ I’m nearly sure
I never meant no harm.
This talk of law is all d-—n fine,
But I’m not in the legal line.
You shift your farm from under mine /’*
Said Brown in accents calm.
W. T. GOODGE.
Sunday I caught a very bad cold,
Monday it took a rather firm hold,
Tuesday I had to take to my bed,
Wednesday I thought I d shortly be dead,
Thursday came and I got a bit wone,
Friday they talked of getting a nurse,
But Saturday brought relief swift and sure—

Graham Berry.
Gkaham Berry —whose latest photo, appears in
this issue ; Berry whom Victoria knew for so
long as the lusty leader of its Liberal party ; the
stalwart fighter; the incomparable stump-speaker,
full of flashing phrases ; daring, energetic, and
strenuous—is now bent under the weight of years,
which within a month will number 81. He is no
longer able to bear a part in the conflicts of the
day. When he was in his prime the fires burned
fiercely, and consumed the more ; now they burn
low, and the contrast is all the more mai’ked.
Perhaps he himself was the first to mark the
change ; and so, while men said that he
was still vigorous in spite of his 65 years,
he went to England in 1887 as Agent-General;
and Australia saw the old Graham Berry—
the daring Tribune of the People—no more.
When he came back he was a democrat still; but,
with more than 70 years upon him, subdued. The
dignity of honored old age had succeeded a per-
haps too strenuous life of battle. Yet, remember-
ing what he had been, Victoria still had honors
for him. He had held many of its portfolios, had
been its Premier, its Agent-General. Parliament
made him its Speaker now ; and the country chose
him as one of its representatives at the Conven-
tion to frame the Australian Constitution. That
was his last—fittingly his last—appearance in the
larger field of public life. His colleagues, the
onlooker noticed, put him in the front, a white-
bearded patriarch, feeble but courtly; they de-
ferred to him ; they did him honor—and in doing
it did honor to themselves. But, in spite
of a warm welcome, his voice was rarely heard ;
and when he did speak it was rather to offer en-
couragement in a general way, as an old man who
had nearly run his race to a young one entering
on his own, rather than to help in working out
the _ troublesome details of the partnership.
Minister, Premier, Agent-General, Speaker—but
he might have been all these and yet less than he
was. Coming into the public life of Victoria in
the earliest sixties, he found the squattocracy
almost supreme. With a fine courage he fought
to substitute for it a purer democracy. Aiming
also at the creation of an Australian sentiment
and an Australian nation—aiming to make
Australia fit to stand on its own foundations—
he blazed the track, too, for Protection. He
was more than a politician ; he was a leader ; he
moulded opinion ; and it may be some comfort to
the old Tribune in his closing days to know that
young Australia remembers his services, remem-
bers them with gratitude, and will continue to
remember them. .

Melbourne Age, in a recent lament about
the wholesale departure from Victoria of its most
vigorous bread-winners, writes thus about the
elect of Kyabram:
The M'Kenzie regime at the Lands Office was exactly
what the Oameron occupancy of the Mines and Water
Supply Department is, and what that of Mr. Murray is in
the Chief Secretaryship. It would have stagnated any
State as far as bad government can do so.
Bent, Shiels, and Irvine are also fallen idols.
“6 x 8” : Irvine’s blackleg crews of
“loyal” engine-drivers are no doubt just now,
and will be for some little time, despised by the
union drivers. That sort of thing never lasts
long. One of the faults of the unionist is his
habit of too quickly forgetting things. There are
to-day paid office-holders in Australian trades
unions (I am personally acquainted with some)
who have in the past “ ratted ” on their respective
unions and otherwise worked against Labor in-
terests, and both the men who elected them and
their fellow officers know it.

Apropos of the cry about how “ Socialistic
legislation ” is driving foreign capital out of Aus-
tralia, it is interesting to remember that foreign
capital has also been almost entirely driven out of
the tlnited States, France and Germany. The
people there simply set to work under huge Pro-
tective tariffs, and made capital for themselves.
Their own capital drove out the foreign article.
Let us do likewise.
“Victoria has been hitherto governed by
a squattocracy, and men willing to work bad to
pay men who did not work as much as 15s. an
acre for the privilege of cultivating the soil.” —
Tom Mann.
Governor Phillip’s first piece of land
legislation granted to married soldiers 40 acres
each, two assigned servants each, rations for three
years, and seeds, farm implements and building
materials —all to induce settlement. Australia
started with State socialism.
The man on the Land is always in trouble,
but his troubles end when he places his business
in the hands of Land Agent W. N. Willis, of 21
Bligh-street. No results, no fees, is his business
shingle.*
SUFFERING WOMEN.
To women suffering from chronic constipation*
headaches, biliousness, dizziness, sallowness of
the skin and dyspepsia, Chamberlain’s stomach
and liver tablets will invariably bring relief.
“ Just What You Need.” Price, Is. 6d. and for
sale by all dealers.*
For the past seven years Canada has not
added a penny to its public debt, but, besides
wiping something off the slate, has paid for all
current works out of revenue. For the preceding
18 years it borrowed at the average rate of
£1,300,000 per annum. And the gain in popula-
tion and wealth has been greater in the seven
years when there was no borrowing than it was in
the 18 years when the debt was being piled up.
Taxation more than sufficient to allow the country
to pay its way lias thus had the very opposite
effect to scaring away either capital or people.

N.S.W. PATRIOTIC FUND
REPORT OF EXECUTIVE COMHITTEE
For the Year 1902=1903,
Presented to and adopted by the General Committee at the Meeting
held on Wednesday, 22nd July, 1903,
THE RIGHT HON. THE LORD MAYOR (ALD. THOS. HUGHES) PRESIDING.
Your Executive Committee have met twenty-three (23) times since 30th July, 1902.
First Applications for Relief during’ that period totalled 334, which were dealt with by
grants being made in all but two instances. ,
Applications for Further Relief. —No less than 500 applications “ for further assistance ’’
have been considered, several of the applicants having applied over and over again. These applica-
tions have been most carefully examined to enable your Executive Committee to judge whether any
new points have been adduced by the applicants which would warrant the granting of the “ further
assistance ’’ asked for. Wherever your Executive Committee were satisfied on the merits of the case,
further relief has been granted.
Many applicants seem to be imbued with the idea that the fund was intended “to support” and
not merely “ to assist.”
The total donations to the fund since its inception (2nd November, 1899) amount to £52,467 13s.
10d., and interest earned has amounted to £3674 18s. sd.

The Executive Committee have again to thank the authorities for their courteous assistance in investigating
applications from the country districts.
The place of Mr. Richard Teece, whose resignation from the Committee was received at last annual meeting
must, in compliance with By-law No. 4, be filled at this meeting.
As provided for under By-law No. 8, a statement of receipts and expenditure has to be published in the month
of July in each year.
This statement, duly signed by the Trustees, and audited b.v Mr. David Fell (C.A.A.), Public Accountant is
now placed before you, and, on adoption, will be published as required by the By-laws.
AUGUSTUS F. ROBINSON, Chairman of Executive Committee

Sydney, 9th July, 1903. J. RUSSELL FRENCH / and Trustees.
The centenary of Tasmania is to be cele-
brated on 12th Sept. On that day, 100 years ago,
Lieut. Bowen landed near Hobart, from Sydney,
with a party of soldiers and convicts and began
the settlement.
The Freetraders are bidding for the
woman’s vote at next Federal elections with the
dazzling promise of cheapened drapery and
things. Already the voice of the genteel bargain-
hunters is heard proclaiming that all women are
Freetraders.
It is somewhat of a burlesque on repre-
sentative institutions that, according to Joe
Chamberlain’s table of precedence, baronets—
Rupert Clarke, for instance—take precedence in
Commonwealth functions over members of either
Federal House, Ministers excepted, and over
State Ministers and Judges.
W. P. Crick’s duties as N.S.W. Minister
of Lands are wide-embracing. Take this, posted
up at the Necropolis : “No corpse can be removed
from one grave to another without written per-
mission from the Minister of Lands.”
A “Barmaids Abolition Bill” has been
introduced in Maoriland’s House of Reps, by a
private member. It provides that, after July,
1904, no female, other than the licensee, shall
serve drinks. If it becomes law, the vision behind
the bar passes away in less than 12 months, and
some uninteresting object with whiskers takes its
place.
From the letter of a prominent English-
man who knows Australia:
We are all busy thinking of our colonies and whether
they will join in Mr. Chamberlain’s preferential tariff
scheme. I have assumed that Australia, at any rate, will
not do anything of the kind. It would cripple her
nascent industries without giving any adequate return.
Mr. Chamberlain has broken up one political party and
now seems likely to spoil another. The difficulty is the
extraordinary weakness of Mr. Balfour. . . . The Tory
party is rent in twain and quite unable to appeal to the
country until it has settled its own differences.
“Ilex” writes:
Re the recent prosecution of Vic. State school teacher
Mead for disapproving of the Bent-Irvine Government:
In the “new revised programme of Public Instruction”
it is set down explicitly that teachers must, among other
things, hold forth to their pupils on “ Howthe wish of
the people becomes law,” “ How the laws are carried
out,” and the “ Duties of a citizen.” Moreover, he is to
treat his topic in such a manner as “to give children an
intelligent appreciation of the origin of and the reasons
for our leading national institutions.” And yet the
teacher isn’t to talk polities, and mustn’t speak disre-
spectfully of the Upper House.
“If we have not the pluck to undertake
our own defence we should blot out the stars from
the Commonwealth flag and substitute a miser-
able skulking dingo, or a kangaroo with its tail
between its legs.— A. Paterson, on the
Naval Tribute Bill.
Laseelles, the mighty Vic. Mallee land
grabber, has requisitioned for the inclusion of Pay-
trap and other big estates in his lease in addition
to the 35,000 acres now held at Tyrrell Downs, and
it is generally understood that the Deformed
Government is considering the matter in a
friendly spirit.
If honorable members of Australia’s 14
Houses of Legislature ceased calling each other
honorable, and took each other’s honorableness
for granted, it would save a good deal of time and
print. Surely the honorableness is sufficiently
conspicuous to stand by itself without all this
repetition.
The Imperial Government is about to
erect a naval prison on Garden Island at a cost of
£6OOO. The curious part of this quiet arrange-
ment is that the British Government builds the
prison while the local Government defrays the
expense of all the other buildings. The new
gaol is for the punishment of refractory seamen,
tried in hole-and-corner fashion and practically
condemned unheard, yet the prison will be within
the Commonwealth, but not subject to any super-
vision by the Federal or State authorities.
From London Truth :
We see no reason why Africa should be ransacked to
provide the mines with the cheap labor of crimped blacks,
nor do we believe that we ought to allow the mine-owners
to contract for gangs of Chinese in order to make money
without paying English workers a fair price, and to pre-
vent the “ serpent ” of trade unionism finding an entry
into the Transvaal. The working men of the United
States and Australia have put down their foot in regard
to cheap colored labor. The working men of South Africa
are against it, whilst in England, if a coal-owner were to
import Chinese labor to enable him to get better divi-
dends from rich coal seams, and to work poor ones with a
profit, any Government that did not very speedily step
in to prevent this lowering of trade-union prices would
have a very short shrift. What, I wonder, would be
thought if an English coal-owner were to say that coal-
owners should be allowed to employ black savages and
Chinese to work in coal-pits, but that all municipalities
must employ Englishmen at trade-union wages, in order
that the coal-owners should have a monopoly of cheap
labor? The real method of dealing with the labor
question in the Transvaal is to follow the example of the
United States, and to permit no laborers, whether white
or black, to enter the country if they are under any con-
tract to perform work when there. Workers imported
under contract are slaves, except in name. They are
picked up by some speculator, and they sign under-
takings about which they know nothing.


POLITICAL
POINTS
Record of N.S.W. Parliamentary session
at time of going to press : Five counts out, two
censure debates, one fight, and no work done.
N.S.W. Government is at last making a
move to regulate the sale and use of firearms, and
the annual pea-rifle mortality is thus likely to be
reduced.
Fremantle (W.A.) is seeking for powers
to construct and work municipal tramways. It
is about up to the Snow-diddled city of Adelaide
to follow suit. !
Long ago an ex-colleague of Kingston’s
said : “ Ybs, it’s rather hard work to get on with
Kingston sometimes. But it’s a lot harder work
to get on without him. ”
Almost every member who spoke on the
Address-in-Reply in S.A. Assembly kicked and
cuffed the Jenkins Iscariot Ministry. And Iscariot
merely smiled, and said “Thank you.” That
training as a Yankee book-canvasser was invalu-
able.
The Queensland Government proposes an
Act to suppress the sale of John Snorting’s paper
on Sunday. If it passes, the only result will be
that John’s paper will talk to Philp with extra
vigor on Saturday night, and the gain will be
dubious, to say the least.
Consider the words of one Dick, member
for Newcastle East, in N.S.W. Assembly: “It
looks as if it was absolutely hopeless for any
money for the ordinary loan services of the State
to be obtained.” This is surely a new term in
political economy; what are the “ordinary loan
services ” of any State ?
Page, Federal Member for the Maranoa
(Q.), exhibits vouchers showing that, in a recent
tour of his electorate (and he skipped about one-
tliird of it), his hotel expenses and coach travelling
amounted to JE23 2. The Democracy must tackle
such problems if it is ever to escape from repre-
sentation by the M‘Billions.
DESIGNED.
The Ministry separates, and Kingston goes his
own Road.
Governor Le Hun be had hardly settled
down in Adelaide and got his night-shirt unpacked,
when he was waited on by a deputation of the un-
employed. Once the unemployed only called, on
Ministers who might be able to give them- work;
now in a general, sorrowful, hopeless sort of way
they call on everybody. It isn’t a change for the
better. -
It has come at last. A meeting of the
Box Hill (Vic.) branch of the Kyabram Reform
League has' passed a resolution in favor of abolish-
ing payment of members and asking the Central
League to get a direct vote of the electors taken
on the subject at next election. But why bother
about the ejectors ? Why not pass a small measure
of that sort on to Irvine?
Westralia’s Governor, a retired vice-
admiral who never makes a speech without some
reference to “Britain’s grand old navy,” has
apparently taken warning from the recent protest
in Federal circles against State Governoi s’ inter-
ference with politics. He had arranged to deli ver
an address to members of the A.N. A. on “Britain’s
noble fleets,” but has now cried off.
The begging socialistic farmer again ! A
recent meeting of Wangaratta (Vic.) tobacco-
growers unanimously resolved that “the educa-
tion of tobacco-growers in improved methods of
growing, handling and curing leaf, hitherto
adopted by, the State Government, having been of
great value to the grower, should be continued.”
It also demanded a big subsidy to encourage it to
export its own tobacco. The meeting was enthu-
siastic as well as unanimous, and its approval of
the idea that the city artisan should be taxed to
pay for State assistance to the farmer was almost
violent in its intensity.
Haven Shiels, of Victoria, who knew
about so many superfluous public servants
loafing on the Treasury, becomes one of the
clan himself. He is to be Railways Minister at a
big salary, but railway construction isn’t to be in
his department, and railway management is to be
looked after by Commissioner Tait, and Shiels is
to be a loafing pauper with a sinecure.
There is one aspect of Bill’s dirt money
which is apt to be overlooked. Where are the
men who were sacked from the Fitzroy Dock to
make room for those who brought letters of
recommendation from a M.L.A. ? The man who
will crawl to a Minister or a member for a job
can’t always be accommodated without sacrificing
the probably better man who doesn’t crawl.
The Federal Capital Commission, ap-
pointed by the Australian Government, was not
so constituted as to command any confidence ; one
member in particular meant a discount of 75 per
cent, on the value of its conclusions. It is not at
all surprising, under the circumstances, that it puts
in the forefront of its recommendations two un-
suitable sites in Minister Bill Lyne’s electorate.
Brisbane Government press was howling
indignantly some weeks ago about the audacity of
Federal members who dared to meddle in State
politics and of State politicians who mixed up in
Federal affairs. In the midst of the denuncia-
tions State Premier Philp arose and announced
that he intended to fight the Federal Government
next election for all he was worth. The press
subsided.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor party
has degenerated woefully in its ideas of political
honesty since it professed to throw out Reid over
a far smaller financial misdoing than Owe’Sulli-
van’s Fitzroy Dock scandal. Reid unjustifiably
paid away a few hundreds, and the Labor party
ejected him. Owe’Sullivan threw away many
thousands, and the Labor party actually sup-
ported him.
The first Federal candidate on deck in
Tasmania is W. J. M‘Williams, of Launceston
Daily Twaddlkgrapii. He says :
I am strongly opposed to the lavish expenditure, actual
and proposed, of the Federal Government and Parliament,
and the extravagant manner in which the affairs of the
Federation are being conducted. lam an advocate of a
Revenue Tariff.
What a useful and impudent lie is that “lavish
Federal expenditure”—mainly based on the fact
that it was proposed to appoint five Federal
Judges to do law business for the whole Common-
wealth, with its 4,000,000 people, while Tasmania,
with less than a twentieth of the population,
thinks it needs three Supreme Court Judges for
its local business alone. Every dullard who can’t
think of anything else to say says he is “against
Federal extravaganoe.”
At recent manoeuvres of the British
squadron located in Australia the Wallaroo was
accidentally struck twice by torpedoes fired by
the Royal Arthur. Fortunately, they were only
practice torpedoes; if they had been fully loaded
.there would have been no more Wallaroo. An
official explanation will, doubtless, show that it
was nobody’s fault, but it gives an uneasy feeling
with regard to the British navy when, even in
moments of peace and calm, it shoots so wildly.
N.S.W. Public Service Board advertised
for two typists for the Arbitration Court the
other day, and, , without any appearance of shame,
printed its scandalous conditions. Applicants are
to pay a fee of 10s. to have their qualifications
tested. “But,” the shark-officialdom . goes, on,
“if, after examination, it is found that a person
permanently in the public service is qualified, he
will be preferred even if lie obtain less marks than
other candidates.” Surely the Commissioners
should at least have tested the employees in the
service before inviting outsiders to compete and
contribute half-sovereigns. Having invited out-
siders, surely merit should tell. Is it all merely a
dodge to raise money ?
About the must cheering cabled news
received from the Cold Country for some time
is the statement that the leadingEnglishunder-
writers have decided not to underwrite for some
time to come any more Australian loans. If this
decision is strictly adhered to, Premier Irvine is in
a tight place with the big loan that is now falling
due. It will be a dreadful thing if that prim
lawyer hp,s to tell the British Cohen that the loan
is renewed whether Cohen likes it or not. Also,
there are indications that a time is rapidly
approaching when Premier Irvine will be abso-
lutely driven to tax the wealthy landowners of
'Melbourne.
SHIELS’ SAVINGS. —THE VICTORIAN BUDGET POSITION.
“F.C.” : It is ever hard to overtake a lie,
but it is worth while starting in pursuit, anyhow.
Victorian employers already blather abroad
Works Inspector Davidson’s alleged pronounce-
ment against day labor as being 40 per cent,
dearer than the contract system. Davidson
said nothing of the sort. What he did say was in
a report on the Mount Fatigue roads, started as
unemployed relief works, and was to the effect
that the system adopted—piece-work, not day-
labor —cost 40 per cent, more than contract prices
would have done.
South Australia’s new Governor, LeHunte,
spoke thus at an Adelaide Salvarmy function :
England always welcomed those who found it hard to
stay in their own land, from whence they were driven by
persecution. . . . England always took people on trust,
and it would be well if Australia always looked on people
with trust, and not with suspicion.
The Englishman, who decides that four-fifths of
the people of his own blessed empire can’t be
trusted with any self-governing powers whatever,
is always quite sure that he doesn’t look on any-
body with suspicion.
At the Argos’ instigation Vic. Minister
Carrieron, a dour Scotchman with no humor of his
own, played off a beautiful joke upon his life-long
political enemy, David Syme. The Argus gravely
announced that Cameron was recommending the
Cabinet to negotiate for the purchase of the View
Hill, Killara, and Dairy estates, which were all
elaborately described, with details as to the excep-
tional railway facilities, and the wasteful profusion
of railway stations in the middle of the properties.
The humor lies in the fact that all three belong to
David Syme, who has strongly advocated compul-
sory resumption of big estates for closer settle-
ment, though it doesn’t necessarily follow that he
wanted his own estates resumed.
“ Q.” : Judging by some of his remarks to
the Ballarat branch of the A.N.A., the Cool Calm
Cuss, who is afraid to tax the wealthy landowners
of Melbourne, is hard at work trying to screw up
his courage to go in for a compulsory land re-
sumption scheme. It is all very well to talk
about the thousands of acres of well-watered, fer-
tile lands which are at present carrying sheep and
must, in the future, carry men ; but why doesn’t
the cool, strong bounder do something? While
he talks at banquets his agricultural population is
streaming away solely because it can’t get land to
settle on. Already Victoria is about to lose one
Federal member because of its diminishing
importance, as compared with other States, and
there are other humiliations to follow.
Tt> a deputation of Sydney unemployed
which waited on him with a request that he
should help them to find work in America (!),
U.S. A. Consul Baker took some pains to ex-
plain the severely restrictive nature of the U.S.
immigration laws. No person is admitted to
the States who is likely to become a pauper
through disease or otherwise, who is under
contract, who has ever been in a poor-house or in
receipt of alms, or who has not 30 dollars in his or
her possession. With all these restrictions the
U.S. attracts a huge number of immigrants
yearly; but then the country can offer them work.
When the Australian tariff is built on American
lines we will be able to do the same. The un-
employed and their friends would do well to
encoiirage Protection in Australia—to make work
here instead of deluding themselves with wild-cat
emigration schemes.
Jenkins Iscariot includes barrage works
at the mouth of the Murray among his public
works proposals. The thing has been talked
about for 40 years or more ; the object is simply
to dam back the sea-water, and so im-
prove the country with frontages to the
lower river and shallow lakes near its
mouth. But it's a small scheme to talk
about so long; something less than
£60,000 would do it all. Jenkins rightly
proposes to apply the betterment prin-
ciple to the job; so, locally, it isn’t
quite so popular as it was before they
went into details.
If the N.S.W. Labor party had thrown
out Owe’Sullivan and Co. over the Fitzroy Dock
disgrace, the new Premier would have depended
on it for his majority, for it still holds the
Parliamentary balance. That would have been
policy. A very few more Owe’Sullivan revela-
tions, and the direct Government following will
disintegrate so badly that the next Premier won’t
depend on the Labor party for his majority; lie
will have enough followers without it. ' That will
be just muddle. Moreover, if the Labor party
had quitted Owe’Sullivan the other day it would
have had the joy of seeing Carruthers eat his
proud assurance that he would never hold office
and be dependent on the Labor vote. Another
year or so and Joseph may be able to keep his
promise by accident, and then his pride .and
bounceableness will be insufferable.
The way Irvine is shuffling the Ministerial
cards looks as if Vic. Government is to be a one-
man affair. Lately the cool calm person told
Mines Minister Cameron that he would attend
personally to all important matters in Cameron’a
Department. Much flattery by the dailies has
made Irvine mad enough to reckon he can run
the whole show on his own ; but he hasn’t
Seddon’s endurance, and already cracking sounds,
like the rustle of trodden-on locusts, can be heard,
as the small dry man gives at his oilless joints
with the dead-weight and ballast of portfolios.
■Not only did the British Privy Council
decide in favor of Kingston, in upholding the law
under which ships’ stores must pay duty when
the vessel arrives at its first Australian port, or
must be sealed up and remain so till the vessel
leaves its last Australian port ; but it threw dirt
on the whiskers of the Australian Foreign-trade
press in the process of doing so. For in addition
to deciding that the law held good, it pointed out
that the law was a reasonable, ordinary law,
almost exactly similar to one which had been in
force in Britain for thirty years past. So all the
lies of the importers’ papers about Kingston’s
novel, incredible, and revolutionary device and
his unheard-of act of tyranny fall through
Kingston was only following British precedent 1
There was a Man who had a Relation'
in such Reduced Circumstances as to be almost
a Poor Relation. The Man had Loaned him
some money. The Poor Relation had a Pup-
of the Bull Dog Breed. Said the Man:
“That is a very Little Dog. Put him away
and I will Lend you a Bigger Dog. You;
can find its Tucker, but I will hand it to-
him, for Dogs don’t Bite the Man that Feeds
them. And, as you Owe me Money, the Bailiff
may have to be put in Some Day—it would be
awkward to have a Big Pup of the Bull Dog
Breed in a Poor Relation’s Back-yard. I will
only Take Away My Dog if I Want him
somewhere else. Meanwhile he will be an Orna-
ment to your place, and your girls can Play with-
him.” And the Poor Relation said—well, it
looks as if he were going to say “Yes,” for he is-
very Badly Advised.
Premier See says that he is very sorry that
the N.S.W. Government ever interfered with the
Sydney Rocks resumptions at all. Yet if the
business: had been better engineered there would
have been no cause for grief. It should have-
been enacted that payment for the resumed
‘jJroperties wohld be made in 3J per cent.
Government, securities. There would have been
complaints of “ tyranny ” no doubt, but they
would have died away. Then there would have-
been no unseemly struggles and breakdowns in
the London money market, and the debt would'
have been mostly a local instead of a foreign
liability. Then there should have been a sinking
fund for repayment of the debt; there should
have been no cooking of the accounts and no-
silly grandiose Owe‘Sullivan ; and £250,000 a year-
should have been supplied out of revenue for re-
construction purposes. The want of these things
is the cause of the sorrow.
Queensland Government, which still sticks-
to its scarcity-of-white-labor myth when referring
to the sugar industry, has over
200 men employed on relief?
works, and hundreds
more are waiting.
