Another Cure by
VITADATIO
POVERTY OF BLOOD AND
GENERAL WEAKNESS.
428 Ohurch-Btreet, Richmond,
16th August, 1902.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Dear Sir, —About six years ago I became very ill, suffer-
ing from poverty of blood and general weakness. My
medical adviser ordered me to the Ararat Hospital. I
remained there for one month, then left, removing to
Ballarat, where I became much worse and very weak.
About four years ago I returned to Melbourne, and
eventually, became so weak I had to take to my bed, and
remained.there eight weeks. Having read a great deal
about Webber’s VITADATIO, I made up my mind to give
it a trial. The first bottle upset me very much, and I
laid it aside for a fortnight, then I called to see you at
Bourke-street, when you strongly advised me to continue
it, stating that it would certainly cure me; so I per-
severed, and after I had taken the fifth bottle I began to
feel much stronger, and, by continuing, my health was
completely restored. It is now three years since I took
the last bottle, and I can truthfully say I would have
been in my grave long ago had it not been for VITA-
DATIO. I can recommend it to anyone suffering as I did,
and hand you this to make use of as you please for the
benefit of other sufferers. I will be pleased to answer
any questions, either by letter or personally, at above
address.
Yours faithfully,
MARJORIE SMITH.
I have known Mrs. Smith for a number of years, and
can truthfully certify that her statement is true in every
particular. She was very low and weak when she com-
menced taking VITADATIO, and the effect was mar-
vellous. Being my next-door neighbour, I saw her daily
until she was quite well. I consider her case a wonderful
cure.
MARY FRY.
61 O’Grady-street, Albert Park.
For further particulars,
8. A. PALMER ,
Head Office: Clarendon Street N. ,
South Melbourne.

That magnificent thing Private Enterprise,
which was going to start nearly two years ago and
immediately build electric trams in Adelaide, and
which said then that it had all the money ready,
has again been heard from. The latest news is
that “the whole of the engineering details have
been perfected and the exact scheme prepared
which will be laid before the various tram com-
panies as soon as the papers are to hand.” As
for the money, it doesn’t seem to be to hand, and
nothing is said about beginning work. After all
this delay it appears that some bits of paper are
ready.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Wheat State turfdom generally threatens anni-
hilation of S.A.J.C. as the ruling body of that State
because of many alleged evidences of bias and vacillation,
also through invidious position occupied by S.A.J.C. with
regard to Owners and Trainers’ Association. Existence
of last-named was due to fact of present governing body
repeatedly refusing to give Port Adelaide R.O. another
day’s racing, from which position (as well as one or two
similar ones since) the new organisation and public opinion
compelled S.A.J.C. to clamber down. Proposed by Mr. W.
Robertson (14 years chairman of Adelaide R;C., and at
present steward of S.A.J.C.) that a tribunal unassociated
with any racing club be elected by ballot of members of
all registered clubs, or by dividing the State into three
sections and having representatives from each on some
equitable basis. Another nail' in S.A.J.C.’s coffin was its
refusal, without explanation, to sanction A.R.C.’s appli-
cation for a week’s postponement of latter’s two days’
Grand National meet, the idea being that leppers might
go Adelaidewards after V.A.T.O. gathering, especially as
A.R.C. had added lOOsovs. to prize-money.
* * » *
The annual registration of the “ bookies ” by the
A.J.0., Sydney, fell due recently, and, judging by the
horde of applicants for registration in N.S.W., followers
of sport will have plenty “layers of odds” to contend
with. There are but two classes of people who appear to
thrive nowadays—the “bookie” and the swindling im-
porter—both by questionable practices. If gambling is
allowed to flourish, it is time the N.S.W. Government
introduced a Totalisator Bill on the same lines as those
now in force in other States—as a protection for the
gambler and as a source of revenue.
• * # *
The late Tommy Wilson, of Dowling Forest,
materialised at Hobart (Tasmania). In his 17th year he
went to sea. Then he made tarpaulins for £1 a day at
Geelong ; and later, at Ballarat, dug £l5OO worth of gold
out of one hole. Then back to the sea as second mate of
the barque Glencoe, till he took a turn at coach-driving
between Geelong and Ballarat, and becoming a livery -
stable keeper, drifted into horse training. Tommy’s
famous frieze coat, worn off and on for more years than
anyone can remember, will be much missed at Flemington
coming spring.
* * * *
Maoriland House of Representatives, by 34
votes to 14, has squelched the Totalisator Abolition Bill—
the instrument with which the combination of howlers
and “ bookies ” hoped to wreck the system of moderate
and legalised gambling. The League will now crawl back
into its hole, and reflect that its boast of support
from Premier Seddon and almost all the members of the
House looks very like a record lie.
■■* * * •
One of the Maoriland Mo fraternity, fined for
booking bets in the street, pleaded that he was merely
receiving subscriptions towards the burial of two poor
children who had died from measles, or something. No
witnesses were called, however, to prove that they had
been backing “ dead uns.”
* * * *
This happened at a Sydney suburban ground
recently. Horse-owner carpeted for alleged inconsistent
running, and “ disqualified for 12 months.” “ I expected
this,” said the owner, looking at one of the tribunal,
“ you told certain people you were going to do it.”
“ You’re a liar,” quoth the steward. “You’re another,"
retorted the horse-owner. Tribunal, in'conclave few
minutes later, made the disqualification “life.” Next
morning respectable daily press announced that the big
penalty was inflicted for “abusive” language. Most
puzzling thing in the connection is where any offence
came in. At previous meeting same owner and his nag’s
rider had been cautioned that if anything suspicious
occurred again they would be punished. ‘Race imme-
diately following saw the horse win. What more did these
sapient officials want?
* * m *
Six residents of Gundagai, said to be all poor
men, drew Lowland Chief in Ta t’s. sweep on G.N.
Hurdles and came in for £2OOO. They pooled £5 each and
sent £3O to L.o.’s jock (Wheeler).
* * * * •
V.R.C. annual report reveals a falling off of
£ll,OOO. While Spring meet of 1901 brought a profit of
£5608, last year’s gathering resulted in £3303 loss, and
though Grand National, New Year’s Day and October
meetings were profitable, the Coronation, Autumn, April
and Prince of Wales Birthday gatherings showed short-
ages.
* • * *
A.J.O.’s new rule compelling inclusion of
jumping events in Sydney suburban club programmes is
balm to most Victorian cross-country jocks, who, ’tis
alleged, have had a parlous time for long, mainly because
they don’t always get paid unless their mounts get home
first. V.R.O. rule insists that fees shall be lodged at
scale, but jocks frequently have to refund to owners the
whole or half the money. Stated that front-rank rider
Carter went to India mainly because there was nothing
in Melbourne for him unless he rode for the sake of
riding on the off-chance of winning.
* * » *
How they do things at Broken Hill. Hurdle
race, Wednesday last week, caused something of a mix
up. Said that nearing home turn Listnaghar ran off but
still kept in the race, and at last jump again shirked
bringing Avymore and Pat with him. Judge declared in
favor of Greygown, The Christian, and Coalbox, as named,
then he altered his ruling, giving the places to Listnaghar,
Pat and Greygown. Protests from Greygown’s owner
against Listnaghar and Pat, and from Pat’s owner against
Listnaghar followed. That night stewards dismissed
both kicks and upheld judge’s second decision. Grey-
gown party are appealing to the A.J.O. —and small
wonder 1
* * * »
Color-Sergeant Davis, who with 311 won the
King’s match concluded at Bisley, Saturday, annexed
same honor 10 years ago. Nearest Australian was H.
Motton (Q.), 38th on the list (with 289) ; he came in for
£l2 and a badge. First in this event is entitled to £250
presented by the King, a gold medal, and a badge.
Prizes total 600 and aggregate £2420. Each of the first
100 gets a badge. Last year’s winner turned up in Lieu-
tenant Johnson, with 307 of a possible 344 ; then most
successful Australian was W. Todd (Vic) who (45th on the
list with an aggregate of 286) secured £l2 and a badge.
♦ * * *
Cabled announcement that young North Syd-
ney shot R. O. Dawson annexed Duke of Cambridge
match at Bisley didn’t surprise much, as several big
Australian prizes for long-range shooting have come his
way. Said that he placed 10 bull’s eyes in a 14in. square.
Range was 900yds., and his total (50) the possible. Last
year saw 433 compete for this, and an accumulation of
48 points won it—Carter (Vic.) filling second place with 47.
* * m *
Australia’s second win in succession of Kolapore
Cup at Bisley last week caused the spilling of much
liquid in Australian rifledom, especially when the ob-
stacles to be overcome before the team was sent ’Ome were
recalled. Last year’s win saw Australia top all previous
figures with 770 of a possible 840 ; this time they have
gone 1 better. Members of the team, A. Carter (Vic.)
and G. Howitt (S.A.), secured the possible at 200yds.
G. Bradley’s (Q.) aggregate (102 of a possible 105) is the best
compilation in any team match during Bisley’s history.
Sergeant-Major Whitehead (Vic.) scored highest last year
with 101; next came A. Carter (Vic.), 99, and J. T. Lake
(S. A.), 98. Strangely enough, Carter notched 99 again
this time, while his fellow Victorian, W. Ison, put a
similar number together. Of the Australians’ last 16 shots
a year ago 15 were bull’s-eyes. Then Southern Rhodesia
was second, Canada third, and Bull-land fourth. This
time Bull-land was second, Canada third, and Natal
fourth.
* * * *
“K.” : Champion wrestler, Buttan Singh, is a
hawker, and his beat is in Vic. Western District, where
he runs rather a good turn-out—a big waggonette and
two horses. Buttan, with an eye to business, has
“ Champion Wrestler ” painted on the waggon tilt, and
during the temporary eclipse of his game, after his first
defeat by Gunga Brahm, he had to endure much pointed
satire in connection with the inscription. The wrestler
is a big, heavily-built man, and keeps himself in constant
training by lifting a couple of huge stones that he carts
about in his waggon, and by wrestling with his mate,
who is a fair performer. Early the other morning I saw
Buttan carrying a small tree on his shoulder to replenish
his fire. The tree was a good load for two average men.
Buttan consumes vast quantities of butter, milk, and
mutton when in full training for a contest; and no doubt
the major part of his success is due to the constant and
systematic method in which he prepares himself. By the
way, a countryman of Buttan’s told me that both the late
wrestling matches were fakes, in spite of the apparent
ferocity of Buttan during the last one.
* * * *
Barring a few barefaced tricks, Kensington
(Sydney) Coursing Club’s three days’ meeting last week,
during which four stakes were run off, supplied good
sport. Victorian-bred Vicar of Wakefield (Goldsmith—
The Witch) gave nothing else a hope in Novice Stakes
General Booth—lnnisfail dog Golden Gate (fresh from
Victorian successes), in Second Trial Stakes, scored with
a deal to the good. Speedy puppy Cannongate romped
away from everything till a broken toe, sustained in the
semi-final, caused his party to suggest a division to
Ohaddesley’s owner; though Cannongate would probably
have won the run-off. Feature of the meeting was that
one absolute winner (Vicar of Wakefield) and two
dividers (litter brothers Cannongate and Crossbow) came
from kennels of ex-Victorian trainer Greg Keighrey, who
has been remarkably successful in Sydney.
* * * *
Failure of officials at Kensington 0. C. to do
the right thing in at least three instances has given the
sport a shock. The cases were very glaring, and one
happening was foreshadowed at card call the previous
evening. Another mistake for which no excuse can he
offered is allowing dogs almost as small as whippets to
start; one competitor last week only scaled 361b5., and
should have been sent baok.
* * * *
“ David Harum ” : The walking contest craze
has got into the blood of the Adelaide public now, and
the people are as enthusiastic over it as they were over
Sandow developers six months ago. All, ‘from beery
stockbrokers to five year old school-children, are
looking to their legs and wind. Not only have men taken
it up—women, too, are having waddling contests. I have
a young lady friend, who works in a draper’s shop, whom
I take home every night. She is going in for a walking
match, so I have to pace her home. Blank, one of the
portly stockbrokers in our street, goes for a two-mile
walk every morning before breakfast now, and Dash, a
ditto, is also training. Has been living for three weeks
on half his usual allowance of whisky, but tells me he
intends to make up for it after a certain race is over.
Most business men in our district have given up riding
in cars; any morning between 8.30 and 9 one can see
them, each one endeavoring to reach town before the
others. Tramcars are empty save for invalids, cripples,
and women with babies. When I take my girl out
she won’t go to the theatre, or sit down on a seat in
the park—she’ll only walk. Was sitting outside a pub in
the hills last Saturday, and saw a commercial traveller
friend, wearing little more than a silk shirt; come pant-
ing along the road. “ Hullo, Ted,” said I, “ come and
have a drink?” “N-n-n-oo,” he spluttered (it was the
first time I had known him to refuse a drink) “ can’t stop.
I’m in a walking match." He met me a couple of days
after, and reminded me I had asked him to have a drink !
The exercise is good, so is the sport, but not for long—
already bribery is being used.
* * * *
Advance reports of wonderful physique of M.L.
football forwards haven’t been quite borne out. One or
two are big, powerful chaps, but generally they are of
the compact, speedy class, and range from 12J to 13£st.
M.L. combination in Sydney just now includes
some veterans as far as battles are concerned. Fanning
has a record of 54 provincial battles; Udy, 47 ; Duncan,
45 ; Wallace, 35 ; Armstrong, 33 ; and Stead, 31.
* * * *
Second M.L.-N.S.W. football rough at Sydney
Cricket Ground (Saturday) was swum under heavy rain
in a lake of mingled mud and water. A greasy ball made
passing a farce, and as M.L. men usually shine in that
department they probably suffered most. Dribbling and
open kicking were all that could be indulged in, though
the tendency to lie down and slide, made even that un-
reliable. Play, if not high class, was certainly amusing—
to the men in the grandstands. Forwards all round
shaped more than capably, and in the scrums N.S.W.
did better than before, but visitors were still slightly
their superiors, playing more concertedly. In the loose
the home combination had the opposition at fault, and
N.S.W. backs were sure at catching and handling, which
was surprising, considering the Maorilander is more
accustomed to slush. N.S.W.’s defence was at least
equal to M.L.’s. It was with the ball at toe the Maori-
landers got to the home goal-line, but N.S.W. picked up
more accurately, and did better at passing, which was,
however, never reliable on either side. Combined
forward rushes and impregnability of defence both ways
proved remarkable under the circumstances, and, in this
connection, Wallace (M.L.) and Maund (N.S.W.), the two
full-backs, did some fine things, with the result that
neither side secured a try—the only score was three in
M.L.’s favor—a penalty goal. Winners were very lucky,
for, as far as actual play went, the match should have
been a draw. Wood (five-eighth) provided a fine game
for M.L., and perhaps (as in the first match) did better
than any other on his side. Contended that visitors
suffered through absence of Duncan and R. M‘Gregor,
but N.S.W. was without Wickham and Finlay, which at
least equalised matters.
* * * *
The necessity of a uniform code of rules was
strongly emphasised at the recent inter-State College
football fixture at Adelaide. Victorian rules allow for
kicking in the ruck, and also kicking out of a player’s
hands—both of which tend to dangerous play. South
Australian rules penalise such modes of play, and,
on the other hand, permit the wearing of gloves on wet
days, which isn’t allowed in Victoria. The visiting Vic-
torian team, therefore, played at a disadvantage in every
way, and didn’t take at all kindly to the adverse condi-
tions of the game.
* * * *
The Amateur Athletic Association of Aus-
tralasia is taking a vote of the whole association on the
question—“ Is an Aboriginal an amateur? ” Really the
question is—“ Will we condescend to compete with
blackfellows ? ” The definition of an amateur is plain
enough, and if the members honestly answer the conun-
drum in its literal sense, it can only have the one
answer, and that is that a black man is an amateur
under the same conditions which make anybody
else an amateur. Whether the blackfellow is to
be allowed to take an equal part in athletic sports
(and the natural objection to our black brudder, notice-
able in one or two well-known cases, is quite understand-
able) is a fair question, but the A.A.A.A. is shirking a
straight-out and honest solution by coolly branding the
abo. as a “ professional ” in its query put to the
plebiscite.
* * * *
Amateur athletics in Sydney are practically
dead. Though the N.S.W.A.A.A. meets frequently and
deliberates solemnly there hasn’t been a blow struck this
season beyond one meeting and an open handicap during
“half time” of first M.L.-N.S.W. football match, both
promoted by North Sydney club. The formation of dis-
trict clubs a year or two since gave the game a fillip
for a while, but enthusiasm and interest petered out
quickly, and things are now moribund. Evidence of
how flattened out the game is was discovered in open
handicap (£m.) referred to. Starters numbered 27, but ere
a quarter mile had been negotiated they dwindled deplor-
ably and presented a depressing spectacle.
* * * *
Patrons of Sydney Cricket Ground are to be
denied in future the added attraction, generally a cycle
or foot race, run at half-time during the football season.
In the interval of the first Maoriland match some two
score half-clad and shivering youths ran a 880-yards’
handicap round and round the arena. To the surprise of
the Athletic Association, which meant to perpetrate a
similar event at the return match, the CrickeD Ground
trustees flatly refused their sanction. Not for propriety’s
sake in the interests of the ladies’ reserve, or for a desire
to prevent the contestants risking pneumonia. Simply
the bar in the members’ stand. Unlike in summer, when
the thirsty pavilionite can, at the termination of an over,
invite his pals to “ Cumanavanother,” the football fol-
lower can only spare the time for a drink during the
lOmin. spell. The bar-tenders lamented loudly at the
first match that the great majority stuck glued to their
seats and speculated not in the bar-coupon. Also was
added the wail from the stands, whence came the reports
that the young men, instead of suggesting tea and
scones, betrayed a sudden and absorbing interest in the
“880,” and their fair companions had perforce to show
an equal anxiety in the chances of the leading ped. in
the shortest of pants and with no singlet to speak of. So
the trustees have decided to err no more in this direction.
• * * *
The post mortem held on M.L. wrestler Harry
Dunn disclosed a very bad state of things internally. The
operator stated that the stomach was four times the size
of a normal stomach.
* * * *
Fight between heavyweight pugs—black Felix
(13.6) and woodchopper Bill Heckenberg (13.1)—at
Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday night, brought the first big
attendance for months. Sharp on time Heckenberg
landed a left hook that lodged a little too high, other-
wise the match might have ended there and then. The
lathy negro ducked and clung for recuperative purposes
while Heckenberg slashed and lunged—mostly at vacancy,
though occasionally powerful rights narrowly missed
their mark. The pair stood up to each other and fought
vigorously, Felix rarely resorting to footwork, though
’twas anticipated he would take full advantage of the
22ft. square- Excitement was at high pitch, and it looked
either’s fight until, while staggering from the force of
two successive left jabs, the white got a heavy right chop
on the jaw (beautifully timed) that floored him for 9secs.
He would have been beaten then but for “corners.’’
Twice Heckenberg had the colored man rattled, but
Felix’s clever clinging and ducking enabled him to pull
through. Ultimately, as they broke away from a clinch
Felix hurled a right, which landed ’tween chin and cheek-
bone, and the scramble was over—the giant axeman lay
inert and oblivious to the count. Felix made more
friends than ever—not that he has advanced in skill, but
against Heckenberg he evidenced a zest for fight pre-
viously supposed to be foreign to him. Likely that
’Murkan Scanlan and the black may be matched now.
* * * *
The quality of Westralian pug. material.
Ancient Jim Richardson, who couldn’t make the sem-
blance of a show against Soldier Thompson in Sydney,
squelched Frank Anderson at Her Majesty’s, Kalgoorlie,
recently for W. A. middle supremacy. Thing lasted four
rounds, and Richardson won from the jump. Ten years
ago, before going to S’Africa and the old country,
Richardson might have beaten the best his weight in the
continent, and they were much better then than now.
« • * *
Bruise between Jim Barr (who cut even with
lightweight champion Bob Turner over a year ago) and
Paddy Martin, at Sydney National S.C. last week, was
bright. Second round was Barr’s, and the third very
much his—Martin dazed and oscillating ; but Paddy came
round soon, and, Barr’s power petering out, things went
against him afterwards till the seventh round closed,
when he gave it best. Martin fought better than for a
long time, but fell weak early—possibly through not
having had too much luxury in the way of living lately,
and want of ways and means to prepare.
* * * *
There is a little flutter among prominent Can-
terbury (M.L.) bike amateurs, the league, in accordance
with one of its rules, having called upon them to produce
their trophies. These “ much-prized” specimens of metal
ware are usually melted into coin nowadays, but similar
trophies can always be borrowed to satisfy the hungry
gaze of inquisitive league officials.
* # * *
Entries for the Goulburn to Sydney road-race
closed, yesterday, at the office of the Dunlop Pneumatic
Tyre Company, 150 Clarence-street, Sydney, and the
great interest being taken in this classic road event is
fully demonstrated in the large number of entries
received. Over 100 names have been handed to Mr. E. J.
Branagan, general secretary of the N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen, and the Official Handicapper, Mr. Williams,
has commenced his arduous task of compiling the handi-
caps, which will be published on Thursday, 23rd inst.*
The effectual settlement of the N.S.W. League
■ of Wheelmen dispute is as far off as ever, and, whereas
1 one alleged governing body has granted permission to the
riders who wish to compete in the forthcoming Dunlop
road race, the “ council ” of the League has notified each
rider entered that the race isn’t sanctioned by the
alleged League, and any rider competing in the event
shall be disqualified by an alleged rule for 12 months for
taking part in an alleged unregistered race. The piggish-
ness of each side in refusing to abide by majority rule
renders the matter extremely hard to settle ; and the
outcome seems to be that each side will ultimately run
a separate show, which will either wreck the sport or
cause wheelmen to curse and abjure both parties.
* * * *
Cable man gave personnel of eight acceptors for
P .F. Warner’s combination to visit Australia, and also
mentioned terms—expenses for amateurs, and ditto for
professionals with £3OO each added for latter and a bonus
should profit result. Promoting Marylebone 0.0. wants
to make nothing out of the venture. Hirst, Rhodes,
Arnold, and Braund, with Bosanquet, Fielder and Relf to
help, are among the possibles.
* * * *
Good judges have always doubted whether
Rhodes, who needs a wicket to help him, would do much
good in Australia ; but, now that he is at last coming, he
seems to be losing even his English form. Up to the
middle of June, with an unusually wet season, his average
of 21 per wicket did not put him in the first 30 on the
bowling list. Braund was even lower down. The first
four were Hirst (21 for 8 apiece !), Hargreave (51 for 11),
Blythe and J. T. Hearne. Fry, Jessop, John Gunn,
Perrin and Dowson headed the batting averages, ranging
from 76 to 56.
» * * »
Some billiard enthusiast went to the trouble of
keeping averages, breaks, and times of Stevenson’s best
displays during his recent Sydney visit, and comparing
them with those of John Roberts opposed to Weiss (also
in Sydney) three years ago, and discloses the following :
—Highest breaks : Roberts, 500, 340, 272, 266 ; Steven-
son, 500, 416, 347, 336. Highest averages : Roberts
(14,000), 38'44 ; Stevenson (10,000), 39'11. Averages for
whole day : Roberts, 54'67 ; Stevenson, 87'68. Speed :
Roberts, 500 in 32mins. ; Stevenson, 500 in 29mins.

How the pigeon murderers prosper ! N.S.W.
Gun Club has just bought its Botany grounds for £lOOO,
and yet shows a credit balance of over £5OO on the year's
business.
It is generally understood—principally on the
strength of a vague scare got up by the S.M.
Herald and the Telegraph— that N.S.W. is at
present losing population by exoess of departures
over arrivals. On this subject, the Telegraph
announced last Monday :
Never before in the history of N.S.W. has the emigra-
tion exceeded the influx of people.
This, it is needless to say, is the Telegraph’s
usual lie, designed presumably to imply that this
sort of thing only happens under Federation. In
1893,1895, 1896, 1897, 1900 and 1901 N.S.W. lost
population by excess of departures. The figures
appear on page 536 of Coghlan’s latest issue of
the “ Seven Colonies,” and are so well known that
the Telegraph’s statement looks like premedi-
tated inaccuracy.
The Bulletin received an invitation the other
day from Messrs. Swift and Company to call and taste the
1898 vintage champagne of M. Charles Heidsieck. The
result was so eminently satisfactory, that the moderate
drinkers of this paper hope to have the same invitation
regularly once a month at least; the oftener, the better.*
How strongly Kingston has impressed his
personality on public and press is shown by the
appreciations of his work everywhere volunteered.
It is as though the man were just dead, instead of
being still a living, fighting force. Probably the
finest “notice” he got was from a man of dia-
metrically opposite way of political thought—
Scarlett, Yic. Chamber of Manufactures’ Presi-
dent: “He is not a man to move hurriedly.
He waits always until he fully understands the
matter before him, then his decisions are rapid
and final. He is a man of indomitable will-power,
yet withal very gentle. He is a still, .strong
man. ... As first Federal Customs Minister
the work before him was enormous, and he knew
no more about it than the ordinary man outside.
But he was not daunted,... and soon made himself
master of all the details. . . . Even his severest
critic must say that lie tried to do everything for
the best, and that he at least treated all alike.
He has done the lion’s share of the work of
the department, and it will be a comparatively
easy matter now to conduct its affairs since he
has started the machinery, got it going, and
made a deep groove for his successor to drop
into.” And his subordinates are equally appre-
ciative. He made them work, but he never
spared himself; and for their blunders his
treatment was- ever more generous than just.
Things should sort themselves out shortly, at the
elections if not before ; and the right billet will
then be open to Kingston—that of Prime Minister
and leader of the Australian Democratic party.
THE WALKING MATCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The clerks of have arranged for a walking
match.” —News item in any paper.
The outward course was ten short miles,
And then ten long miles back ;
And ten short clerks and ten long clerks
Stood ready on the track.
And Robert Jinks, of Jones and Co.,
The favorite was he;
And when the signal came he took
The front place manfully.
And when they dipped beneath the hill
His friends remarked with pride,
How Robert Jinks increased his lead
With every mighty stride.
And when they passed the flve-mile post,
Although-with main-and might
The others walked, they saw with grief
That Jinks was out of sight.
And when the clerks had all come home,
The crowd upon the flat
Saw X. Y. Jones, of Jones and Co.,
A-leaping on his hat.
For Robert Jinks had not returned,
He’s somewhere walking yet;
If you can tell his whereabouts
A good reward you’ll get.
And X. Y. Jones, of Jones and Co.,
Whose business has gone smash,
Would like to meet with Robert Jinks
Who walked off with his cash.

The only modification so far secured of
the Naval Tribute Bill and its accompanying
arrangements is the withdrawal of the proposal
that the poor little vessels which Australia already
owns shall be sold. Practically, the effect of
this is slight—the boats are almost useless. Sen-
timentally, it is important, for Premier Barton
has been forced to admit that Australia can own
warships of its own; and though they are poor
ones now they may be better later on when a
Premier is in power who has not succumbed to
the fascinations of Joseph of Birmingham.

Orders now received for “Such is Life ! ”
The Bulletin’s new book. By Tom Collins.
Ready Ist. August. 4s. 6d.
J. L. Dow (Vic.) writes :
A sore-headed friend of The Bulletin, recently, is not
pleased with my accounts in the Age of the new wheat-
growing development in the Victorian Western district.
Nevertheless, it is quite true that several hundreds of
sturdy wheat-growers, who have been driven out of the
drought-stricken Mallee, are down there growing big
crops, and, for the privilege of doing so, paying the land-
lords up to 10s. per acre per annum rental. The monopoly
that enables these landowners to extract this charge from
needy workers was conferred upon them by previous
selectors, who conveyed their lands to them for a con-
sideration. Of course, dummies and land-pimps, who
take up selections under liberal land laws for the purpose
of selling to the squatter, don’t grow crops. That’s not
their business.
The point of the above isn’t very clear. If Mr.
Dow means that because the Western Victoria
landlords built up big estates by buying out a
dead generation of selectors, and have proved by
their rapacity that they shouldn’t have been
allowed to do so, therefore no one should complain
of their rapacity, or object to the landlord system,
or object to the glorification of the landlord
system, or try to undo the evil that has been
done—well, if Mr. Dow means that, it would be
better to say so as clearly as possible.

Recently the unemployed at Hillston
(N.S.W.) refused to accept charity from the State
and demanded relief works instead. Well, the
two are practically the same thing, and it is time
that the fact was recognised. Relief works, when
they become chronic, as they are in Australia, are
simply charity, thinly disguised by the pretence
of doing something useless which is paid for out
of loans. Calling them relief works gives the
State an excuse for not paying the bill out of
revenue ; also it lends a bogus dignity to the busi-
ness which prevents the public being so thoroughly
ashamed of it as it should be ; and it prevents the
pauper statistics showing the real state of the
country. The facts of the case are that there are
.1,400,000 people in N.S.W. and only legitimate
employment enough to support about 1,200,000;
that unless we start to manufacture about

Sozne people seem to live to make work
for lawyers. A man at Kadina (S.A.) has been
invoking the Supreme Court to look into his case.
He was sued at Port Broughton, some miles
away, for an alleged debt, and objected on the
ground that his own local court at Kadina was
the proper place to try the action. The magis-
trates “ passed ” on this point, and gave a verdict
for plaintiff. Defendant journeyed to Adelaide,
went before a judge (with counsel), and obtained
a stay of proceedings for 14 days. Having got it,
he slept on it peacefully for a month or so, till
the bailiffs came in in pursuance of the verdict,
only to retire on finding no goods distrainable.
Then this enthusiast went before the Pull Court
(with counsel on both sides) to ask for an order
to prohibit the local court from doing what it had
already done! And when the three judges
solemnly assembled found that the amount
originally in dispute was £4 7s. 7d, they talked
very candidly to him and to his lawyer, and
talked still more frankly on hearing of the bailiffs
official report that the litigant wasn’t worth even
that sum. The civil law has no great fondness
for a man who can’t find £4 7s. 7d. to pay for the
excitement he gets out of it. The criminal law is
more democratic. It would cheerfully hang a
man even if he hadn’t 2d.

Some precedents to support the Australian Par-
liament in its insistence on white labor conditions
oh mail vessels which it subsidises, and to suggest
to it Protection for the Australian seaman in the
forthcoming Navigation Act:
The British Navigation Act of 1845 (since unwisely
repealed) provided that British vessels must be navigated
by a British master, and that, in the case of sea-going
vessels at least three-fourths, and in the case of coastal
vessels all, of the crews must be British.
The United States shipping law of the present day pro-
vides, that every officer who has charge of a watch must
be a U.S. citizen.
The French shipping law enacts that on board a French
vessel all the officers and at least three-fourths of the
crew must be French.
The Italian shipping law states that on an Italian vessel
the master and at least two-thirds of the crew must be
Italians.
The Swedish and Norwegian law is that on a Scandi-
navian vessel the master, the mate and three-fourths of
the crew must be Scandinavians.

Adulteration.
When any Australian State attempts to legislate
against the adulteration of drink or foodstuffs
such an outcry is raised by vendors that if a Bill
passes it soon becomes inoperative. Indeed, not-
withstanding hysterical attempts at repression, it
is questionable if any State is capable of effectu
ally saving consumers from fraudulent vendors,
and in the interest of the public at large the
Commonwealth Parliament should be urged to
move in the matter on uniform lines. The French
Senate has just.adopted a Bill against fraudulent
vendors which is a model worth serious considera-
tion. A few articles run :
Article I.—For deceiving, or attempting to deceive,
the. purchaser, either as to the nature, quality, com-
position, etc., of the various products, the vendor shall
be liable to imprisonment, the minimum term being
three months and the maximum one year, along with
a fine ranging from £4 to £2OO. Article 2.—The term of
imprisonment may be extended to two years if the
offences set forth in Article 1 be committed by the aid of
false or inexact weights, measures, or other instruments
used with intent to defraud. Article 3.—The penalties
stipulated in Article 1 will be imposed upon (1) those
found guilty of adulterating the provisions intended for
either man or beast, etc., and (2) those who expose for
sale foodstuffs knowing them to be adulterated. In the
event, however, of the adulterated substance being
injurious to health, imprisonment will be compulsory
(the term being from three months to two years),
together with a fine of £2O to £4OO. Article 4.—A 'fine of
£2 to £l2O, or imprisonment of from six days to three
months, will be imposed upon any person found to
possess, without a legitimate reason, upon his shop,
workshop, business premises, etc., any false weights
or measures or other inaccurate apparatus used in
connection with merchandise.
That is not harsh. It is just. Less than that
plays into the .hands of fraud. A twopenny-
ha’penny fine is laughed at by the systematic-
defrauder ; the illicit profit on the output which
has caused the fine pays it many times over.
Imprisonment, if not for the first offence, should
certainly follow the second. The reason why the
Commonwealth Parliament should deal with this
matter is that it is the only Parliament which can
legislate for all Australia; which can shut out
imports of adulterated woollens that are cotton,
and adulterated tweed that is shoddy, and
adulterated silk that is largely mineral; and it is
the only Legislature which can protect one State
effectively against the influx of adulterated goods
from another, by pursuing the local adulterator
to the farthest ends of the Commonwealth.
♦
Thebe is an urgent need in the Commonwealth
of an Association for Minding One’s Own Busi-
ness. The worst—but by no means the only-
offender against the one essential rule of the
Association is the common or incompetent muni-
cipal councillor. He has already a vast quantity
of work set before him. He is supposed to see
that his district is kept clean and wholesome ; to
look after food adulteration, weights and measures;
to levy sufficient rates to keep his council solvent;
in fact, he is supposed—and elected —to do a num-
ber of things that he hasn’t the least intention or
idea of doing; but one thing that he is not
elected to do is to interfere in Federal or State
politics. Another frequent offender in this re-
spect is the average member of a Chamber of
Commerce. Melbourne Age lately urged this per-
son to do a little of the work he reckons it is his
Divine mission to leave undone, and a few days
later even his own pet Akgus directed his atten-
tion to the trader’s pleasing habit of selling 12oz.
of candles and other articles as a pound. This was
pre-eminently a matter for the Chambers of
Commerce to deal with—else what are they for?
But, up to date, the commercial person’s time has
been fully occupied by weird dissertations on the
extravagance of a High Court and plaintive regrets
that, there should be any such horror as Industrial
Arbitration. So far the school boards haven’t
given their official opinions on Federal politics;
nor have the cemetery trusts published any notes
on the foreign policy of the Hempire, but they
only need a very little encouragement to start
them on their wild career.
Pbemibr Dick Seddon, of Maoriland, threatens
to take the first backward step from his old policy
of trusting the people, and what the results will
be it is hard to say, but the chances are that they
will be disastrous. It was the Seddon party which
gave every adult the franchise, and the people
promptly set to work to use it as an instrument
for the destruction of the liquor traffic. In some
places prohibition was carried; in many other
places reduction was carried. Also, in a multi-
tude of places where prohibition was not
carried, the Prohibition party was strong
enough to elect teetotal Licensing Committees,
which administer the laws regulating public-
houses with all the rigor these laws admit
of, and order the abolition of barmaids, counter-
lunches, and other frills. A considerable public-
house interest has always been behind Seddon,
and Seddon is a perplexed man. He says the
Licensing law is being administered in a way that
was never intended, which seems a sadly futile
argument, for the teetotal committees are keeping
within the letter of the statute, and are adminis-
tering the people’s law strictly as the people
direct.. If the people direct them to administer it
for all it is worth, that seems to be the people’s
affair. But Seddon, in his disgust, is said to con-
template disfranchising the whole community in
one act as far as concerns its right to elect Licensing
Committees, and entrusting licensing business to
the nominee magistrates. There, in all proba-
bility, Seddon will fall in. He got his power by
enfranchising the people. If he starts to disfran-
chise the people anything may happen to him.

An interesting and timely item from the report of
a British Naval Committee:
There should always be an effective reserve squadron,
absolutely confined to home waters, sufficient to hold the
Channel and protect the coasts and commerce of the
United Kingdom, in addition to the coast defence ships
which would be required for active local defence.
From this it will be seen that British naval
authorities have no intention of taking the medi-
cine they offer us. All the available jeer-power
of every Jingo hereabouts is directed at the idea
of Australia proposing to keep a fleet for purely
local defence. The thing, we are told, is foolish,
impracticable and opposed to all naval science ;
what we want is a fleet that will go and hunt for
the enemy, and if the enemy dodges him, said
enemy can burn Sydney and Melbourne
to the ground before the fleet finds out
its mistake and comes back. But Britain
sticks closely to the policy of keeping a coast
defence fleet always on hand to protect its
Eqrts. Sydney and Melbourne don’t matter, but
liverpool and Cardiff matter a great deal, and the
chances that are good enough for us are not good
enough for Britain —not by a long way. The dis-
crepancy calls for inquiry. Premier Barton’s
proposals go dead against British precedent, and
it is disrespectful to kick British precedent in
the mud as Barton is doing.
KIDNEYS. LIVER.
The Cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago,
Sciatica, Neuralgia, Blood Disorders,
Gravel, Bladder Disorders, Indigestion,
Biliousness, Sick Headache, Anaemia.
HOW TO EFFECT A PERMANENT CURE.
The Kidneys. —Blood is conveyed from the heart to the kidneys through arteries, and it is the
function of the kidneys to filter the blood, and otherwise separate from it water and various im-
purities. These impurities are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter. When the kidneys have done
their work of purification, the blood enters the veins and traverses the body, accumulating, in its
course, a fresh supply of effete matter produced by the wasting of the tissues. The heart again
delivers the blood to the kidneys, and the process is repeated. Night and day the work of the
kidneys goes on without ceasing.
When the kidneys, through weakness or disease, are unable to perform their duty efficiently,
the blood retains the uric acid and urea which would otherwise be removed, and becomes con-
taminated by these urinary poisons. Nature’s method of letting us know that the kidneys are
working indifferently is to make us conscious of the presence of these poisons by causing us to
suffer from Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica, Neuralgia. Blood Disorders,
Gravel, Stone or Bladder Troubles. The commonest first symptom is a
PAIN IN THE BACK.
but it is not necessary to wait for this acute signal. If some urine, passed the first thing in the
morning, is placed in a covered glass and allowed to stand for twenty-four hours and is then cloudy,
shows a sediment like brick dust, is an unnatural colour, or has particles floating about in it, then the
kidneys are weak or diseased, and immediate steps must be taken to restore them to health and
activity.
The Stiver.—The health of the liver and of the kidneys is closely connected. It is almost impossible
for the kidneys to be affected and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa. The liver is an automatic
chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or
three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood, con-
verts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood, gradually,
as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is
completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles, which have lived their life and
are useful no longer.
When the liver is inactive or diseased, we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia,
Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders, because the blood is laden with urinary and biliary
poisons to such an extent that it cannot absorb nutriment from the food we eat, red corpuscles are no
longer formed, and it is these red corpuscles which nourish the nerves. In other words the blood is
vitiated and starved.
The importance of keeping the kidneys and liver in vigorous health will therefore be realised,
and an explanation is afforded why
Warner’s Safe Cure
possesses such wonderful power in curing so many seemingly different disorders as those mentioned.
Warner’s Safe Cure possesses the remarkable property of being able to restore the kidneys and liver
to health and activity. All urinary and biliary poisons are then duly removed by the simple process
of Nature and all suffering due to the presence of these poisons in the system necessarily ceases.
Mr. JEt. H. Blackman, 32 Greek Street, Glebe, Sydney, says About seven years ago I was
suffering from Gout, and was suddenly seized by Sciatica, which attacked me as I was driving, and
the pain became so bad that I had to be carried home. I was advised the next morning to take
Warner’s Safe Cure. After taking a few doses I felt relief, and after taking one or two bottles was
cured altogether.”
Mr. A. Newcombe, of 53 Arden Street, North Melbourne, says : —“ For several years I was a
martyr to periodical attacks of Indigestion. Eating became almost a terror to me. The attacks became
more frequent as time went on. I tried various remedies, but derived no benefit from any of them.
Having heard a good deal about Warner’s Safe Cure, I took some of that medicine, together with
Warner’s Safe Pills, and was quite cured. I have not had an attack now for many months.”

This gorgeous Sydney monopoly tramps through the
years on two unequal legs—the long December leg and
the short June leg. Apparently there has been a shrink-
age in profits ; but, as a matter of fact, the profits for an
off” half were never so large. The paid-up capital is
now £750,000, or £68,202 more than it used to be ; and
there must be an extra average profit of £5115 per half-
year in order to pay the 15 per cent, dividend. For the
last two years before the capital was increased the half-
yearly average profit was £51,425 ; for the last year,
£56,624, or £5199 more, so that the extra capital appears
to be just earning the 15 per cent, and a shadow over.
Besides the reserves mentioned above, there is another
£7953 in the shape of a reserve for the replacement of
meters.

The net profit (that is, the gross profit less allowance for
depreciation) for the last half-year was £57,661 as against
£54,755 in the previous half-year, and £47,941 in the
corresponding half of ’O2. The average net price realised
for silver (fine) during the last six months was a shade
over 2s. o£d. per oz., and for lead £lO 11s. 2d. per ton.
The gross profit was thus equal to £2 Bs. Bd. per ton of
lead. In other words,.with silver at the price averaged
during the half-year (it is now somewhat higher), lead
could fall to £8 2s. 6d. before the co. would be out of
pocket. During the last two years very striking
economies have been made; not only have costs
been reduced, but recoveries have been greatlv improved
In the half-year to May, 1901, silver was 2s. 6|d. and lead
£l3 2s. 9d., and the profit was £102,505. If silver had
been 2s. o£d. (the average of the past half-year) the profit
would have been only £27,276, or at the rate of 19s. 8d
per ton of lead. That means that, if the methods which
prevailed down to two years ago had been continued,
lead could only have been produced last half-year at a
cost of £l2 3s. Id.; and the co. on its output would have
lost £50,000 instead of making £BO,OOO. The economies
have been spread practically over the entire range of the
co s. operations, but the most important have been in
connection with the desulphurising of ores, local produc-

“Miner,” from Norseman (W.A.): “Don’t
agree with the statement that, when present levels at
Princess Royal are depleted, the mine will be worked out
According to a survey by Central co., it will have to sink
a vertical shaft 900 ft. before it cuts the lode. Said co
reported to be getting in £20,000 to buy machinery!
Present depth of Royal, 380 ft. No. 4 level driven about
70ft. on lode, which is 25ft. wide, and no hanging wall
met with. All underground work stopped at North co.
as it is about to erect a compressor capable of driving 10
rock drills.” 6

“Late Menzies Resident” writes: Par in B.
(20/6/’O3) anent the Lady Shenton, is another instance of
a mine being libelled to cover up blunders. Properly
worked, mine should have paid £50,000 or £60,000 more in
dividends in the two or three years past. Worse than the
disastrous blundering so well-known locally in connection
with this good little mine, is the oft-repeated and widely-
circulated covering-up lie about "patchy ore-bodies”
and "necessarily expensive conditions,” as capital has
thereby been scared from a valuable district, and many
promising ventures left unworked. Employees freely
assert that shaft, sunk to hit the reef at 450 ft., and now
down 650 ft., being located in a large “ break,” was fore-
doomed to failure. Borehole to 1000 ft. is officially reported
barren of results, so continued sinking promise's more for
the management (at about £l5OO a year) than for the co
or the credit of the district. As regards “obsolete
machinery ’’—that is a cute cry to excuse a record for
poor milling (1300 tons monthly with 35 stamps)
As a matter of fact, mine is hard to beat for quality and
up-to-dateness of equipment.
* * * •
Golden Link, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), continues to
do very well on its Eclipse lease. The ore body is so
wide that the manager estimates the reserves already
opened up above 100 ft. at 40,000 tons of 12dwt. ore The
200 ft. level is now being driven, and may possibly double
the estimate. Instead of merely adding five-head to the
existing mill it is now contemplated to double it. June
return of 2177 tons for 16400 z., worth £6692, leaves a
margin of well on for £4OOO, as costs are very low On the
Golden Link lease driving at the 1300 ft. level shows that
the L. V. Consols and Perseverance lodes junction about
280 ft. south of the shaft. At this point the ore is 18ft
wide, and worth 18dwt. The 1400 ft. level has just been
commenced.
* * * *
The New Moon Co., the parent of the Bendigo
group which just now provides Victoria with its boss
gold-mine, completed its most successful half-year on
June 30. On a revenue of £32,458 it made a profit of
£21,967, after allowing £B6O for wear and tear of plant
Of every £1 of gold won 13s. 6d. was profit. In dividends
during the past 5J years it has paid £ll4,ooo—on a paid-
up capital of £11,560, of which only £4200 is represented
by the paid-up capital in the present co. (the old co
tasted from 1870 to 1886). Up to a few months ago the
co did not trouble about a reserve fund, but it has now
made a start to build one up; though, as the directors
have an idea of using it “ for renewing machinery ” it
will probably >»t be there when it is most needed. ’
<.* * m
Extract from a pessimistic letter written by a
miner on the Tarnagulla (Vic.) rush : “ We have done no
good since we got that l£oz. piece. Our dirt has only
averaged about ldwt. to the load. We are sinking a new
shaft- expect to bottom this week. It is a very patchy
Elace. The newspaper reports are grossly exaggerated,
ut it is no use contradicting them—they won’tTpublish
contradictions. It has been raining heavily, and the
rush is in a nice mess. The carters can’t cart the wash-
dirt, so all hands are stacking it. I refused £2O for my
share in claim some time ago—would be inclined to take
it now.”
• * * *
Glenfine South (Pitfield, Vic.) finished a poor
half-year on June 30 ; 14,450 tons stone gave 32960 z., an
average of only 4dwt. 13Jgr., worth £12,975. Concen-
trates added £1144, and crushing £2O, making a total of
£14,139. Expenses were £13,654, leaving a profit of
£485. The dividend of £1750 which was paid was, there-
fore, not earned. Moreover, whereas the first crushing
for the half-year gave 6dwt. llgr., the last gave only
3dwt. Bgr.; and the manager can only see six months’
supply of stone of the lower value available. The co.,
which has paid £65,625 in dividends, has a cash balance
of £lO4O after meeting current liabilities.
At Poverty Point.
MU. E. NAPOLEON BOFFARD, heralding Australia amongst the Nations at the Alfred
Hospital Ball, recently held in Melbourne.
Hypocrisy is an understudy o 1 Virtue, and replaces her
in the second act.
An English hospital committee, deciding to raise funds
fora “charity concert,” carried out the entertainment in
a brilliantly original fashion. Tickets were printed, in
value from 6d. to 2s. 6d.,and the charitable urged to
buy with the assurance that they need not attend the
concert, for there would be none. Tickets went like wild-
fire, and there was no scandal as to the huge bite that
expenses made in receipts.
The distressful fact that Cecil Ward has to wear a
neatly-parted top-piece to preserve the continuity of his
hair and his place among juveniles came to light during
a performance of “ Are You a Mason ?” at Napier (M.L.)
recently. Desmond, the giddy youth of the piece, in-
advertently shifted the hirsute roof and exposed the
place where the dome of thought had climbed through
Its covering.
“ T.D.” : Hugh Ward should have an uncommonly fine
show in London, his versatility giving him a great pull
over the average one-rut man. Ward only evidenced a
little of his cleverness as a legitimate actor when he
made his small part in “ Arizona ” stand right out of the
ruck. Also, Ward’s wife, who sings as Miss Grace Millar
Ward, has a voice far above the mere utility work that
nowadays falls to Melbourne' singers, and she would have
been heard more often but for the fact that fees are too
small in these parts. If she takes up concert work in
London there should be plenty of room for her.
“ K. 0.” : The large and enthusiastic audience at Mar-
shall Hall’s last concert may have the effect of reducing
the deficit left from the last, as tariffs were raised all over
the hall. In any case, the work partakes largely of phil-
anthropy. When all exes, are paid, the salary of each
member of the big orchestra works out at exactly 7jd. an
hour. The practising is a very thorough business —all the
different instruments are taken many times in sections
before the whole band is assembled, and there are frcm
eight to a dozen full rehearsals. The long, bony con-
ductor gets nothing out of it but kudos.
Robert Courtneidge, when he was in Melbourne looking
after the Shakespeare shows, talked interestingly about
Australian, as compared with English, stage troubles.
The average Australian novice actor suffers from a
neglected accent and a combative self-sufficiency which
go to make him unteachable. But the occasional earnest
striver shows a quicker intelligence than the average
teachable young Britisher. Stage hands are more alert
and generally useful than the men in the Cold Country.
Comparing the productions of the same heavy shows in
Manchester and Melbourne, Courtneidge found that the
scenes here were set by fewer hands in rather better time.
“ 0.K.” : Probably the foreign accent of Rudinoff is a
big factor in his personal magnetism, and the magnetism,
there can be no doubt, is the whole secret of his success.
He does nothing that is remarkably clever ; nevertheless
the Melbourne and Sydney public in turn have —about
90 percent, of them—been bluffed into a lively admiia*
tion for the tall, shapely, lime-lighted showman. Ihe
man, the manner, and the accent combine to do the
trick, whilst people in front think they are impressed by
the cleverness of one “smoke” picture and three or four
lightning sketches (always the same), a whistle, a third-
rate baritone warble, some ordinary shadowgraph work,
and some very ordinary patter. Rudinoff can talk
English without his stage-accent, and when he drops
that effective disguise he would pass.for a Cockney of
Polish Jew parentage. All the same, he has been quite
as popular a foreign artist in London as he now is in
Australia. Another man offering the same entertain-
ment might be reckoned an audacious pretender, but
Rudinoff is a genuine success. He impresses the unini-
tiated multitude every time.

Thomas Bent was busy promising railways
in the Mallee when the news of his downfall as
Victorian Minister of Railways reached him;
just as he had undertaken to build a line to Went-
worth, some bitter cuss handed him a paper which
mentioned that the broad man was sacked. The
Agk, which, a short time ago, devoted a three-
column biographical sketch to Bent, with a por-
trait in the Leader thrown in, now scoffs like
this : “ While he was promising a railway to the
mosquito-ridden country of Wentworth, he had
ceased to reign at the Railways, and the crown
was borne by the aspiring William of Norman by.’'

City Improvements.
The extensive premises at the corner of Eliza-
beth and Bathurst-streets, known as The Freeman
and Wallace Electro-Medical and Surgical Insti-
tute, are at present undergoing a conspicuous
change in their interior and exterior aspects. The
adjoining premises have been secured by the firm
as an addition to the Institute for correspondence
and electric bath purposes, and at present a large
number of tradesmen are busy renovating and
fitting the various chambers with the most com-
plete and scientific systems of electric bathing
obtainable in Europe. The new premises will be
opened shortly for public inspection and patron-
age. Visitors from the country would do well
when in town to pay a visit to this famous Insti-
tute ; the Specialists of which are amongst the
most famous practitioners in the spheres of grave
nerve and blood diseases afflicting humanity.*

The proposition to appoint, con equent on
Kingston’s resignation, Langdon Bunython to
an honorary portfolio in the Australian Ministry
is an instance that provincialism has not yet died
out in Australian politics. The sole reason
advanced why Langdon should be, selected is
that his appointment would be “a compliment to
South Australia.” The idea is that each State
wants one man in the Government, which is a silly
idea anyhow. In a State Government no one
bothers . because the S.S.W. and £ W., corner
has not a representative in the State Cabinet, and
why should not the same rule hold good all round.
Speaking at an A.N.A. banquet the other
evening, Yic. State Premier Irvine complained
bitterly that under Federation the people, of the
State were taxed through the. Customs but only
received back a portion of the money taken from
them; the rest being squandered in Federal
extravagance. And no one called him an im-
pudent and malicious slanderer. It is time
Federal politicians took a turn at criticising State
Governments, and complained of the people being
taxed through the Federal Customs for the benefit
of the extravagant State administrations. State
extravagance is ruining the country, and what is
wanted is an extension of the present thrifty,
careful Commonwealth management. Men like
Irvine and Philp are spending too much good
Commonwealth revenue on, Kanaka employers
and wealthy farmers and other rampant paupers.

“ Ilex ” : Why get unhappy because the
standard height for admission to the British army
was lowered to sft. ? Also, does the fact really
indicate British physical degeneracy in toto, or
only royal physical degeneracy? How do you
suppose that Mighty Atom, the Booklet of Corn-
york, was to get his billet in the army with the
standard admission height at sft. Bin. ? Standards
must be adapted to circumstances. Wasn’t the
British standard “yard ” once determined by the
length of the king’s arm ? [But they don’t reduce
the standard height of the private even if the
honorary general is stumpy; any more than they
cut the campaign short because the king is
short-winded.J
N.S.W. Importer and M.P., David Storey,
at a farewell meeting, moaning over the harsh
law which punishes the forged entry and the
faked invoice:
Some of those who ranked as their best citizens were
not only discouraged after long years of valuable service
to commerce and trade, but were persecuted in such a
way as to keep them in perpetual anxiety. That was
scarcely the treatment which should be accorded public-
spirited, honorable men. It found no precedent in the
land of their forefathers where the flag: of freedom still
proudly waved.
Importers object to be reckoned dishonest as a
class, but can they wonder when they are so free
m their public expressions of sympathy for
swindlers who have been found out ?

The Fugitives.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fati man stole stealthily up the dark lane and
paused in the shadow of the fifth gate.
“This looks like it,” he muttered huskily to
himself, looking up at the back windows of the
house. Then he found a dust-box and started to
climb the gate, for it was locked.
“ Darn it! ” The fat man paused and felt for
the nail that had ripped his garments. After
groping for-a minute he discovered it, and tore his
finger badly trying to wrench it out.
“ Darn it! ’’ He whispered fiercely to himself
and sucked his wounded finger. “That nail
always tore everything!” After a while he
clambered down into the yard and slunk into the
shadow of the wood-shed, where he managed to
bruise his shins on an empty packing-case. Then
he cursed again and sat down on the packing-case
to think.
A door at the upper end of the yard opened
suddenly, and a man stepped out and walked,
whistling, down towards the gate.
Opposite the wood-shed he stopped and started
back.
“ Who’s there ? ” he snapped.
The man on the packing-case was as if stricken
dumb.
Then came the sudden flare of a match, in which
the man on the case was discovered, blinking like
an owl.
“ It’s only me! ”he sighed, wearily.
“ You!” The match hissed in a puddle, and
the two faced each other in the gloom.
“I thought—you were dead/”
The man in the shed sighed again. “No,” he
murmured, “ I ain’t dead.”
“ I thought you were drowned in that,wreck? ”
“Missed th’ boat,” said the mysterious one.
“Good God!’’gasped the other, and put his
hand against the'fence to steady himself.
Then the visitor, in an apologetic whine, began
to talk, and the other man listened —listened help-
essly to the end.
1 • * • *
“Well, now that you’ve come back, what are
you go.ihg to do l ”
The returned shuffled uneasily in the shadow.
“P unno >” l* e muttered feebly.
The other peevishly cursed him.
“ You dog, why’can’t you decide ? If you will
come back from the dead, why can’t you come
respectably: to the front door ? ”
Tliere was a faint murmur of reproach.'
“Shut up, you dam sea-ghost!” hissed the
host. “ I don’t believe your yarn about missing
the boat. I don’t believe you’re alive. You’re
dead, d’ye hear—dead as a door-nail! ”
“ Steady, Bill. Now ”
“ Bali! It’s a lie, I tell you—a lie !•”
“It ain’t!” protested the dead man. “It ain’t
a lie, an’ what’s more, I don’t want t’ come back
an’ bust th’ show. I didn’t ’ave no picnic with
’er when —when I was—.” He faltered and
Btopped.
. “ Alive ! ” suggested the other.
“Put it that way if yer like. Anyway, I don’b
want to come back.”
“Just as well for you.”
“I don’t want t’ come, Bill.”
. Bill gave a grunt of contempt.
“You can’t come back ; you’re dead ”
“Far as she’s concerned, Bill, I am, an’—’tween
ourselves—l ain’t sorry. Come here a minit.”
After a little whispered persuasion Bill went
and sat beside him, and his visitor laid on his
knee the heavy hand of confidence and poured
forth his sorrowful soul.
“When I left ’er, Bill—seven years ago—l
didn’t do it because I was too well treated. I left
’er, Bill, because she’d been givin’ me a devil of a
time. D’ye believe me now, Bill ? ” He leant
forward and peered into the other man’s face, but
the other man was eloquently silent.
“ I was .drove to it, Bill—drove to it. So when
me chance came I gave it best an’ cleared out t’
make another start. I did it as much for ’er
sake as for me own, for th’ energy she was puttin’
inter naggin’ at me was wearin’ ’er out, body and
soul, an’ I could see it. Ain’t that right, Bill ? ”
Bill shifted uneasily on his seat, and kicked
softly at the wood-pile. “That’s right, Joe.
She told me often and often she couldn’t stand
the sight of you.”
Joe gratefully patted his friend’s knee. ‘ ‘ ’Course
she couldn’t, Bill I don’t blame ’er a bit neither.
Me dyin’, so t’ speak, was th’ best thing that
could ’ave ’appened, an’ now that I’ve found out
’ow good ye’ve been, an’ ’ow ye’ve stood by ’er,
an’ ’ow ’appy y’ are, I thought I’d just borry five
bob or ’arf a quid orf ye, ’an we’d say no more
about it.”;
Bill sat up, with a jerk. “ What did you say ? ”
“ I said, Bill ” —Joe’s voice was tremulous with
emotion—“ now, that I find yer so ’appy, I
thought as ’ow ’arf a quid—— ”
“ Who the blazes told you I was happy ? ”
“W’y” (in a tone of pained astonishment).
“Ain’t yer ? ”
“ You fool! Why, if I thought I’d have to
stay with her much longer, I’d cut my throat.
Now ”
‘ ‘ Don’t—don’t she treat yer well, Bill ? ”
“ Treat me ! ” Bill leant over and thumped
impressively on his shirt-front. “ She leads me a
dog’s life. Says I’m ten times worse than you
were, and wishes I was dead ! ”
“ That’s what she uster say to me, Bill. It’s a
sort o’ sayin’ o’ hers. Look ’ere, now—don’t you
mind what she says. She’s got a temper, but
she’s a good girl, Bill—a jolly good girl, an’ you
stick to ’er! ”
“Now, what the devil are you snivelling
about ?”
“Me, Bill!”
“Yes —you. What do you mean by crawling
round now and preaching about her to me. You
were glad enough to clear out ! ”
“Well, Bill, I—l ain’t denying it, I know I
did it! ”
“Very well, then. Now listen to me.” Bill
leant forward and whispered something through
his clenched teeth that made Joe clutch at his
arm and gasp as if in pain.
“ Bill, ole boy ! Oh, Billy—you wouldn’t leave
’er now—would yer ? ”
“ Shut up! After you were reported dead, I
married her, and what sort of a time have I had !
.... Look here! If you don’t take me with you
—you cur —if you don’t help me out, I’ll ”
“ Don’t, Bill! I’ll take yer ! ”
“ Unless I go with you to-night ”
Bill tightened his grip and pointed to the
shadow on the blind.
So they stood, for a space, while the original
husband thought it out.
At last he whispered: “I suppose, Bill, she’ll—
get ’long all right, eh ? ”
‘‘ I suppose so.”
“Seems a bit —just abit’ardon ’er, don’t ver
think ? ”
“ Harder on us ! ” hissed Joe, pulling his arm.
They tip-toed, gently to the gate. Even then
Bill turned and looked back as if his conscience
troubled him. Then, at a nudge from Joe, he
slowly turned, grabbed the top of the gate with
both hands and bent his right leg at the knee.
“Gimme leg up!” he sighed, and was hoisted
for ever out into the cold, unfeeling World.

Curious item from the Argus :
John Kendall has been missing from his home in
Drummond-street, Oarlton, since 23rd inst. He was a
probationer from the Sunbury Asylum, and suffered from
the delusion that he was going to obtain work in Mel-
bourne.
He suffered from the delusion that he was going
to obtain work in Melbourne ! Have the reduced
tariff and the Irvine-Bent Government brought
things so low as that ?
These “ society ” items are taken in dead
and solemn earnest from an English paper, de-
voted to the doings of the British aristocraoy:—
We were sorry to hear after going to press last week of
another death among Miss ’s pets, and now she writes
us : “ It is with great grief I am' writing to tell you my
dear, sweet Marquis Ito of Kobe has not been spared me.
He passed away this morning. He made his debut at
Marl’s Court, November, 1901, and since then he has taken
seven specials, nine seconds and twelve firsts. He is
sorely missed, as he was too sweet a companion and
friend. lam most wretched. This makes my ninth dog
taken from me. It is very hard.”
Mrs. passed through many trying moments last
night when her good brood bitch, Ping Sheng, was having
her litter by Prince Kwang. Three dead puppies were
removed, and she suffered greatly from exhaustion, but
happily she recovered, and is now getting on well with
her four red bitch puppies, which were afterwards born
alive.
“ Kati-Kati ” : Struck a philosopher the
other morning. Came to the door with a basket
of oranges to sell. It was raining. The lover of
knowledge seldom knows enough to come in out
of the wet. Bought some, and asked him why he
didn’t get a horse and cart. Said he used to have
one, but the horse dying, and there being no
sinking fund to replace it, he got a hand-cart and
wore it out; replaced it with a wheelbarrow, and
when that was finished bought his basket, which
was getting a bit shaky. Thought an old sugar-
bag would suit him when it was done. Seemed
very like our politicians.
YVestralia lias been shuddering at some
Zolaesque descriptions of the local prime steak
and undercut. A goldfields correspondent gave
one of his experiences:—
While passing a butcher’s yard the other day I stopped
to watch a bullook being killed. I saw them cut the
head off, and oh 1 what a mess with lumpy jaw. When
the liver and lights were being removed about a bucket-
ful of rotten matter came out, and the stench was some-
thing terrible. Prom what the slaughterman told me
they were condemned cattle, and were supposed to ba
destroyed for pleuro and tick, of which he said they were
full.
None of these places have public abattoirs; and
without public abattoirs meat inspection is mostly
a farce. Even when a very big firm (with a
Minister among its lawyers) was accused by the
Stock Department of dodging a tuberculous
mob past the inspector, the Government took the
inquiry out of the hands of the Perth S.M., to
whom it had been remitted. Certainly a Minis-
terial inquiry was promised; but, of course,
nothing came of that. After Australia has got
its importers into the way of being honest, it
might do a lot worse than give some serious
attention to its butchers and stock-dealers.
