Phantasy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Darkness—f! irkness over all—
Midnight dim and deep —
Wearily the wild winds crawl
Over strath and steep.
Dreaming swings the world away,
Whirling to a cleaner day :
Shall I rail or shall I pray,
Since I cannot sleep ?
Lone—alone ! Across the gloom,
Watching in the night,
I can mark faint things of doom,
Dim shapes of affright,
Reeling in fantastic crowds,
Softly chanting drifting clouds,
Girded all around with shrouds,
Trailing faintly white.
Waking from a fever-spell,
In my room alone,
Strange eyes from the back of Hell
Freeze me cold as stone !
Flying voices, eyes a-stare,
Take grey wings and cleave the air—
Here, before me, everywhere,
Crying in soft moan.
Lone—alone ! The vampires jeer
At my poor estate.
Fiends, who stole, her hut last year
When the Spring was late,
Grant her now a brief release,
That her face may bring me peace,
Respite, and a sweet surcease
From a world of hate!
And—a voice is in my ear,
Gentle, soft, and low !
Trembling in a breathless fear,
Like the underflow
Of a sightless spring that cries
From its darkness for the skies ;
So, she stoops with radiant eyes,
Stoops unto my woe 1
Fade the grey wings: Darkness slips,
Rolling out of space ;
All things vanish but her lips
And her saintly face.
Low beside me close, she kneels,
Round my neck a soft arm steals,
And my reason rocks and reels
In its hiding-place.
Swift she rolls the veil of years
Backward to a June,
Smiling through its nightly tears,
Full of mellow moon.
June, when side by side we crept
Down the moony meads that wept,
Hearts and hands that swung and leapt
To an elder tune.
Frail our little world of love,
Dark the things to be,
Stillness in the willow-grove,
Moaning on the sea.
When they robed her as she lay,
Blinded to a singing day,
And the old hearts crept away
Quaking mournfully.
But to-night she lays her head,
Shining, on my breast,
And ecstatic things are said,
Lips to lips hard pressed;
Arms entwining crush and cling,
Strange birds in a nowhere sing
Paeans of eternal Spring,
In a golden West!
From my heart the shadows roll,
And this soul of mine
Goes out forth to meet her soul,
Radiant, divine.
In sweet sanctuary they meet,
Locking in communion sweet;
She is kneeling at my feet—
How her dark eyes shine !
VIC.
C. L. RIVERS.
A Morning Caller.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One day in Nor’-West Australia I had a
long, hard day’s ride, with a good deal of foot-
climbing over and up and down broken gullies.
Coming to a comfortable-looking point on a sandy
creek with a bit of fresh feed, I determined to
camp.
The sun was nearly down ; I hobbled out
quickly, lit the fire, and put down my quart pot
to boil whilst I made my bed. The day had been
very hot, so I had a refreshing bath, finished my
meal, and turned in. I am a good sleeper, going
comfortably off as soon as I lie down, hardly ever
waking until near daylight. This night I
slept as usual, waking a trifle earlier to find a full
moon lighting up the surroundings. I lay for a
few moments, planning my day’s work, when to
my horror I saw a large and hostile-looking
crocodile steal noiselessly out of the water at my
very feet.
For a moment I was spellbound ; then, realising
the situation, without once removing my gaze I
grasped and brought my rifle to bear upon him,
but he was in such a direct line that there was no
vulnerable spot for a safe shot and the bullet
might glance off. Should he prove to be a man-
eater he might fight for it then. I could fire if he
moved closer.
I was still as a statue and the saurian was as mo-
tionless as the rocks around us. I could see down
the partly opened jaws that seemed tongueless in
their blackness. He was within a few yards of
me when, like a flash, lie rushed upon the dog,
then just stretching himself from his sleep at my
side. The dog, however, saw him in time to
nimbly leap aside. T en round and round they
went, the huge brute being quite as agile as the
dog, whilst ever and anon the vast cavernous jaws
smote together with a slam like the falling of a
dozen suits of armor. The vicious sweeps of the
great muscular tail were so sudden, and swept so
close to the dog, that I felt he must be taken.
Yet I could not help until I could get a fair open-
ing. Kneeling on my bedding, with finger on
trigger, I keenly watched for a chance.
It was clear the crocodile trusted more to the
quick, wide sweeps of his tail than to catching
with his jaws, though the great teeth gleamed
savagely, and the narrow evil eye seemed to fairly
blaze with venom. They were now. about a dozen
yards from my bed, and though minutes seemed
to have passed I dare say it was really only
seconds. At last the dog was cast half-way to
the bed by a spent blow of the tail, and the
monster was on him in an instant, but the rifle-
bullet was first, and over the enemy rolled from a
lucky shot behind the ear.
I ate a bit of the foe that had intended eating
me. Very little sufficed. The dog wouldn’t touch
it raw, but filled up well off the grilled steaks.
The flesh was as delicate-looking as that of a
young pullet.
It was a narrow shave, but the odds are it was
the dog he wanted and not me. I measured the
prize by my whip, and found him between 14 and
15 feet long. The head was particularly big for
his size, and I fancy he must have been pretty
• old, for most of the teeth were very rounded and
worn at the points. I have two of them still—
one made into a matchbox. The brute’s head was
broader and thicker than that of any other croco-
dile I have ever seen, and the teeth had a pro-
nounced backward slope.
HARRY STOCKDALE.
Explorations in Civilization.
(BEING THE LETTERS OF AN AUSTRALIAN IN
EXILE.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
January, 16th.— I did not speak in my haste—
these people, and, I suppose, all the other peoples
of the old world, live on tradition, and all the lies
and ornaments which make up tradition—Gaud
Mayor’s Shows, and other squalid circus parades;
presentations of the “freedom of the city” to
some ancient Pistol who should be rewarded with
an axe, and only with an axe; cheap skite about
the flag that has braved a thousand years, and so on.
The worship of unnecessary ceremonial pervades
the national character. The papers gravely
chronicle as a fact of public interest that
“Lord Chelmsford is to be Gold Stick-in-
Waiting, and Colonel Colley Silver Stick during
March.” In municipal life the beadle still orders
ratepayers to appear before the alderman of the
ward at a wardmote, and the Gaud Mayor and
the aldermen go in a carriage of appropriate brass
to open the session of the Old Bailey—as if we
were still in those days when a Judge was a bitter
prosecutor and went in state to open his court
with his hangman going before him.
You know that the idiocy of “ beating the
bounds” is still practised in some Australian
towns, but the most they do is to drive the Mayor
and councillors round the boundaries and to give
them, on their return to the town hall, more or
less drink. The silly performance dates from a
time when surveying was cutting its first tooth,
and every man’s land had an alignment according
to the man’s individual taste and fancy; and at
that time the necessity for the grown generation
teaching the youths the boundaries of their towns
and villages was probably great; although one
cannot help being amazed at the marvellous kind
of intellect which could skite about its greatness as
a nation and yet was unable to chart a village.
But to this day the silly practice is perpetuated
in London. On a day in January or February the
beadles convoy a number of boys to the town
boundaries. The boys carry wands, but at certain
points on the city boundaries the boys only bear
the wands—that is to say, they bear the half-
dozen blows inflicted on their pants by the beadles
at each halting-place. The idea of beating the
boys at the bounds—or “beating the bounds”—is
the same brilliant idea which flogs boys in public
schools who fail at the fifth proposition of Euclid.
After the beating of the bounds each boy receives
an orange and sixpence ; and each beadle gets a
roast of beef. This is not a description of a cus-
tom of the Chinese before the opium war—it is
written of London in 1901.
The King is not allowed to pass the Memorial
of Temple Bar that blocks the Strand, without
the Gaud Mayor presenting the city keys—
although everybody knows that there are no
gates—and then riding before Edward to the
Guildhall. Ask the reason for the continuance
of this, and you are told that you are a low fellow
who wishes to “destroy the picturesque.” The
picturesque ! The memory of the Gaud Mayor
trying to mount his horse, after presenting a
ridiculous key that doesn’t fit any known lock, to
a stout monarch in a brass hearse, is one of the
funniest memories I have.
In 1585, Queen Elizabeth granted to the people
of Corby—a village in Northamptonshire—an
exemption from all town and bridge tolls in
England. The privileges have, of course, long
since disappeared, but the people of Corby hold
the Toll Fair regularly to celebrate the dead Act
of 1585. After the rector and the chairman of
the parish have read the charter and opened the
fair they are put in the stocks. Other digni-
taries are strapped to a pole and carried through
the village.
Queen Anne was crowned in 1702 and in 1902
the bi-centenary was celebrated at Kingston-on-
Thames, and—touching fact !—a newly gilded
statue of the late Queen Anne was unveiled by a
Jap., one Hayashi, who is also a Viscount, but
who is primarily a Jap.
On Whitsunday, 1000 years ago, that is in the
year 902, Edward the Elder was crowned at’
Kingston. There is evidently somebody still alive
who remembers this Saxon king, who gnawed his
meat direct from the bone and died without ever
seeing a clean floor or using a fork at meals, for
in 1902, a thousand years after, Edward the Elder’s
coronation was celebrated at Kingston.
There was never before such a collection of
dressed-up and tricked-out children as the Judges
and the parties and the witnesses of the Court of
Claims—constituted for the Coronation to fix
certain procedure and positions. There was the
Lord Chancellor as President, in black and gold
robes, the Duke of Norfolk in the mediaeval finery
of an Earl Marshal with the ribbon and Star of
the Garter. One Frank Dymoke claimed to be
the King’s Champion by right of ownership of the
Manor of Scrivelsby. The duty of this burlesque
officer is to go before the King in the Coronation
procession and invite people to tread on the tail of
his coat at least all such people as consider the
Kingnottobeafitand properperson. Nomantook
up the challenge at the last Coronation, but one of
these days Mr. Dymoke will challenge once too
often. The humor of taking up the challenge has
evidently not occurred to anybody—when it does,
the joker may be rewarded with three months’
imprisonment.
The Duke of Newcastle and the Earl of Shrews-
bury claimed “the right to provide a glove for the
King’s right hand, and to support his Majesty’s
right arm while he is holding the sceptre.” The
Duke of Newcastle won his claim for the reason
that he is “ Lord of the Lay Manor of Worsop ”
—whatever that may ,be. The Earl of Shrews-
bury was comforted by being granted the right to
“carry a white staff.” I don’t like to see an earl
in tears, and I hereby give him the right to carry
a telegraph pole, if he wants to.
The Lord Mayor claimed the right to “serve
our Lord and King in the Abbey by carrying
before our said Lord and King the crystal sceptre
or mace of the said city.” For an hour this
Solemn Circus argued the important matter.
Finally a book of 1629 proved that in 1629 the
Mayor went to a Coronation in a red robe, and so
the Mayor was granted what he had asked for;
and departed as pleased as any other savage who
has inherited four beads and a foot of hoop-iron.
There were fierce fights for the right to carry
a sword, a pair of spurs and a second sword ; and
finally the Earl of Errol said he had a right “ to
walk as Lord High Constable and to have a
silver baton tipped with gold at each end.”
When thi3 was granted he asked for =B9 10s.
to pay for a new baton; and the Solemn Circus
agreed, with the grave elation of a man who had
just discovered a continent. After four days
of this portentous rot—this anxious competition
for a thing as valueless as a last year’s grass-
hopper—the court dissolved itself with the en-
thusiasm of flat ginger-beer.
England is not alone in this matter of glori-
fying the penny-bun and gilding the trivial. All
Europe has the complaint badly. But some other
parts of Europe seem to know they have it, while
England doesn’t.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
A man’s right to marry his deceased wife’s
sister Jane is good enough for a country where
man is politically supreme; but, when woman gets
as high up the ladder as the man, she wants just
the same right to marry her departed’s brother
Ebenezer if she feels that way and the brother
thinks he can stand it. The first Bill introduced
by the first Attorney-General that Maoriland has
had for something like 10 years is intended to
give her that right in certain circumstances.

Nirvana.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ At even it shall be light, ”
.Thus said the Christ to men.
Nay,” said Lord Buddha, “ when
Draws nigh the cosmic Night,
When the sun of Time is cooling, westing,
The wearied son], like a bird, would be nesting ;
The eventide is the time for resting;
Peace, not Knowledge, and Rest, not Light,
Shall be yours in that solemn Night.”
Too much of light has man
In this world of stress and strain ;
Dazed eyes and reeling brain
Are his through all Life’s span.
Restless and eager, in wasteful measure,
He hunts a shadow and calls it pleasure,
He heaps up dirt and he deems it treasure
O, to be free from this foolish strife-^
This mad unrest called Life 1
Is Life, then, in living worth ?
Yes ; with this truth impressed—
That we but attain to rest
Through countless birth on birth.
Through strife of unit, family, nation,
In cycle, century, generation,
We shall reach at last Life’s consummation,
And in Nature’s soft embrace remain,
Nor ever be born again.
’Twas Goethe’s earthy soul
That called for “Light, more light,”
When he felt the shades of Night
Over his being roll.
Spirits pure, by no earth-fear haunted, .
Welcome the Night, serene, undaunted ;
No tears are shed and no dirges chaunted ~
When these, with heaven-erected head,
Through the god-like twilight tread.
The Day hath many eyes;
The Night, with sweet control,
Blots from the placid soul
Fear, Wonder and Surmise.
Full many splendors the Day concealeth ;
Millions of glories the Night revealeth ;
At Nature’s the spirit kneeleth.
Then hardly to feel, and not to know,
Is sure surcease of woe.
We shall not be glad or afraid
In the Night whither all must go ;
There shall neither be high nor low,
Neither Maker nor made ;
No man, no woman, no tribe, no nation,
No heavenly crown, no Hell’s damnation ;
Only the Oneness of Creation;
God and the Devil, beast, rock, and tree,
Shall all be One as we.
Then shall we taste true bliss—
Neither to think nor to speak,
Neither to know nor to seek;
Conscious only of this—
That all which Time once marred is mended,
That Nature and we in one are blended,
That Hate is conquered and Strife is ended,
That nevermore is there worst or best—
Only most perfect Rest.
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
Bung in the Bush.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bung’s is a sorry calling when sifted right through,
and Bung’s people, generally, are vicious and poor
and mean. Bung has got the bush as well as the
cities of Australia. This is one of our curses.
The really independent publicans of the bush
might be counted on one man’s fingers in any
State. All the others are tied, bound to iniquity,
compelled to rob, cheat and betray; tempted to
do a good deal more if they want to get a “ nest-
egg.” They have no wholesome traditions behind,
no honest prospects ahead. The very earliest
dealers in rum were among the very “nicest”
people in the land —Johnstones, Macarthurs,
Wentworths, Blaxlands—all of them panned off
gold from the lees of the rum casks, but in a
wholesale sort of way. They were the Bungs of
their time, and doubtless had their retail distri-
butors who were not too scrupulous.
Our earliest shanties were dens where men met
secretly to shake off the horrors of a life that was
hell on earth. The earliest shanty-keepers were,
without exception, copvicts ; generally “bad-
uns,” and they ha've established the traditions and
shaped the laws of the trade. There never was a
Boniface amongst them : there never was an inn
worthy of the name. On some main coaching-
lines good hotels grew (and generally decayed) but
they were never for bushmen, never for the people
of the land, never for the man with a shilling or
two in his pocket, who wanted food and comfort,
with no more fire-water than would quicken his
appetite or soothe him to rest. A man of that
sort is of no use to the bush-publican, to Bung’s
man. And what use would the bush market-town
publican have for the farmer who would drive in
with his own horsefeed and his pound of steak, or
chops; who would say to the hostler, “Stable and
feed ’im, lad and to the. missus, “Ha’ they
frizzled for me in half-an-hour, and taties wi’
em ! ” Table tp lay and knives to clean, cooking,
waiting, attendance, with a pint of honest malt
liquor—all for aK.shilling! Beelzebub, Muggins
and Murdoch are not in business for that sort of
thing. They are Bung’s men. With them a man
sliould. breast the bar when he comes in, and take
his food as it is put before him.
Suppose a traveller comes along at 3 or 4, keen
to eat,./willing to drink, ready to pay, what is his
welcoriie ? I will quote two instances in strange
contrast. One was a big hotel back of Western
Port in Victoria, and four of us, not all undistin-
guished, arrived at 3 ; sharp-set, eager. “ Some-
thing to eat, something to eat.” “Well, it’s an
awkward time of the day.” “Awkward be
hanged. Is there anything awkward about some
cam and eggs > or a spatchcock, or a red-hot steak
or chop with omelette or pancake to follow ? ” It
was all very well to talk ; the best that could
be done by Bung’s man was a neither-hot-nor-cold
with sour bread and pickles. That was “A
Motel, written big.
Ihe other instance was in the old world and
near the city of Oxford. Three of us, lads, and
one senior, got in trouble over a “lasher’’—beat,
smashed, and some of us half-drowned. We had
to tramp four miles through wet meadows to a
visage, where we found an inn—Yew Tree Inn.
lne house looked as old as the yew tree. In 20
minutes we were in bed, with a steaming rummer
or grog. In lialf-an-hour tjiere was a dish of
bacon and eggs, with toast, butter, coffee and
cream. inree hours later our clothes were dried,
and merrily we footed it into town, mulct of but
Is 3d. each, for all that meat, drink, and com-
r?us.*' <^e > me die in a proper inn ;
but, while I live, heaven keep me out of all
Australian hotels, save a few in the city and a
tew more in the larger inland towns.
6 shall never get good bush accommodation
till real inns are established and Bung is hobbled
short; till no man is allowed to obtain a license
who cannot show that he has reasonable expecta-
tions of obtaining a livelihood, and that he is abso-
lute owner or State-lessee of the land whereon
the house is built. . Such a man should get an inn
license for a nominal fee. Scores now running
hotels could not, by legitimate business, make
enough to pay for their licenses. Then how do
they live ? In this way.
•i a pub down one of the rivers, 50
miles from a township and 30 miles from one of
tbe mggest stations in Australia. There was not
A5O worth of fair trade on the track in the year,
and yet M JSeil flourished. Shearing was coming
on, and men passing down in dozens every day.
Some were foolish enough to give M‘Neil a try.
All cursed him, and prayed they might be stiffened
if he saw more than the dust of their heels when
shearing was over. Then when 150 rouseabouts
and washers (it was hot-water-washing in those
days) came along with their cheques, or the
balances the hawks had left them, M‘Neil was at
the door with a white shirt and a new face. He
~ a blackfellow inside with a concertina, and a
gai from town * behind the bar. He got more
than 50 of them, and those who had protested
loudest were first down.
“What is a man to do?” asked M‘JS T eil after-
wards, and he produced Bung’s letter. Bung was
content to let things remain till after shearing,
but if by the end of November £lOO was not forth-
coming, certain steps would be taken. M‘Neil
was not disposed to wield a pick in a cutting or a
tank again, and so took steps to meet Bung’s
demands, just as three-fourths of the lessees of
those big, new hotels in town and suburbs do.
they can no more live on legitimate trade than
can the out-back publican. And so three out of
tour of the big beer palaces of stone, stucco, and
brick, should properly be called something else
than hotels. And does Bung know it ? Bung
playing the ostentatious game in public charities-
relieving one where he has debauched and ruined
a, hundred—Bung has got to sell his beer: and,
because assignations and prostitution, drugging’
robbing and debauchery help him, they arf his
accepted allies in town and bush.
But Bung must not have all the blame of the
bush horror. The squatter who runs his business
on strictly commercial lines (generally because,
mortgaged up to the hilt, he has no alternative)
has nearly as much blame as Bung. It never
occurs to him that man cannot live on “ten ten
two and a quarter” alone; that an occasional
spree is as necessary to his existence as is a holi-
day to the well-being of any hard-working citizen.'
Bverv other country under the sun has recognised
tins. Jews, Pagans, Turks, Infidels, as well as
Christians—all, save Australians, provide festivals
for their workers at all great harvests. Look at
the fine homely spree provided by the Thames
yeoman, when he has gathered in his hundred
acres of hay. Not much more than bacon, cheese
and beer, maybe, with cake and pasties for the
children ; but still a hearty and heartening feast
which sends the worker on his way rejoicing, and
leaves the master in no way impoverished. Com-
pare it, then, with the paying-off on a big
Australian station, where £40,000 worth of wool
has been shorn and a hundred men are going on
the road. It is stiff, formal and heartless as the
regular pay-day at colliery or factory. The man
who wants a change—a little bit of the light o’
things—must just “go up to M‘Neil’s.” And
there Bung s satellite-Satan’s Ganymede—melts
him down.
It is the same with the harvest. No joy for the
workers. What’s called a harvest festival is some
mane and inconsequential church-parade in town.
Ay ! and the same even with the vintage The
grapes are plucked in devilish and deadly fashion
—never a song or a dance, or such a loving cup
as would leave folk quiet, warm, filled with plea-
sant memories and hopes for the future. Bung is
the only purveyor of anything that takes the
worker mentally or physically off the treadmill of
the bush ; but he who goes to Bung goes to the
-Devil also. Pity it should be so; but who’s to
mend it ? The strict commercial idea, becoming
day by day more dominant in all things, offers no
hope. There is not the smell of a “ spree ” in it,
and without an occasional wholesome spree no
man may live and be a man.
There is a doctor in this city, very well to do,
mature and wise now, who tells a tale of grim old
student days in Glasgow. “We were a poor set, ”
he says ; “none too well clad or fed, and desperate
hard-workers; but once a week we had a sympo-
sium. Everyone managed to bring along some-
thing, if it was only an inch of hard liquor in the
butt of the bottle. And so, for one night of the
week, we drank, talked, sang and gloried to-
gether.”
“A pitiful .waste of money, time and health,
surely,” said the commercial man. And the
doctor answered, “By Heaven, sir, I believe it
was the only thing kept us alive and sane.”
For lack of something of the sort—something
twice a year, even, might suffice, if sure—a good
many of our bushmen become insane or die, and
Bung doesn’t even bury them.
F.M.
One of those Victorians who are leaking
haste to get away from the land of Bent and
Shiels thoughtfully wrote to Melb. Herald
before he left, explaining that he did so because he
was a dairyman and couldn’t get land at a reason-
able price—a price that would allow him a decent
living for his labor. “ Country lands,” he wrote,
are practically a monopoly, the price being so
high that the man who buys sells himself into
slavery pretty well for his natural life. ... If
agricultural lands were about half the present
rates, we would not only stop the rush from here
but would attract desirable settlers from other
Earts of the world.” So, instead of staying and
el ping the Labor party to fight for a progressive
land tax that will force big estates into the market
in small lots, he and his kind run to the green
distant fields of S’Africa. The average farmer-
person, such as this emigrant, wants land, yet he
almost goes into hysterics if you talk to him of. a
graduated land tax that will force big locked-up
properties into the market.
Biliousness
FOR many years I have been a martyr
to Biliousness,” says Mrs. M. Jordon,
of Kelly-street, Scone, (N. S.'W.). “ Al-
most every fortnight I would have an
attack, and try what I would, I could get no
relief. The pain in my head was unbearable,
also the pain across the eyes. After eating I
would vomit terribly. I tried several so-called
remedies, but gained no relief. I then decided to
give Bile Beans a trial, although I had little faith
in them after my experiences with other medi-
cines. To my surprise I gained relief from the
first dose. Thus encouraged, I continued with
the Beans, following the directions closely, and
can now truthfully say I am enjoying splendid
health, and have never had a bilious attack since
I first began using Bile Beans. My work is now
a pleasure, and my friends have noticed the great
improvement there is in me. Words cannot
express my gratitude to you for your wonderful

medicine, and I hope that many others suffering from Biliousness will be persuaded
to try Bile Beans.

“T.D.”: Those two Italians mentioned by Melbourne
Harbinger of Light as having- been the mediums
whereby spirits conveyed operatic music from the outer
world are really repeating something alleged to have
happened right here in Australia. When Clifford Halle
(son of Charles) was here some seven or eight years ago
telling people how to sing', but giving very moderate
illustrations of the art himself, he engaged David Cope,
junior, as accompanist. One. night that musician made
the astonishing announcement that he had a composition
to play which had been communicated to him whilst in a
somnolent state by the spirit of Mendelssohn. The
music was passable enough, in its way, but scarcely up to
the Mendelssohn mark ; evidently the mighty Hebrew
had deteriorated.
Talking of a recent revival of “ Caste” a London critic
says:—“ Miss Carlotta Addison moderates the asperities
of the haughty Marquise, whose supercilious sneers at
‘ trade’ are strangely out of date in an age when half the
fashionable bonnet-shops and tea-shops are run by mem-
bers of the aristocracy,” “ Caste ” was produced by the
Bancrofts in 1867, and perhaps author Tom Robertson’s
notions of class distinction were a little old-fashioned
even then, but they passed criticism. The Marquise in
question—if writer remembers rightly—snorts at the
social pretensions of Captain Hawtre.v, a mere moneyed
person—“ Captain Hawtrey ! His father was in trade —
something in the city—Soap I believe.” The status of
soap-makers has risen considerably since 1867. So has
the status of Soap.
Durward Lely—Lely is an anagram of his patronymic,
Lyle—a Scotchman, who studied singing,in Italy, and
speaks Italian perfectly, and who gave Scotch monologue
entertainments some few years ago throughout Australia,
displaying one of the finest' tenor voices yet heard in
these States, has tieen “syndicated ” under the style of
Lely’s, Limited. Nothing is sacred to the company-
promoter. He may some day swoop down on our Sydney
Domain orators. Fancy-“ George Perry, Limited!”
Also he may float the holy places of Jerusalem, and call
them, “ Christianity, Limited.”
A London transpontine theatre, in the New Cut, had,
until recently, a notice posted to the effect that “ Ladies
without shoes or stockings are not to be admitted into
the dress circle.”
Marshall Hall’s Conservatorium girls can always be
distinguished on Melbpurne Block by the kind of clothes
they wear. They make a point of going about in heavy
colored gowns, severely plain in cut, wkh a surmounting
Panama (the older the better) practically devoid of
decoration. The more the student is able to afford stylish
dressing the less she looks like it, and the Melbourne
shop girl could give the “Con.” girl many yards and a
beating where dress is concerned. But when she makes
her frequently-repeated debut, the Marshall Hall product
emerges like a butterfly from her drab-condition. Every
Melbourne singing girl, by the way, makes her platform
debut at least three times.
“ P.” : For downright cheek the average “musical
competition ” judge takes the biscuit. At a well-known
series of contests the remarks of the adjudicating official
were so violent that many competitors, supposed to have
excellent chances, stood down rather than have the
functionary “ sling language ” at them in a crowded hall.
There are musicians who would make the Angel Gabriel
speak in exclamations and fireworks, but being a gentle-
man he would hide his head under his wing where no one
would hear him.
“Vul” lie that episode at Perth (W.A.), when three
shots came, at different times, through the window of a
hotel apartment occupied by Mr. Thorley, Of Williamson’s
Opera co. I reckon this is the beginning of a series of
tragedies. Australia exports an immense number of singers
to London, and they mostly fail. By-and-bye, there will
be a return-tide of insane Australian singers, who have
gone crazy with failure, and some of these individuals
will run amok. Naturally, they will begin to shoot at
the musical profession, as the one they are most interested
in. In the instance quoted above the Australian singer
who failed had evidently come back in a deranged state
by the cheap German boat, had got ashore at the'first
port in his haste to shoot somebody, and promptly com- .
menced operations by firing at the comic opera co.
Under these circumstances, all reasonable precaution
should be taken. If anybody with a wild expression and
a gun or a large axe—especially if there is blood on the
axe—calls at the theatre inquiring for Nellie Stewart he.
ought to be refused admission.
A picture now exhibiting in London contains 350 like-
nesses of actors and variety artists. The painter put his
wife in the foreground when he started the work three
years ago, and she remarked at the time that some of the
subjects would be dead before the picture was com-
pleted. She was the first to die.
Amusing to read in Melbourne Argus that “ Mr. Fritz
Mueller was'the guest of the University Conservatorium
students yesterday afternoon at the close of the second
term. He gave a very poetical reading of the so-and-so
sonata, &c., &c.” Mr. Fritz Mueller ! Four years ago he
was a “ child of tender years,” a “ small boy in knicker-
bockers,” and so forth. The Argus of June 19th, 1899,
reported his farewell benefit concert in this style : “ The
appearance of Fritz Mueller on the platform was the
signal for a hearty round of applause, and opera glasses
were eagerly focussed upon the youngest of the little
band of youthful musicians who have lately challenged
attention. . . . Master Fritz Mueller had on the printed
programme four pianoforte compositions placed before
his name, namely Gavotte in D major (Bach), &c., &e.
These were of course beyond the grasp of such a liliputian
performer, but the sturdy little fclloui attacked them in
no uncertain fashion, &c.” And now the sturdy little
fellow is Mr. Fritz. How rapidly these children age—
after they have had their farewell benefits in knicker-
bockers or short frocks 1

'T^T - OWHERE is literary material more abundant
than in legendless Australia. Off the well-
-1. 1 beaten tracks of the spurious-pigturesque and
the unconditional-hoggish, lie spacious areas
of subject-matter, irreverently challenging the Biblical
axiom that “there is no new thing under the sun."
And countless types of character, evolved by the indus-
trial conditions of heretofore inarticulate life, confront
the eye and invite the graphic pen. For tragedy, pathos,
humour, fancy, however indissolubly linked with human
■
nature, mustassume
unhackneyed forms
in a continent which
resembles Europe
or N. America only
inasmuch as there
is a river in both.
Thefictional liter-
ature of Australia
—scanty at most,
and uneven at best
—has advanced by orderly gradation from Anglo-Austra-
lian, through Colonial, to National. The successional
replacement of the immigrant, with his old-world
ideals, by the impressionable representative of a new
type, has made such a transition inevitable. But the
mass of our literature is British; and book-begotten
sentiment dies hard, except among pioneers of opinion.
Conventionality persists in viewing Australian charac-
teristics and deviations as more or less exotic; whilst to
the fully-emancipated Australian these stand as self-
established units of comparison, as normal and legitimate
phenomena, in opposition to the hearsay conditions of
moister and less homelike lands.
The corresponding penalty of this National conscious-
ness is a certain narrowness of outlook, a spontaneous
impression that the sun rises over the Barrier Reef, and
sets behind the
Leu win. When
darkened by stolid-
ity, such focalised
regard is sometimes
amusing, sometimes
regrettable, though
always pardonable
except when it de-
generates into pro-
vincialism.
A Yarn of
the Riverina.
But when illuminated by intelligence, the same insular
survey crystallises into far-sighted patriotism. When
further reinforced by acute observation and culti-
vated forecast, it invests with interest the minutest
details of National life. Add humour, sympathy,
and felicity of phrase, together with that rarest of
literary qualities, initiative, and the subtle touch of
nature is achieved. Then a new light of signi-
ficance is flashed on the commonplace ; a shade of
actuality softens the bizarre ; and each scene, action or
colloquy, carrying authenticity on its face, becomes an
indispensable accessory to the scheme. This describes
Tom Collins—at his best.
At his worst, he discloses an immoderate egotism,
apparently undisciplined by controversy, and evidently
fostered by solitary self-communion. Yet his introduc-
tion of Himself, if decided, is invariably courteous, and
often keenly interesting.
The book, Such is Life, which The Bulletin will publish
on the Ist August, is original in scheme. The author
premeditated pur-
pose of amplifying
the diary memor-
anda of one week,
and his book natur-
ally falls into seven
chapters. The first
date is taken at
hazard, from aseries
of filled-up pocket
•V V.
diaries.
This random text introduces five typical bullock -
drivers, with three or four assorted nomads. The day is
uneventful, but the night’s camp (a risky grass-steal) is
enlivened by Out-back topics. The scene is on the
Lachlan plains.
Then, for sufficient reasons, the diary-records cease to
be consecutive, and are henceforth separated by monthly
intervals. The scene of Chap. II is placed in the Lach-
lan-Darling scrub country, amongst station hands. Chap.
11l opens on the Murray, between Echuca and Albury.
Chap. IV displays the Lower Lachlan, with actors proper
to the scene \ and Chap. V falls in an adjacent locality,
with all accessories widely dissimilar. Chap. VI returns
to the vicinity of Chap. I, but the aspect is squattocratic,
not nomadic. Chap. VII is arbitrarily built on a date six
4s
■
or seven weeks sub-
sequent to that of
its forerunner, in
order to serve as a
sequel.
Underneath this
obvious dislocation
of anything resem-
bling narrative hfn
several undercut'-
rents of plot,
manifest to the reader, though ostensibly unnoticed bv
the author. For example, an incidental camp-fjre varii,
in Chap. I, casually- furnishes the key to some strong
character-painting in Chaps. IV and VI. In fact, the
studied inconsecutiveness of the “ Memoirs is made to
mask intricate coincidences and cross-purposes.
The book is handsomely bound in cloth, decorated ; the
price is 4s. 6d. Orders, accompanied by postal note, mar
be sent to The Bulletin office ; and the book will be for-
warded to any address.
